# Fior di Battaglia — Translation Comparison

Three translation sources aligned by folio:
- **Claude Vision OCR** (2026-04-07) — first-pass from manuscript images
- **Colin Hatcher** (via Wiktenauer) — scholarly translation from Getty MS
- **Michael Chidester** (via Wiktenauer) — scholarly translation from Morgan MS

---

**Coverage:** 92 folios total, 7 blank
- All three sources: 83 folios
- Claude only (no Wiktenauer): 2 folios
- Wiktenauer only (no Claude): 0 folios

## Folio 01r
**Section:** Preface

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

### Left column

[F]urlano de Ciuidà d'Ostria che fo di misier Benedetto de la nobel casa di deli Liberi da Premariaco d'la diocesi dello Patriarchado de Aquilegia, in sua contenuta uolse imprender ad armiçare l'arte de combatter in Starri de lança, Açça, Spada, e daga, e de abrazare a pe e a cauallo, in arme e sença arme.

¶ Anchora uolse sauere tempere de ferri.

¶ E fatteçe di chascuna arma tanto a defenderse quanto ad offenderse, e maymamente chose de combatter ad oltrança.

¶ Anchora altre chose merauigliose e oultre le quale a pochi homini del mondo sono palese.

¶ E sono chose uerissime e di grandissima offerta, de quinde diffessa, e chose che no si po fallare tanto sono liçere a fare.

¶ E la quale arte e magistreio che ditto di sopra.

¶ E lo ditto Fiore sia imprese le ditte chose da molti magistri todeschi.

¶ E di molti Italiani in piu prouinçie e in molte cita di cu grandissima e cu grand spesa.

¶ E per la gracia di dio da tutti magistri e scolari.

¶ E istà in corte di grandi Signori, prinçipi, Duchi, Marchesi, e Conti, Chaualieri e Schudiceri in tanto a impressa dista arte.

¶ Che lo ditto Fiore a strado piu e piu uolte richesto da molti Signori e chaualieri e schudiceri per imprunder del ditto Fiore si fatta arte d'armiçare e d'combatter in starra a oltrança, la quale arte a le a monstruda a piustori Italiani e todeschi e altri grandi Signori che ano debudo combatter in starri.

¶ E ancho ad infiniti che non ano debudo combattere.

¶ E de alcuni che

### Right column

sono stadi miei scolari che ano debudo combatter in starria.

¶ De quali alchuni qui ne faro nome e memoria.

¶ Primo de loro, si fo el nobile e gaglardo chaualiero di misier Piero del Verde el quale debea combattere cu misier Piero d'la Corona, i quali forono ambi doy todeschi.

¶ E la Bataglia debea esser a Perosia.

¶ Anchora alo ualoroso chaualiero misier Nicolo de Riçalino thadesco che debea combatter cu Nicolo inghileso. Lo campo fo dado ad Imola.

¶ Anchora al notabile valoroso, e gaglardo chaualiero misser Buleago di Captani di Grimello chiamado di Mantua che debea combattere cu lo ualoroso chaualiero misser Bugichard de França. Lo campo fo a Padua.

¶ Anchora al valoroso schudicero Lalotto di Becharia de Pauia el quale debea de vij. punti de lança a ferri moladi a chauallo contra lo ualente chaualiero misser Baldassaro todesco, i quali ad Imola debea combattere in starria.

¶ Anchora al valoroso schudicero Gioannino da Dayo di Milano che se in Pauia in lo castello contra lo ualente schudicero Siamo todesco tre punti di lança a ferri moladi a chauallo.

¶ E e por se a pe tre colpi d'Açça. e tre colpi d'Spada e tre colpi di daga in presença del nobilissimo prencipio e Signore misser lo Ducha di Milano e d'madonna la duchessa. e altri infiniti Signori e donne.

¶ Anchora al caualeroso chaualiero misier Acço di Castell Barcho che debea una uolta combattere cum Guarne di Ordelaffi.

¶ E mastra uolta cu lo ualente e bon chaualiero misser Jacomo de Bosin, el campo debea esser al piasere dolo Signore Ducha de Milano. Di questi e altri i quali io trouo o magistrudi. io son molto contento per che io son stato ben riamerado e o habudo l'onore e l'amore di miei scolari e di parenti loro.

¶ Digo anchora che questa arte io lo mostrada sempre ocultemante si che no gle sta presente alchuno

### Translation — Claude Vision

Furlano of Cividale d'Austria [Cividale del Friuli], who was the son of Messer Benedetto of the noble house of the Liberi of Premariacco, of the diocese of the Patriarchate of Aquileia, in his youth wished to learn the art of arms -- the art of fighting in the lists with lance, axe, sword, and dagger, and of wrestling on foot and on horseback, in armour and without armour.

He also wished to learn the tempering of iron.

And the properties of each weapon both for defending and for attacking, and especially those things pertaining to fighting to the finish [a oltranza].

And also other marvellous and extraordinary things which to few men in the world are known.

And these are most true things and of the greatest power, both offensive and defensive, and things that cannot fail, so easy are they to do.

And the said art and mastery as described above.

And the said Fiore learned these said things from many German masters.

And from many Italians in many provinces and in many cities with very great expense.

And by the grace of God, from all masters and scholars.

And he stayed at the courts of great lords, princes, dukes, marquises, and counts, knights and squires, so much did he learn of this art.

That the said Fiore has been requested many times by many lords and knights and squires to teach them this art of arms and of fighting in the lists to the finish, which art he has shown to many Italians and Germans and other great lords who had to fight in the lists.

And also to an infinite number who did not have to fight.

And of some who have been my scholars who had to fight in the lists.

Of whom I shall here name some and make a record.

First of them was the noble and valiant knight Messer Piero del Verde, who had to fight with Messer Piero della Corona, both of whom were Germans.

And the battle was to be at Perugia.

Also to the valorous knight Messer Nicolo de Rizalino, a German, who had to fight with Nicolo, an Englishman. The field was given at Imola.

Also to the notable, valorous, and valiant knight Messer Buleago dei Captani di Grimello, called of Mantua, who had to fight with the valorous knight Messer Boucicaut of France. The field was at Padua.

Also to the valorous squire Lalotto dei Beccaria of Pavia, who had to joust seven points of lance with sharpened tips on horseback against the valiant knight Messer Baldassare, a German, who at Imola had to fight in the lists.

Also to the valorous squire Giovannino da Baggio of Milan, who fought in Pavia in the castle against the valiant squire Siamo, a German, three points of lance with sharpened tips on horseback.

And afterwards on foot three blows of the axe, and three blows of the sword, and three blows of the dagger, in the presence of the most noble prince and lord, my lord the Duke of Milan, and of my lady the Duchess, and other infinite lords and ladies.

Also to the chivalrous knight Messer Azzo di Castel Barco, who had to fight once with Guarneri degli Ordelaffi.

And another time with the valiant and good knight Messer Jacomo de Bosin; the field was to be at the pleasure of the lord Duke of Milan. Of these and others whom I have found or taught, I am very content, because I have been well rewarded and have had the honour and the love of my scholars and their families.

I say also that this art I have always shown secretly so that no one else was present

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

I, Fiore the Friulian, born in Cividale D’Austria, was the son of Sir Benedetto of the noble order 
of the free knights of Premariacco, in the diocese of the Patriarchate of Aquileia.

As a young man I desired to learn armed fighting, including the art of fighting in the lists with spear, poleaxe, sword, dagger and unarmed grappling, on foot and on horseback, armored and unarmored.

| 
 In addition I wanted to study how weapons were made, and the characteristics of each weapon for both offense and defense, particularly as they applied to mortal combat.

| 
I also desired to learn the wondrous secrets of this art known only by very few men in this world.

And these secrets will give you mastery of attack and defense, and make you invincible, for victory comes easily to a man who has the skill and mastery described above.

| 
I learned these skills from many German and Italian masters and their senior students, in many provinces and many cities, and at great personal cost and expense.

And by the grace of God I also acquired so much knowledge at the courts of noblemen, princes, dukes, marquises, counts, knights and squires, that increasingly I was myself asked to teach. My services were requested many times by noblemen, knights and their squires, who wanted me to teach them the art of armed combat both for fighting at the barrier and for mortal combat. And so I taught this art to many Italians and Germans and other noblemen who were obliged to fight at the barrier, as well as to numerous noblemen who did not actually compete.

And below are the names and a little of the history of some of the noblemen who have been my students, and who were obliged to fight at the barrier.

The first of them was the noble and gallant knight Piero del Verde who fought Piero della Corona. Both were German, and the fight took place in Perosa.

Next was the brave knight Niccolo Voriçilino, also a German, who was obliged to fight Niccolo Inghileso. The field of battle for this fight was Imola.

Next was the well-known, valiant and gallant knight Galeazzo de Capitani da Grimello, known as da Mantova, who was obliged to fight the valiant knight Buçichardo de Fraca. The field of battle for this fight was Padova.

Next was the valiant squire Lancillotto da Becharia de Pavia, who exchanged six strikes with a sharpened steel lance against the valiant German knight Baldassarro, in a fight that took place in the lists at Imola.

Next was the valiant squire Gioanino da Bavo, from Milan, who, in the castle in Pavia, fought three passes with a sharpened steel lance, against the valiant German squire Sram. And then on foot he fought three passes with the axe, three with the sword and three with the dagger, in the presence of the very noble prince and lord the Duke of Milan, and his lady the Duchess, and numerous other lords and ladies.

Next was the cautious knight Sir Açço da Castell Barcho, who was obliged to fight one pass against Çuanne di Ordelaffi, and another pass against the valiant and good knight Sir Jacomo di Boson, the location chosen by his eminence the Duke of Milan.

Of these and of others whom I, Fiore, have taught, I am very proud, because I have been well rewarded, plus I earned the respect and the affection of my students and also of their relatives.

Also, I should tell you that I always taught this art secretly, and so no one was present at my lessons except for the student and occasionally a close relative of his, and if anyone else was there by my grace or favor, they were only allowed to watch after swearing a sacred oath of secrecy, swearing by their faith not to reveal any of the techniques they saw me, Master Fiore, demonstrate.

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

Fiore Friulano de Cividale d'Austria, the son of Sir Benedetto of the noble house of the Liberi of Premariacco in the diocese of the Patriarchate of Aquileia, in his youth wanted to learn fencing and the art of combat in the barriers (that is, to the death); of lance, ax, sword, and dagger, and of wrestling, on foot and on horse, in armor and without armor.

Also he wanted to know of the temper of iron, and the qualities of each weapon, as much for defense as for offense, and most of all matters of mortal combat.

Also other marvelous and occult things that are apparent to few men in the world, and are very true things and very great for offense and defense, and things that cannot fail you, so easy are they to do, which art and mystery is described above.

And the aforesaid Fiore did learn the aforesaid things from many German masters. Also from many Italians in many provinces and in many cities, with great fatigue and with great expense, and by the grace of God from so many masters and scholars.

And in so many courts of great lords, princes, dukes, marquises and counts, knights, and squires did he undertake this art, that the aforesaid Fiore was more and more times retained by many lords and knights and squires for learning from the aforesaid Fiore to do the art of fencing and of combat in the barriers to the bitter end, which art he demonstrated to many Italians and Germans and other great lords that were obliged to combat in the barriers (and also to countless that were not obliged to combat). And of some that have been my scholars that have been obliged to combat in the barriers, of these I wish to name and make here a remembrance.

And the first notable and gallant knight is Sir Peter von Grünen, who was obliged to combat with Sir Peter Kornwald (who were both Germans). And the battle was required to be at Perugia.

Also the valiant knight Sir Nikolo [illegible] (the German), who was obliged to combat with Nicolo (the English), and the field was given at Imola.

Also the notable, valiant, and gallant knight Sir Galeazzo Cattaneo dei Grumelli, called da Mantua, who was obliged to combat with the valiant knight Sir Boucicault (Jean Ⅱ le Maingre) of France, and the field was at Padua.

Also the valiant squire Lancillotto Beccaria of Pavia. That was 6 thrusts of ground-iron lance on horseback against the valiant knight Sir Balthasar von Braunschweig-Grubenhagen (a German), and also obliged to combat in the list, and this was at Imola.

Also the valiant squire Giovannino da Baggio of Milan, who in the castle in Pavia, with the valiant squire Sirano (the German), struck three thrusts of ground-iron lance on horseback. And then on foot he made three blows of axe, and three blows of sword, and three blows of dagger, in the presence of the most noble lord Duke of Milan, and of the lady Duchess, and of countless other lords and lady.

Also the cautious knight Sir Azzone di Castelbarco, who once was obliged to combat with Sir Giovanni di Ordelaffi. And another time with the valiant and virtuous knight Sir Giacomo da Boson, and the field was set at the pleasure of the lord Duke of Milan.

These and others have I, Fiore, taught, and I am very content because I have been well-remunerated and I have had the honor and the love of my scholars and of their relatives.

Also I say that to whom I have taught this art, I have taught secretly, that there was no person other than the scholar and some close relative of his. Also that those who were present had sworn with sacrament that they would not reveal any play that they had seen from me, Fiore.

### Notes

- This is the opening folio of the prologue, with an elaborate illuminated border of red and gold foliate decoration, and a large historiated initial "F" (blue and red with gold leaf).
- The text is in two columns of dense Middle Italian (Venetian/Friulian dialect, c. 1410).
- The existing OCR was largely accurate; minor corrections made for consistency (e.g., expanding abbreviations more consistently, correcting some misreadings).
- The text breaks mid-sentence at "alchuno" at the bottom of the right column, continuing on fol. 2r.
- Key historical references: duels at Perugia, Imola, Padua, Pavia; patronage of the Duke of Milan (Gian Galeazzo Visconti).

---

## Folio 02r
**Section:** Preface

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

### Left column

al mostri se no lu scolaro et alchuno fo discreto parente. E se pur alchuno altro gle sta per gracia o per cortesia cu sagramenti gli sono stadi prometendo a fede de no palentare alchum cago incuido da mi Fiore magistro.

¶ E maforamente me o guardado da magistri senimidori e da fuoi scolari. E loro per inuidia come gli magistri m'ano conuido a çugare a spide di taglo e di punta in çuparello d'armare sença altra arma saluo che un paro di guanti de çamoça. E tutto questo e stado per che io no o uagludo praticar di loro ne o uagludo insegnare niente di mia arte.

¶ E questo accidente e stado .v. uolte per fio que io son stado requirido.

¶ E .v. uolte per mio honore m'a conuegnudo çugare in luoghi strani sença parenti e sença amisi si no habiendo sperança d'altrui, se non in dio, in l'arte, e in mi medesimo, e l'amia spada.

¶ E per la gracia di dio io Fiore son rimaso cu honore e sença lesione di mia persona.

¶ Anchora io Fiore dipuia a miei scolari che debeano combattere in sbarra che lo combatter in sbarra e assay assay di menore periculo che a çugare cu spide di taglo e di punta in çuparello d'armare. Po che cholui che çugua a spide tacienti una sola couerta che falla, se quello colpo gli da la morte.

¶ Et uno che combatte in sbarra e ben armado e po ricieuere feride assay. Anchora puo uincere la bataglia.

¶ Anchora sie un'altria chosa che niue uolte de perisse nisuno, per che si piglano a presone.

¶ Si che io digo che uoria inanco combattere tre uolte in sbarra che una sol uolta a spade taglienti come foura ditto.

¶ E si digo che lo modo che de combatter in sbarra sendo ben armado, e sapiando l'arte del combattere, e habiando li auantaçi chi si pon pigiare se ello no e ualente ello si uoraue ben spuchare. Ben che possa dire per la gracia di dio che çamay nissimo mio scolaro non fo perdente. L'ista ante due in ella sono sempre remasi cum honore.

¶ Anchora digo io preditto Fiore che questi Signori chaualieri e scudicieri a chuy io mostra di questa arte da combattere sono stadi contenti del mio insegnare no uaglando altro che mi per arfagistro.

¶ Anchora digo che nessuno di miei scolari in speciale li sopradetti no aue may libro in l'arte de combattere altro che misser Galeaço da Mantoa.

¶ Ben ch'ello diprua che sença libri no fara çamay nissimo bon magistro ne scolaro in questa arte.

¶ E io Fiore lo confermo uero, che questa arte si longia che lo no e al mondo homo de si granda memoria che podesse tenere a mente sença libri la quarta parte di questa arte.

¶ Adoncha cu

### Right column

la quarta parte di questa arte no sapiando piu no m'tarria per magistro.

¶ Che io Fiore sapiando legere, e scriuere, e disegnare, e habiando libri in quest'arte, e in lei o studiado ben .xl. anni o piu. Anchora no me sento ben perfetto in tutta questa arte. Ben che sia tenudo di grandi Signori che sono stadi miei scolari ben perfetto magistro in l'arte predetta.

¶ E si digo che se io auesse studiado .xl. anni in lege, in decretali, e in medicina chome io studiado in l'arte de l'armiçare, che io seria doctore in quelle tre scientie.

¶ E in questa scientia d'armiçare o habuda grand fadigha cu studuga e presuncion d'esser pur un scolaro de qualch'un altro.

¶ Considerando lo preditto Fiore che in quest'arte pochi al mundo seni trouano magistri, e uaglando che di mi sia fatta memoria, per ella io faro un libro in tuta l'arte, e de tutte chose le quale io so, e di ferri e di tempere e d'altre chose secondo l'ordene lo quale m'a dato quell'alto Signore che sopra gli altri per marcial uirtu di mi piasi piu, e piu merito di questo mio libro per sua noblita ch'altro Signore lo quale uedesse may o ueder potra, coe el mio illustro et excelso Signore possente, principato...

---

[Illuminated initial Q -- dedication to the Marquis]

Marchese da Este Signore de la nobele cita di Ferrara, di Modena, Regio, e Parma rectu, a chuy dio dia bona uita, e uentura prospera e victoria degli inimisi suoy.

---

¶ Incomençamo lo libro secondo l'ordenamento del mio Signore al Marchese e facemo che no gli manchi niente in l'arte, che io mi rendo certo che lo mio Signore mi fara bon merito per la sua grandi noblita e cortesia.

¶ E comiçemo al braçare. L'oquale sie di dua razone: come da solaço e di ira, coe per la uita cu ogni inganno e falsita e crudelita che si po fare. E di quelle che si fa per la uita uoglo primare mostrare per ragione, e maximamente a guadagnar le prese come per sança quando si combatte per la uita.

¶ L'omo che uole abraçare uole esser auisado cu chuy ello abraça. Se lu compagno e piu forte o se ello e piu grando di persona. E se ello e troppo çouene o uero troppo uecchio. Anchora di uedere si ello si mette ale guardie d'abraçare. E de tutte queste chose si e de prouedere.

¶ E nuente meno mettersi sempre o piu forte o meno forte ale prese e le ligadure. E sempre defendersi dele prese del suo contrario.

¶ E se lo tuo inimigo e disarmado attende a ferirlo in li loghi piu dagliosi e piu periuolosi coe in li ochi, e lo naso, e le tempie, e sotto lo mento e in li fianchi.

¶ E nuente meno guarda si tu poi uegnire ale prese e le ligadure o armado o disarmado che fosse l'uno e l'altro.

¶ Anchora digo che l'abraçare uole auere .viij. chose. Coe forteça, presteça, sauere...

### Translation — Claude Vision

[continuing from fol. 1r] ...at the showing except the scholar and perhaps some discreet relative. And if indeed some other person was there by grace or by courtesy, with oaths they were made to promise in good faith not to reveal anything taught by me, Fiore, master.

And above all I have guarded myself from treacherous masters and from their scholars. And they, out of envy -- these masters have challenged me to fight with sharp swords, both cutting and thrusting, in a padded jacket without other armour save a pair of chamois gloves. And all this has been because I would not associate with them nor would I teach them anything of my art.

And this has happened five times, inasmuch as I have been challenged.

And five times, for my honour, I have been compelled to fight in strange places without relatives and without friends, having no hope in anyone except in God, in my art, in myself, and in my sword.

And by the grace of God, I, Fiore, have survived with honour and without injury to my person.

Furthermore I, Fiore, taught my scholars who had to fight in the barriers that fighting in the barriers is much, much less dangerous than fighting with sharp swords, cutting and thrusting, in a padded jacket. Because he who fights with sharp swords -- if a single cover fails him, that blow gives him death.

But one who fights in the barriers is well armoured and may receive many wounds. He may still win the battle.

Furthermore there is another thing: nine times out of ten nobody dies, because they are taken prisoner.

So I say that I would rather fight three times in the barriers than once with sharp swords as said above.

And I say that the way to fight in the barriers, being well armed, and knowing the art of fighting, and having the advantages that one can take -- if he is not valiant he would wish to spit [with disgust]. But I can say by the grace of God that never has any of my scholars been the loser. All of them have always remained with honour.

Furthermore I, the aforesaid Fiore, say that these lords, knights, and squires to whom I showed this art of fighting have been content with my teaching, wanting no one else but me as their master.

Furthermore I say that none of my scholars, especially the aforesaid, ever had a book on the art of fighting except Messer Galeazzo of Mantua.

Although he proves that without books no one will ever make a good master or scholar in this art.

And I, Fiore, confirm this to be true, that this art is so vast that there is no man in the world with so great a memory that he could keep in mind without books a quarter of this art.

Therefore with only a quarter of this art, knowing no more, I would not consider myself a master.

Because I, Fiore, knowing how to read, and write, and draw, and having books on this art, and in it having studied a good forty years or more -- still I do not feel myself perfectly accomplished in all of this art. Although I am held by the great lords who have been my scholars to be a perfectly accomplished master in the aforesaid art.

And I say that if I had studied forty years in law, in canon law, and in medicine as I have studied in the art of arms, I would be a doctor in those three sciences.

And in this science of arms I have had great toil with study and the presumption of being merely a scholar of someone else.

Considering -- I the aforesaid Fiore -- that in this art few in the world are found who are masters, and wishing that a memorial be made of me, therefore I shall make a book of all the art, and of all things which I know, of iron and of tempering and of other things, according to the order which has been given to me by that high Lord who above all others for martial virtue pleased me most, and most deserving of this my book for his nobility than any other lord whom I have ever seen or shall be able to see, that is my illustrious and exalted lord, powerful prince...

[Illuminated Q:] ...Marquis of Este, Lord of the noble city of Ferrara, of Modena, Reggio, and Parma, to whom may God give good life, and prosperous fortune, and victory over his enemies.

We begin the book according to the instruction of my lord the Marquis, and we shall ensure that nothing is lacking in the art, for I am certain that my lord will give me good reward for his great nobility and courtesy.

And we begin with wrestling. Which is of two kinds: one of play [solazo] and one of anger [ira], that is for one's life with every cunning and deception and cruelty that can be done. And of those things that are done for one's life I wish first to show by reason, and especially how to gain the holds, as in earnest when one fights for one's life.

The man who wishes to wrestle must be aware of whom he wrestles with. Whether his companion is stronger, or whether he is larger of person. And whether he is too young or too old. Also to see if he puts himself in the guards of wrestling. And all these things must be considered.

And nonetheless, always set oneself -- whether stronger or weaker -- to the holds and the locks. And always defend oneself from the holds of one's adversary.

And if your enemy is unarmed, aim to strike him in the most painful and most dangerous places: that is in the eyes, and the nose, and the temples, and under the chin, and in the flanks.

And nonetheless see if you can come to holds and locks, whether armed or unarmed, whichever one and the other may be.

Furthermore I say that wrestling requires eight things. That is: strength, speed, knowledge...

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

Now that I have discussed some general rules for grappling, I will discuss the grappling guards. There are a variety of grappling guards, some better than others. But there are four guards that are the best whether in or out of armor, although I advise you not to wait in them for too long, due to the rapid changes that take place when you are grappling.

The first four Masters that you will see with crowns on their heads will show you these four superior grappling guards. The first two are named “The Long Guard” and “The Boar’s Tooth” and they can be used to counter each other. The second two are named “Iron Gate” and “The Forehead Guard”, and they can also be used to counter each other. From these four guards, whether in or out of armor, you can do all of the eight things I listed earlier, namely holds, binds, dislocations, etc.

You will need to learn the guards of the Masters, how to distinguish the Students from the Players and the Players from the Masters, and finally the difference between the Remedy and the Counter. While a Counter will usually be presented after the Remedies are shown, sometimes there will be a special “Remedy” that comes last of all. But let me make this clearer for you.

The four guards or “posts” are easy to learn. Sometimes you’ll take a guard and face your opponent without making contact, waiting to see what your opponent will do. These are called the posts or guards of the first Masters of Battle. And these masters wear a golden crown on their head, to signify that the guards they wait in provide them with a superior defense. And these four guards are best suited to apply the principles of my art of armed fighting, which is why these Masters choose to wait in these particular guards.

Whether you call it a “post” or a “guard”, you are referring to the same stance. As a “guard” it is used defensively, that is you use it to protect yourself and defend yourself from the strikes of your opponent. As a “post” it is used offensively, that is, you use it to position yourself in such a way in relation to your opponent that you can attack him without danger to yourself.

The next Master who follows the four guards comes to respond to these guards and to defend himself against a Player who makes attacks that flow from the four beginning guards shown earlier. And this Master also wears a crown, but he is named the Second Master of Battle. He is also known as the Remedy Master, because he carefully selects his response to attacks flowing from the posts referred to above, and makes remedies that prevent him from getting struck.

This second or Remedy Master has a group of Students under him, who demonstrate the plays taught by the Remedy Master that follow the cover or grapple that he shows first as his remedy. And these Students wear a garter under their knee, to identify themselves. These Students will demonstrate all the remedies of the Remedy Master, until a third Master of Battle appears, who will show the Counters to the Remedy Master and his Students.

And because he can defeat the Remedy Master and his students, this Third Master wears both the symbol of the Remedy Master—a golden crown, and the symbol of his students—a golden garter below the knee. And this King is named the Third Master of Battle, and he is also named the Counter Master, because he makes counters to the Remedy Master and his students.

Finally let me tell you that in a few sections of this Art we will find a Fourth Master (or King) who can defeat the Third Master of Battle (the Counter to the Remedy). And this King, the Fourth Master, is named the Fourth Master of Battle. He is also known as the Counter-Counter Master. Be aware however that in this Art few plays will ever go past the Third Master of Battle, for to do so is very risky. But enough about this.

As I have explained above, the guards of the Abrazare (shown by the First Master of Battle), the Second Master of Battle (the Remedy Master) and his Students, the Third Master of Battle (the Counter Remedy, that is the counter to the Second Master of Battle and his Students), and the Fourth Master of Battle (named the Counter-counter Master), represent the foundation of my Art of Grappling whether in and out of armor. Furthermore, these four Masters of Battle and their Students are also the foundation of the Art of the Spear, which has its own guards, Masters and Students. The same is true for the Art of the Pole-axe, the Sword in One Hand, the Sword in Two Hands and the Dagger.

In summary, these Masters of Battle and their Students, identified by their various devices, although first presented as governing principles of my Art of Grappling, are actually the foundation of my entire Art of Armed Fighting, whether on foot or on horseback, and whether in or out of armor.

And my purpose in structuring my art in this way is to make my system easier to learn, by using the same principles of the guards, the Master, the Remedy and the Counter throughout it, just as you see first in the section on Grappling.

And the text, the drawings and the plays will so clearly show you my art, that you will have no trouble understanding it.

Now let’s move on to study the actual drawings, the plays and the text, and you will see that I have spoken truly.

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

We commence this book following my intellect, in such a way that anyone can know to understand easily. And we make comparison of five things. That is, 1) of masters that stand in guard, and 2) of masters (and of masters) that are remedy, and 3) of scholars, and 4) of players, and 5) of contraries to masters and to scholars. The masters stand in positions, that is, guards—that which are called both positions and guards. Positions are called such because they position one, and guards are called such because they guard one from an enemy, and as such, they are called positions and guards for their strength, that poorly will one be able to break the positions on purpose without coming to danger.

The masters that stand in guard stand one against the other without touching one weapon against the other, and here the masters will bear crowns on their heads. The other crowned masters that will be after them also bear crowns, and they are called Masters Remedy. Those that here play with these masters and with their scholars are called players. And the scholars of these Masters Remedy bear a device under the knee, and initiate the cover and holds following what the Master Remedy does, and doing such plays that the Master Remedy knows how to do. At the end will be found the counter of the Master Remedy and of his scholars. And this counter bears a crown on his head and a device under the knee because he is the counter of the Master and of the scholars, and as such he bears the devices of both the Master Remedy and all his scholars. In some plays the counter will be found immediately after the remedy, and in some plays the counter will be found after all the plays of the Master Remedy. Know that here the counter which is made to the Master Remedy, that the counter breaks all of the plays of that cover or grip that he makes. In the following, you will find them well-depicted and -written so that can be easily understood.

And we will commence first on horse with lance and with sword and with wrestling. And afterward with lance on foot, and then with sword in armor, and with sword in two hands in wide and narrow plays. Then the play of the axe, and then certain special matches, and then sword in one hand, and finally wrestling on foot and the play of the dagger.

And in this way you can see all the art of fencing in this book, that cannot ever fail you, so well-worded are the explanations about the depicted figures.

### Notes

- This folio continues from fol. 1r (mid-sentence) and concludes the autobiographical prologue.
- Key passage: Fiore's five personal duels with sharp swords in a padded jacket (*çuparello d'armare*) and chamois gloves.
- The illuminated initial "Q" introduces the dedication to Niccolo III d'Este, Marquis of Ferrara (the patron who commissioned this manuscript).
- The critical *solazo/ira* distinction begins at the bottom of the right column -- wrestling "for play" vs. wrestling "for one's life."
- The eight attributes (*viij. chose*) of wrestling are introduced at the very end but the list is cut off, continuing on fol. 4r.
- Corrections from existing OCR: "solaço" corrected to "solaço" (confirmed from image), "plazo" corrected to "solaço", various abbreviation expansions refined.
- Text breaks mid-list at the bottom of the right column.

---

## Folio 04r
**Section:** Preface (Latin)

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

### Left column

coe sauer preste auantagiade, sauere far roture coe romper braci e çambe, sauer ligadure coe ligar braci in modo che l'homo non habia piu defesa ne se possa partire in sua liberta. Sauere ferrire in luogo piu pericoloso.

¶ Anchora sauer mettere uno in terra sença periculo di si instesso.

¶ Anchora sauer dislagar braci e çambi per diuersi modi.

¶ E le quale tutte chose si trouarano e porano depinte in questo libro de grado in grado como uole l'arte.

¶ Noi auemo ditto co che uole l'abraçare. Ora disemo delle guardie d'abraçare.

¶ Le guardie del abraçare si po fare per diuersi modi. E un modo e miglore del altro.

¶ Ma queste .iiij. guardie sono le miglore in arme e in sença arme. Auçegna dio che le guardie non ano stabilita per le prese subite che se fano.

¶ Elli primi quatro magistri che uedenti cu le corone in testa, per quegli si mostra le guardie del abraçare. Coe posta longa, e dente di cengiaro le quale fano una contra l'altra. E poi fano porta di ferro e posta frontale l'una incontra l'altra.

¶ E queste .iiij. guardie pono fare tutte chose che denanci sono ditte del abraçare in arme e sença arme. Coe prese e ligadure e roture.

¶ Aro bisogna fare per modo che le guardie se cognossa delli magistri, çugadori, elli scolari da çugadori, elli çugadori de magistri, e lo remedio del contrario, ben che sempre lo contrario e posto dredo al remedio. Et al uolta lo remedio dredo o dredo tutti li soi çogi, e di questo faremo chiareça.

¶ Noi disemo che d'aguardare le guardie o uero poste e ligiera chosa, prima che le guardie ano lor arme in mano l'una contra l'altra, e non si tochano l'una cu l'altra.

¶ E stano auisade e ferme una contra l'altra per uedere co che lo compagno uol fare.

¶ Queste sono chiamade poste ouero guardie ouero primi magistri de la bataglia.

¶ E questi portano corona in testa, per che sono poste in logo e per modo di fare grande defesa cu esso tale apostare.

¶ E sono lo principio di quella arte coe di quella arte de l'arma cu la quale li ditti magistri stano in guardia.

¶ E tanto e a dire poste che guardia.

¶ E guardia e tanto a dire che l'omo se guardi, e si defende cu quella, de le feride del suo inimigo.

¶ E tanto e a dire posta che modo de apostare lo inimigo suo e non offenderlo sença periculo di si instesso.

¶ L'altro magistro che seguita le .iiij. guardie, uene ad onfendere de le guardie, e si uene a defenderse d'un altro çugadore cu gli colpi che esseno de le .iiij. guardie che sono denanci. Questo magistro porta anchora corona, e se e chiamado secondo magistro.

¶ Anchora sie chiamado magistro remedio, per che ello fa lo remedio che no gli siano date de le feride ouero che no gli sia fatta iniuria in quella arte

### Right column

che sono le ditte poste ouero guardie.

¶ E questo secondo coe remedio sia alquanti çugadori sotto di si i quali çugano quelli çagi che poria çugare lo magistro che dauanti coe lo rimedio piglando quella couerta ouero pressa che fa lo ditto rimedio. E questi çugadori portarano una diuisa de lo çinochio. E farano questi çugadori tutti li çoghi de lo rimedio in sin tanto che si trouara un altro magistro che fara lo contrario de lo rimedio e di tutti suoy çugadori.

¶ E per co ch'ello fa contra lo remedio e contra suoi çugadori, ello portera la diuisa de lo magistro rimedio e di suoy çugadori coe la corona in testa e la diuisa sotto lo çinochio.

¶ E questo e chiamado magistro terço, e de chiamado contrario e che fara per gli altri magistri e in assi çogi.

¶ Anchora digo che in alchuni loghi in l'arte si troua lo quarto magistro coe che fa contra lo terço, coe lo contrario de lo rimedio. E questo e lo magistro quarto chiamado magistro quatro.

¶ E de chiamado per contrario.

¶ Ben che pochi çogi passano lo terço magistro in l'arte.

¶ E si piu si fano, se furano a periculo. E basta di queste ditto.

¶ Como noi auemo parlado qui dinanci de le guardie d'abraçare, e del secondo magistro coe del rimedio e deli soi çugadori, e del terço magistro che fa contra lo secondo magistro e a suoy çugadori, e del quarto magistro che e chiamado contra contrario, cosi come questi magistri e çugadori ano a regere l'arte da braçare in arme e sença arme, cosi ano questi magistri e çugadori a regere l'arte de la lança e le lance, e loro guardie, magistri e çugadori.

¶ E per lo simile cu la açça, e cu la spada d'una mano e de dey mani. E per lo simile cu la daga.

¶ Si che per effetto questi magistri e çugadori detti dinanci cu le insegnore loro e diuise ano a regere tutta l'arte d'armiçare a pe e a cauallo in arme e sença arme.

¶ Secondo ch'elli fano in lo çogho del abraçare.

¶ E questo si intende solamente per che cosi bisogna esser guardie e magistri in le altre arte e i remedi e contrari come in l'arte de abraçare, açe che lo libro si possa licertamente intendere.

¶ Ben che le subscripte le figure, e li çoghi mostrarano tutta l'arte, si bene che tutta la si pora intendere.

¶ E nui attendemo ale figure depinte e a lor çoghi e a loro parole le quale ne mostrana la uerita.

### Translation — Claude Vision

[continuing the eight things wrestling requires:] ...that is knowing quick advantages, knowing how to make breaks -- that is breaking arms and legs, knowing locks -- that is binding the arms so that a man has no more defence nor can free himself. Knowing how to strike to the most dangerous places.

Also knowing how to throw someone to the ground without danger to oneself.

Also knowing how to dislocate arms and legs in various ways.

And all of which things will be found and may be depicted in this book, step by step as the art requires.

We have said what wrestling requires. Now let us speak of the guards of wrestling.

The guards of wrestling can be made in various ways. And one way is better than another.

But these four guards are the best in armour and without armour. Although the guards do not have stability because of the sudden holds that are made.

The first four masters that you see with crowns on their heads -- through them are shown the guards of wrestling. That is: Posta Longa [Long Position], and Dente di Cenghiaro [Boar's Tusk], which work one against the other. And then Porta di Ferro [Iron Gate] and Posta Frontale [Frontal Position], one against the other.

And these four guards can do all the things said before about wrestling in armour and without armour. That is: holds and locks and breaks.

Now it is necessary to explain how the guards are distinguished from the masters, the players, the scholars from the players, the players from the masters, and the remedy from the counter -- although the counter is always placed after the remedy. And sometimes the remedy after, or after all its plays, and of this we shall make clear.

We say that to observe the guards or positions is a light thing, since the guards have their weapons in hand one against the other, and they do not touch one another.

And they stand watchful and firm one against the other to see what the companion will do.

These are called positions or guards or first masters of the battle.

And they wear a crown on their head, because they are placed in position and in a way to make great defence with such a posture.

And they are the beginning of that art -- that is, of the art of the weapon with which the said masters stand in guard.

And "position" means the same as "guard."

And "guard" means that a man guards himself, and defends himself with it, from the blows of his enemy.

And "position" means the way to await his enemy and not to attack him without danger to himself.

The other master who follows the four guards comes to attack from the guards, and comes to defend himself from another player with the blows that issue from the four guards that come before. This master also wears a crown, and is called the second master.

He is also called the remedy master, because he makes the remedy so that blows are not given to him, or that no injury is done to him in that art

[continuing in right column:] ...which are the said positions or guards.

And this second, that is the remedy, has several players under him who play those plays that the master before them -- that is the remedy -- could play, taking that cover or hold that the said remedy makes. And these players will wear a device at the knee. And these players will make all the plays of the remedy until there is found another master who will make the counter to the remedy and to all his players.

And because he acts against the remedy and against its players, he will wear the device of the remedy master and his players -- that is the crown on his head and the device below the knee.

And this one is called the third master, and is called the counter, and he will do this for the other masters and in similar plays.

Furthermore I say that in some places in the art there is found a fourth master -- that is one who acts against the third, that is the counter to the remedy. And this is the fourth master, called master four.

And he is called the counter-counter.

Although few plays pass the third master in the art.

And if more are made, they would be dangerous. And enough has been said of these things.

As we have spoken here before of the guards of wrestling, and of the second master -- that is of the remedy and his players, and of the third master who acts against the second master and his players, and of the fourth master who is called counter-counter -- just as these masters and players govern the art of wrestling in armour and without armour, so do these masters and players govern the art of the lance and the lances, and their guards, masters and players.

And likewise with the axe, and with the sword in one hand and in two hands. And likewise with the dagger.

So that in effect these masters and players mentioned above, with their insignia and devices, govern all the art of arms on foot and on horseback, in armour and without armour.

According as they do in the play of wrestling.

And this is to be understood solely because it is necessary that there be guards and masters in the other arts and in the remedies and counters as in the art of wrestling, so that the book may be clearly understood.

Although the figures written below, and the plays, will show all the art, so well that all of it may be understood.

And we attend to the painted figures and to their plays and to their words, which show us the truth.

### Notes

- This folio completes the prologue/introduction of the treatise. The folio number "4" is visible in the upper right corner.
- The eight attributes of wrestling (begun at the end of fol. 2r) are completed here: (1) strength, (2) speed, (3) knowledge of quick advantages, (4) knowing how to break arms/legs, (5) knowing locks, (6) knowing how to strike dangerous places, (7) knowing how to throw without danger to oneself, (8) knowing how to dislocate arms/legs.
- The pedagogical system is fully explained: First Master (guards, wears crown) -> Second Master / Remedy (defences, wears crown) -> Scholars/Players (plays of the remedy, wear garter at knee) -> Third Master / Counter (counters the remedy, wears crown + garter) -> Fourth Master / Counter-Counter (rare).
- The four guards of wrestling named: Posta Longa, Dente di Cenghiaro, Porta di Ferro, Posta Frontale.
- The closing line -- "E nui attendemo ale figure depinte e a lor çoghi e a loro parole le quale ne mostrana la uerita" -- transitions from prologue to the illustrated portion of the manuscript.
- Corrections from existing OCR: "ayagro/ayagri" consistently normalized to "magistro/magistri" (the OCR had many garbled readings of this word); various other abbreviations expanded more consistently.

---

## Folio 06r
**Section:** Grappling

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top-left:
¶ Aqui posta longa e aqui te aspetto. E in la posta
che la porta. Lo mio braco sinistro che [?] fin
mandar avanti. E in la mia fiancha la man drita hoe certo
de meterte in la presa che piu me piaxe. E de con-
preso in ato e faro anchor. ¶ E aquella posta [?] com-
plimento e rixponder. ¶ Lo alboro porti de Zugadore
zascuno intende.

Top-right:
¶ A mi e de Zughare contra e ce acogno. Di
rompere la mia posta corno un cugno. Senpre
in altra [?] mi defendo altero. Lo
se in terra faro e quaralengho. ¶ E aquella dire
piu me folla e con trato [?]. E che nudo anchora
dexo de porta e contra. Che se trovara in guardia
de sbianchare. In quella mude che fino bixognare
lo zugare.

Bottom-left:
¶ In posta de dente io si te aspetto sença molta
e li chapeze la presta strenta mia posta. La gobe
de beze aquella e mal acre. Che li mie braze
freza e grande piere. deno la forza piu
ti malaxe piane. Chi contra me fa stonpeze de
longe zente. E che ale contra me con lo propria
quadraguinta. ¶ E le prese propia si e faro lo era com-
plimento.

Bottom-right:
¶ Io son Frontale fin e quadraguinta lo propria.
Che in questa posta meglio io me faro aspetre
zoe in lo mancho di questa presa. Due ligare
e li mazorni de presa de dente e contra e forte
che punirli i infiermi. De lugidire e remenare e
faro bon reconplire. E per lo abrazare de contra e
quadraguinta. E le presi quadraguinte io mi faro
finaremente.

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
Here I stand in the long guard and here I wait for you. And in the position that bears [the gate]. My left arm reaches forward. And on my right flank I have the certainty of putting you into whatever hold pleases me most. And from the hold I will also act. And that position [has its] completion and response. The tree [of combat] that every player understands.

Top-right:
It is for me to play against you and to break my guard like a wedge. Always in another [position] I defend proudly. I will throw you to the ground and [?]. And that one says more to me with a counter [?]. And who is also bare [unarmed] descends from the gate and against. Who finds themselves in a guard of displacement. In that change that is needed for the play.

Bottom-left:
In the position of the tooth I wait for you without much [hesitation] and I seize the quick, tight hold of my position. The hump of [that] strike is evil and bitter. For my arms are quick and strike with great force. Who comes against me is stunned from afar. And who comes against me with their own counter-hold. And the proper holds are to make the completion.

Bottom-right:
I am the Frontal [position] and the proper counter-hold. For in this position I can best make myself ready, that is in the lesser of this hold. Two binds and the greater holds of the tooth and against the strong one who punishes the weak. To flee and to recover and to make a good counter. And through the embrace against the counter-hold. And the counter-holds I will make finely.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

| 
I am Posta Longa and I seek you like this. And in response to the first grapple that you attempt on me I will bring my right arm up under your left arm. And I will then execute the first play of Grappling. And with that lock I will force you to the ground. And if that lock looks like it will fail me, then I will switch to one of the other locks that follow.

| 
I counter you with Dente di Zenghiaro. And with this move I am sure to break your grip. And from this guard I can transition to Porta di Ferro, which will force you to the ground. And if my plan fails me because of your defense, I will seek other ways to hurt you, for example with breaks, binds and dislocations, as you see depicted in these drawings.

| 
I wait for you without moving in Porta di Ferro, ready to grapple with all of my skill. And this guard can be applied not only in the art of grappling, but also in the art of the Spear, the Poleaxe, the Sword, and the Dagger. For I am Porta di Ferro, full of danger. Those who oppose me will always end up in pain and suffering. And as for those of you who come against me trying to get your hands on me, I will force you to the ground.

| 
I am Posta Frontale, used to get my hands on you. Now if I come against you in this guard, you may lay hands on me. But I will then move from this guard, and with skill I will take you down to Porta di Ferro. Then I will make you suffer as if you had fallen into the depths of hell. And I will serve you so effectively with locks and dislocations, that you will quickly acknowledge my superiority. And as long as I don’t forget my skills, I will gain my superior holds.

### Notes

Four standing figures arranged in a 2x2 grid. All figures appear to be wrestlers in various guards/ready positions. Each wears a crown (indicating the master of that technique). Top-left figure stands with arms extended in a long guard. Top-right figure stands with arms wide. Bottom-left figure is a woman in a dress in a wrestling stance (unusual — likely representing the "Posta de Dente" / Position of the Tooth). Bottom-right shows two figures in close engagement. The number "8" appears in the upper right corner. This is the beginning of the wrestling (abrazare) section. Some text is quite difficult to read due to fading and the manuscript hand.

**Section:** Grappling

i zachemo. E presa cengulara i contra ligare e in guerzella propia.

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
This is the first hold of wrestling. And here the cheek [grip]. It is a belt hold and counter-bind and proper twist. For my right hand goes under the left hand of the scholar. She [the technique] makes me do the frontal bind. And to break [?] the hold of the pledge is above for gripping and revenge. Seize the collar at the waist. And through this play break it. Well with strength, against the counter for the good player.

Top-right:
The Scholar of the first master. And who by the [?] proper to him. Accompanied, who is faithful and of the [?]. The hand guards and in the chain of the player's hold. A good and straight serpentine guard [?] against the established position. The short hold.

Bottom-left:
This Scholar demonstrates that I can well place my hand in the hold of the scholar. And the counter is [controlled] in my cover. Preparing well and the Scholar. For whoever rages, the players place the hand of the Scholar in the hold of the master under the opponent. The counter placed well above. The Scholar knows the cloth of this [technique]. That the hold separates and the Scholar [acts] well. To measure the change. In the [?] a rod. I seize the left at the shoulder to reverse.

Bottom-right:
And this is the fourth coupling of the embrace that the third [teaches] me. The Scholar goes to the [positions] and I will do [?]. The encounter. And also to operate. That this [?] of the Scholar does not belt [?]. That position well carried. And turns to the held turn. And this is the embrace. And whoever loses the foot. And the attachment goes [?] to the hand and then. [?].

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

| 
This is the first play of Abrazare and from every grappling guard you can arrive at this play, and from this position, proceed as follows: jam his right inside elbow with your left hand, and bring your right hand up behind and against his left elbow as shown. Now quickly make the second play, that is to say, having gripped him like this, turn your body to the left, and as a result he either goes to the ground or his arm will be dislocated.

| 
As the Scholar of the First Abrazare Remedy Master says, I am certain to put this man to the ground, either by breaking or dislocating his left arm. And if the Zugadore who fights with the First Abrazare Remedy Master takes his left hand off the shoulder of the Remedy Master in order to make a defense, then I will quickly let go of his right arm with my left hand and instead seize his left leg with my left hand, and grip his throat with my right hand in order to throw him to the ground, as you see depicted in the third play.

| 
The scholar that came before me speaks truly that from his hold he will force his opponent to the ground or dislocate his left arm. As he told you, if the Zugadore takes away his left hand from the shoulder of the Remedy Master, then the Remedy Master transitions to the Third Play, as you see depicted here. Thus, the First play and the Second play are really one single play, where the Remedy Master forces the Zugadore to the ground with a turn of his body, while in this Third play the Zugadore is thrown to the ground onto his back.

| 
This is the Fourth Play of Abrazare, by which the Scholaro [Student] can easily force the Zugadore to the ground. And if he cannot force him to ground like this, he will seek other plays and techniques and use other methods, as you will see depicted below. You should know that the plays and the techniques will not always work in every situation, so if you do not have a good hold, you should quickly seek one, so as not to let your opponent gain any advantage over you.

### Notes

Four wrestling scenes in a 2x2 grid. Top-left: Two wrestlers grappling, one wearing a crown (the master), executing a grip at the face/cheek. Top-right: Two wrestlers in a close hold, one appears to be countering. Bottom-left: Two wrestlers in close engagement with one attempting a throw or reversal, the crowned figure (master) applying technique. Bottom-right: Two wrestlers locked in a tight mutual embrace/bear hug. Text is heavily abbreviated and difficult to read in places. Red and blue paragraph markers throughout. This page continues the abrazare (wrestling) section.

## Folio 07r
**Section:** Grappling

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top-left:
¶ Questa presa che e la mia mane destra. Ala testa
del scolaro. pereme botare e presa. e ze questa la
volta stabile [?] Anchora hopo che in lo pegio zo in la
mia mane sinextra. soto la sua [?] destra i posofora
de fare piu cerco le mundere. in tera.

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
This hold where my right hand is at the head of the scholar. I can then push and grip. And this is the stable turn. Also I need [to place] my left hand, under his right [arm], and I can then make the circle to throw him to the ground.

Top-right:
I am his counter. Because of the hand in position, I take from him and from the hand under the arm and the arms are locked or held. And I send [him] stable. And I change to the nature of retreat. And I change short so that he cannot [resist]. And from me the embrace makes a certain hold.

Bottom-left:
For the hold called the counter-four. In the way that I make him turn. I seize the arms and my strength. And from the point. I take the full cover. The one above who pretends. Of the beak [?]. And the counter alone does not flourish against the counter.

Bottom-right:
With pain you could hold firm your cover. For I wait against you and this one goes in a manner against. Hold the Scholar. And the counter other one [?] I will make the bindings from your [strikes] more times. Which return. And which [?] can be stable. The one who covers those who change. The lock upon them. That through half with the iron [dagger?] holder also.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

| 
This grip that I make with my right hand at your throat will bring you pain and suffering, and with it I will force you to the ground. Also let me tell you that if I seize you under your left knee with my right hand, I will be even more certain of driving you into the ground.

| 
I am the counter of the Fifth Play [10] that is shown earlier. And let me explain that if with my right hand I push up the elbow of his hand that seeks to harm me, I will turn him in such a way that either I will force him to the ground, as you see here depicted, or I will gain a hold or a lock, and so I will have little concern for his grappling skills.

| 
From this hold that I have gained, and by the way I hold you, I will lift you off the ground with my strength and throw you down under my feet head first with your body following. And as far as I am concerned, you will not be able to counter me.

| 
When I press my thumb under your ear you will feel so much pain that you will go to the ground for sure, or I will make other hold or lock that will be worse than torture for you. The counter that can be made is the Sixth play [11] made against the Fifth Play [10] when he puts his hand underneath his opponent’s elbow. This counter can certainly be done to me here.

### Notes

Four wrestling scenes in a 2x2 grid. Top-left: One wrestler (no crown) gripping the other's head with his right hand, preparing a throw. Top-right: A crowned figure (master) in close embrace with opponent, executing a counter-technique. Bottom-left: Two wrestlers locked in a standing grapple, one attempting to control the other's arms. Bottom-right: Two wrestlers in a tight clinch, heads pressed together, both gripping. The number "9" appears in upper right. Continues the abrazare (wrestling) section. Text is faded and difficult in several places.

## Folio 07v
**Section:** Grappling

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

e que se mudo le piu cresene.
Che io ce [?] de stalare ale

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
You seized me from behind and my hands are raised. And so I change to the stronger [position]. For I can [?] stand against my good grip. And from this play it was the parting. That those who wish to make it stand, those who lose fail more.

Top-right:
This is a play of the leg-hook (gambarola) that I know well how to execute and bring to its end. And no one can tell the pain and the [?]. Then the flower [technique] is proper in the run.

Bottom-left:
This is a play. And with a proper arm-lock (Braxolna), the one on the ground [?] and the one who counters. And that one who seizes places [the grip] at the head and through his cover and beyond the punching. And this is the change of the [technique] above only, that those who resist scrape. The cover of this figure and the raised hands run [?].

Bottom-right:
From this I will make the hip-throws of the fourth downward, growing and greater. More attached to the bindings in the line. The flourishing that is [?] that the hip-throw comes from the leg and flourishing in [?] another end of the pain and the sea [?]. Touch the maker of the force when you suffer the throws. And falling to the ground.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

| 
You seized me from behind in order to throw me to the ground, and I turned like this. And if I fail to throw you to the ground you will have a lucky escape. This play is a good finishing move, but unless this is done quickly, this remedy will fail.

| 
This is a play that involves a throw over the leg [Gambarola] which is a risky move in grappling. So if you want to make this leg throw successfully, you will need to do it with power and speed.

| 
This is a finishing move and it is a good way to hold someone, because they cannot defend themselves. For the counter, the one who is being held should move as quickly as he can over to a wall or a post and drive himself backwards against it so that the man holding him breaks his head or his back against the aforementioned wall or post.

| 
This student strikes his opponent with a knee to the groin to gain advantage in order to throw him to the ground. To make the counter, when your opponent comes in quickly to strike you in the groin with his knee, seize his right leg under the knee with your right hand, and throw him to the ground.

### Notes

Four wrestling scenes in a 2x2 grid. Top-left: One wrestler has seized the other from behind around the waist; the seized figure has arms raised. Top-right: A wrestler executing a leg-hook (gambarola) technique on an opponent who is being bent backward. Bottom-left: One figure standing with arm extended (possibly demonstrating a technique), the other at distance. Bottom-right: Two wrestlers grappling, one attempting a hip throw, legs intertwined. Text contains several wrestling-specific terms: gambarola (leg hook), braxolna (arm lock). Some portions heavily faded and uncertain.

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
Again I who place the grip with my arms at the chest of my [opponent]. I find my hands steady in this cover. And in all the grappling in this always-hold of Lucheria. The counter of the seizing sign that is the power that [the one] who grips me uses of the striker. And the cover. Death if his hand crosses to take the ruler of the cover. Through the arms [he] will make and attach the hold. That the Scholar grieves and in his double-strength.

Top-right:
The counter of this is what I will do. The end cut of my play makes a counter. And through the changes the guides and the tripod [stance]. In it I am not covered, ready to counter. [?]

Bottom-left:
And for a hold of the arms alone I maintain my arms and I will do it. And my hands go five [fingers] under the grip. That the cover-maker made [?] the cover of the side. To flourish at the face then do not follow. In the tripod [stance] secure of the tree [technique] part.

Bottom-right:

Of the lost ones [?] cover and Leap Position of the [?] that the tripod of it will make the seizing. And the raised [?] to rule the [?] the sacks of the cover. And ready that I guard, I will make the play.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

| 
If you seize me with both your arms underneath mine, I will strike with both my hands into your face. And even if you were well armored this would still make you let go. The counter of this play is to place your right hand under the left elbow of your opponent and push hard upwards, and you will be able to free yourself.

| 
This shows how I make the counter to the thirteenth play [18]. As you can see his hands have been removed from my face. And from this hold, if I fail to throw him to the ground I will be worthy of your disdain.

| 
If you come to grips with both your arms underneath your opponent's, then you can attack his face as you see depicted, especially if his face is not protected. You can also transition from here into the third play of grappling.

| 
This is the counter to the fourteenth play [20], and to any other play where my opponent has his hands in my face while grappling with me. If his face is unprotected, I push my thumbs into his eyes. If his face is protected, I push up under his elbow and quickly move to a presa or a ligadura.

### Notes

Four wrestling scenes in a 2x2 grid, page numbered "10" in upper right. Top-left: Two wrestlers in close grapple, one (larger figure) seizing the other at the chest/torso area. Top-right: A crowned figure (master) in a defensive posture against an approaching opponent. Bottom-left: Two wrestlers, one controlling the other's arm in what appears to be an arm lock. Bottom-right: Two figures in a striking/grappling exchange at distance — this may be transitioning toward dagger combat. Text is quite faded and the handwriting is challenging in many places; several readings are uncertain.

## Folio 08v
**Section:** Baton

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top-left:

legale. E io bene ze cugie le mane. pocho lauze e
zente in la coperte. ¶ E che fia ello custipe pero che ven scontri

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
Guard, for the lizard-grip (luchertola) holds you for a legal seizing. And I well take up the hands. Little praise and people in the cover. And that he who is in the hold because he comes to meet in the parts. The lizard-grip makes the hour of entry. He cannot return to my equal play.

Top-right:
If I were well armed in this play to then enter. Considering your quickness. My lizard-grip in the counter. And I am equal to this. Acquire the bottles [?] from behind the figure [?] to make the cut.

Bottom-left:
The Sport [?] that counters in the oath. Countered by ugly throws from below in that final grip the gate that from the scholar is countered. The [?] covers the weakness of the cover. And make from above my [?] from the stumble by foot. That [which] I will do in the lowness. The sight of the wash by foot. The counter to my greater is never a counter.

Bottom-right:
To raise [?] I will do this play and then to this I will make my battle with fury. My [?] for the foot. The [?] of my [?] for the play. And to strike [?]. The counter that [?] by the way of the middle [?] to see. And stable with the figures. And the counter of my master is never [beaten].

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

| 
See how with a short staff I hold you bound by your neck. And from here if I wish to throw you to the ground I will have little trouble doing so. And if I choose to do worse to you I can keep this strong bind applied. And you will not be able to counter this play.

| 
If you were well armored then I would prefer to make this play against you than the previous one. Now that I have caught you between your legs with the short staff, you are stuck riding it like a horse, but you won't be trapped like this long before I turn you upside down onto your back.

[3] I am the Student of the Sixth Remedy Master of the Daga, who counters in this way with his dagger. And it is in his honor that I make this cover with my short staff. And from here I will rise quickly to my feet and I will make the plays of my Master. And this cover that I have made with a short staff can also be done with a hood. And the counter to this move is the same counter shown by my Master [in the dagger section].

[4] I have taken this remedy from the Eighth Remedy Master of the Dagger, and I can defend myself armed only with this short staff. And having made this cover I rise to my feet, and I can then make all of the plays of my Master. And I could defend myself in this way equally well with a hood or a piece of rope. And the counter to this move is the same counter shown by my Master.

### Notes

Four scenes in a 2x2 grid. This page marks a transition — the top two scenes show unarmed wrestling (figures are unclothed/in hose), while the bottom two scenes show figures wearing blue clothing and holding daggers (short blades visible). Top-left: Two wrestlers, one attempting the "luchertola" (lizard) grip. Top-right: Two wrestlers in a standing grapple, one wearing a crown. Bottom-left: Two figures in blue garments, one seated on a stool/bench while the other stands with a dagger — this appears to be a dagger defense scenario. Bottom-right: Two figures in blue with daggers engaged in combat. This folio represents the transition from the abrazare (wrestling) section to the daga (dagger) section. Text is heavily faded and very difficult to read in places.
