## Folio 09r
**Section:** Dagger

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Header (top of page):
¶ Queste zinque figure. sofo le guardie de la daga. e tale e duna in arme. e tale e duna senza
arme in arme. e tale e duna in armo e senza arme. e tale e duna in arme. e una senza arme e
fazano queste moy de zugharono.

Top-left:
¶ E fin questa posta. de fioreze e che fiorazugo.
in fin longa. chi armare e piaga. poy che la paga
zoltanere. e che mi propi e presia. ¶ Questo fia
guere e dago e fiaga e fin moi covarieze.

Top-right:
¶ E fin questa presa de fiore e che bixogna. e fin [?]
in arme e fiagha. e piu me sol di Varmerate.
fiagha. e de fin. guardare ali posta vipere braza.

Center:
¶ E fin megza posta. de fiore e che
Regia me ze rebadida. e fin poy in
arme alla fiaga e cho sol posa fien
forma de la fiore. e petre romada e
pagera a de la posta. e la mia. Pixolarma
De zexia zagaveria a e [?].

Bottom-left:
¶ E fin me zagara foro de fioreze. in la
quale lo guardo ogni fiore la guardia.
dello [?] fiol fiaga e Mastro le codere
faro per me. che fiaga e chi pagere e aqua
cavalere de fiaco ali pagere e aqua fere.

Bottom-right:
¶ E fin questa posta. de fiorezze. e la benezia. da
come e fiala. e fin bixogna. ¶ e fin fiori zagare
e [?] guardia. per fiore e piaga. ¶ e fin zagale
aconta mia sol fin firo [?] figolire. ¶ [?] e fiaghe
covariese largale. ¶ e alo [?] pauta
covariese larghe.

### Translation — Claude Vision

Header:
These five figures are the guards of the dagger. And some are [shown] in armor, and some are without armor, in armor. And some are in armor and without armor. And some are in armor. And one without armor, and they make these plays of combat.

Top-left:
And in this position, of the flower [Fiore] and the flower-play. In the long [guard]. Who arms and strikes. Then the payment is held. And my proper hold and grip. This makes war and the dagger and strike and my covers.

Top-right:
And in this position of the flower and what is needed. And in arms and strikes. And more to me alone of armor-piercing strikes. And to guard at the viper's arm position.

Center:
And in the middle position. Of the flower and the line [?] that is rebuffed. And then in arms to the strike and I alone can in the form of the flower. And the stone [?] returns and will pay for the position. And my Scholar. Of [?] the play and [?].

Bottom-left:
And my play outside of the flower. In which I guard every flower the guard. Of the [?] son [student] strikes and the Master makes the covers for me. That strikes and pays and water [?] the cavalier [knight] of the flank to the payment and water [?].

Bottom-right:
And in this position of the flower. And the beauty. From what it is. And is needed. And flower plays and guard. For flower and strike. And play accounts to me alone [?]. And strikes, covers, wide. And at [?] position covers wide.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[1] These five figures are the guards of the dagger; and some are good in armor; and some are good without armor; and some are good both in or out of armor; and some are good in armor but not good without armor; and all these are displayed below.

[2] [Full Iron Gate, Single]

I am Full Iron Gate Single. And I am good in armour and without armour, because I can ward off an attack with or without moving to grapple. And I can play with or without a dagger when I make my covers.

[3] [Full Iron Door, Doubled]

I am Full Iron Gate Doubled, and I am good in armour and without armour, but in all situations I am better in armour than without armour, and with a guard like this I cannot use a dagger.

[4] [Middle Iron Gate, Doubled]

I am Middle Iron Gate with dagger in hand and I am doubled, and I am better and more strong than any of the others, and I am good in armour and without armour, and I can cover low and high on either side.

[5] [Full Iron Door, Doubled and Crossed]

And I am Full Iron Gate with the arms crossed and doubled. And I am like a mighty fortress, and in armour I am especially strong. But without armour I am not sufficient, because I cannot cover long.

[6] [Middle Iron Gate, Doubled and Crossed]

And I am Middle Iron Gate doubled and crossed [with dagger]. And I am good in armour but not without armour, because I cannot cover long, but I can cover above and below, from the right and the left, with or without a dagger.

### Notes

This is a key page: the introduction of the dagger (daga) guards. Five standing figures are arranged on the page (not the usual 2x2 grid) — two at top, one in center, two at bottom. Each figure holds a dagger and demonstrates a different guard position. Several wear crowns (masters). The header text explicitly states these are "le guardie de la daga" (the guards of the dagger) and notes that some apply in armor and some without. The figures appear to be: top-left in a forward guard, top-right in a raised guard, center in a middle guard, bottom-left in a low guard, bottom-right in an open/wide guard. Text is quite faded and the Venetian dialect makes many terms challenging. The word "fioreze/fiore" (flower) appears frequently — this is both the author's name (Fiore) and a technical term.

---

## Folio 09v
**Section:** Dagger

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top-left:
¶ Del covelle prudelle che ze zugadore le beleze
sotte delugra. la mia che fia [?] se fira coverieze
quato che ma le fiore. ¶ Per e covelle mese
contraleze [?] le mia fioreze. ¶ Bene covella maza
ande pri fioreze fiore. cho serto fio e ri ale
che la cria a que [?] tuto le setre ce abandone.
in fie desfiandole e quando la sena.

Top-right:
¶ Per fendereze possi fiorere la mia copete de orpo del [?] in
Posta ala fiorezata dela verta. ¶ Col le mede in ze ch el fa
ra lucheria. cinque piode rostre. co e che questo fiozore
tochase.

Middle (center, below a single standing figure):
¶ La daga che un pe mezo usarpe la mia verta
per fiorere in fin fiorese le petre. chie piu e forte.
¶ E fioreze in le mane. piazdale per muche codare.

Bottom (large text block spanning page width):
¶ Io fin la rubrica acorta ¶ governeda i daga. che li Zigalo fiorese. zucho
per fuora. ¶ Colla se inquete urai maledeta e male corte. ¶ Degno fiodale
contrariado contrat covese fiare ¶ E per fioreze Podere treze covelle larze
gela. e re a fioreze che se ditre mali zugalar. ¶ E che me achidene in le fiorita
[?] recoze coverte. ¶ Coverte e guarde fiorezze de la dalo coverte. ¶ E stampre lo
daga ere verezare e lugitoreze. ¶ E che in che e in le mia contrate arme me
arrendellare.

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
Of the wise covers that are the beauties of the player, under the [?]. My [technique] that [?] will be covers. As much as the flower [teaches] me. For the covers placed as counters to my flower-plays. Good cover of the mace [?] first flower plays. That [?] certain [?] and to the cry [?] that all the secrets are abandoned. In the challenging and when the scene [unfolds].

Top-right:
To defend, I can flourish my cover of the body in the position of the flowered [guard] of the truth [virtue]. With the changes that make the lizard-grip [luchertola]. Five rooster-steps [?]. And that this flower-play touches.

Middle:
The dagger that by half-measure usurps my virtue to flourish finally the flower-plays and the stones [blows]. Who is the stronger. And flower-plays in the hands. The pieces [?] for many tails [?].

Bottom:
I am the short rubric, governed by the dagger, that the players of the flower play from outside. With restless, cursed and evil court. Worthy [?] countered against the covers to make. And to flower the Power draws covers wide and sharp. And to the flower that is said [to be] evil play. And that my [?] in the flower-covered recovers and covers. Covers and guards of flower of the given cover. And always the dagger is to turn and the lizard-grip. And that in my countered arms surrenders me.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[7] I am the noble weapon named the dagger who plays at very close range, and he who understands my malice and my art will also gain a good understanding of many other weapons. And since I finish my fight fiercely and quickly, there is no man who can stand against my method. Whoever witnesses my deeds of arms will see me make covers and thrusts as I move to grapple, and will see me take away the dagger by dislocating and binding arms, and against me neither weapons nor armour will be of any use.

| 
[8] Everyone should take care when facing the perilous dagger, and your arms, hands and elbows must go quickly against it, to do these five things, namely: take away the dagger; strike; dislocate the arms; bind the arms; and force your opponent to the ground. And never fail to do one or the other of these five things; And may he who seeks to defend himself protect himself in this way.

[9] With the fendente I can strike to the head and the body from the elbow up to the top of the head. But below the elbow I cannot be sure that I can make this strike without danger, and therefore I am reluctant to strike lower.

| 
[10] From the left (reverse) side, you can strike from the elbow to end at the temple of the head. And these are called the colpi mezani (middle strikes). And these reverse strikes from the left cannot be delivered if you are still waiting to make cover against your opponent’s attack.

| 
[11] From the right side you can strike or cover if needed, and your target ranges from the elbows to the temples of the head. And this strike is more safely made from the right side than made from the left side.

| 
[12] The dagger that goes through the middle towards the head strikes below the chest and never higher. And while striking you should at all times make cover with your left hand.

### Notes

This page has an unusual layout. Top section has two text blocks flanking illustrations (top-left: two figures grappling/fighting with daggers; top-right: text only with a small partial figure). Middle section has a single standing figure holding a dagger in each hand with arms spread wide, wearing a crown — this appears to be a master figure demonstrating a guard position. A large block of text spans the bottom of the page. This appears to be a summary or rubric text for the dagger section, as the text itself mentions "la rubrica" (the rubric). The crowned central figure with two daggers is distinctive. Text is very challenging throughout — heavily abbreviated, faded, and in dense Venetian dialect.

---

## Folio 10r
**Section:** Dagger

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top-left:
¶ Io son lo primo zugadore. e le mie mane a prexe. zo la
ponta. lo mie braze de lo fiore e son invenzebelle. che batagla
lui mie rege in la lo posa de la matere al bizo
la fiorezta de zugare. ¶ che la presa za contra del
lo fiorezta soy fiorezta. Cho le mie fore ze le pe
le fiorezta.

Top-right:
¶ Io son li coveze. tuto che soy perta. in tenpla due cele.
che za questa. Fioria covere moderare. che se atin no pina e
Machardo in mie sole. cola covarie moi oposte il salutero.
cover faro. ¶ E la mie foro perno in fin e [?] coverte
soltate.

Bottom-left:
¶ Io fin zugolare de covarte. quando me fiorezta. io so e la
scolaro. dare in coverte un suo fiore. ¶ in lo mio trezene e [?] moi
io potrere. Che ze perta le [?] scolaro e retegna. che e chel
covire leze me de fiorire.

Bottom-right:
¶ Io son de demostraxe e che io e tregna aquello che baste arme
che mio prala. e che coviglereze no posta. e tele zugadore
e li mane. deta dele e fizogna. ¶ E lo contrar lo re che ce posa
moe. e la le mane. fortefra per alo che lo mio
alo fie reteze.

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
I am the first player. And my hands seize the point. My arms of the flower are invincible. The battle, my king in the position of the master to the need of the flower-play of combat. That the hold against the flower-play, his flower-play. With my strength at the feet of the flower-play.

Top-right:
I am the covers. All that is open. In the temple, two heavens. For this. The flower covers moderate. That is not [?] and [?] in my sole. With my opposite covers I will salute the cover I make. And my [?] through finally the covered [?].

Bottom-left:
I am the player of covers. When the flower-play [comes], I am the scholar. To give in cover his flower [strike]. In my drawing and my [?] I can [do it]. That the open [?] scholar holds back. And that the cover teaches me to flourish.

Bottom-right:
I am the demonstrator and I hold that which is enough arms [weapons]. That my [?] and the covers are not positioned. And such a player and the hands. Said of the [?] need. And the counter the king that can [?]. And the hands. Strongly for that which my [position] holds back.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

| 
In my right hand I hold your dagger, and I gained it through my skill, which is so good that if you draw a dagger on me, I will take it from your hand. And I know well how to strike to finish you, no matter what advantage you might have.

| 
I choose to symbolize my skill with the broken arms I carry. And I do not lie when I tell you that I have broken and dislocated many arms in my life. And whoever chooses to go against my art, will find me always ready to use that art against him.

| 
I am the Master of the unlocking and locking of the arms of those who choose to oppose me. I will cause them great pain and suffering with my techniques of binding and dislocating. And therefore I carry these keys to signify the value of my art.

| 
You ask how it is that I have this man held under my feet. Thousands have suffered this fate because of my art of Abrazare. And I carry the victory palm in my right hand, because no one can stand up to my grappling skills.

### Notes

Four figures in a 2x2 grid. All appear to be dagger combat scenes. Top-left: A figure holding a dagger with arms raised, wearing a crown (master), facing an opponent in a guard position — both in short tunics. Top-right: A crowned figure holding what appears to be a mace or short weapon, in an upright stance facing an opponent. Bottom-left: A figure in long robes/garments (possibly a scholar/student figure) with a dagger, in a defensive posture. Bottom-right: A figure kneeling/on the ground while another stands over them — this may depict the conclusion of a technique or a submission. Page numbered "12" in upper right corner. The dagger section continues with plays and counter-plays.

## Folio 10v
**Section:** 1st Master

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top-left:
¶ Io fin primo e magistro e chiamado remedie per che.
armado vado e calori che soltare me condotare che me no
fa tolo. e cheli possa e fioreze la mie covare non pora.
esse. ¶ E le questo mie meteze no fi per fiorezze. la mie dago
fiore covarieze. Lainando la mia covize e querto
finetore.

Top-right:
¶ E con mia daga me revenire in un braze finco.
mia solta. ¶ E che fiazete guarda con la beze me no
felada arestere. la beza me ma
delle fioreze senza Daga in la mia fioreze. ¶ E me le
gotte.

Bottom-left:
¶ Elo la contrario e fendereze. de covarieze breuzze e [?] fiorezze reuale
de ze no [?] me per fiorezze deliqueza. [?] le in regla e in soltare
ze mia fiore e podere foreze. e che le figamente no in lo fo-
reza.

Bottom-right:
¶ La contrariaze del covize che mia fiorezzai. ze lo [?]
mie preste vedere a quel piazale za le preda. ¶ E con que in
za le braze. e batesela zo in la ira verta.

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
I am the first master and called the remedy because armed I go and with valor I lead, so that he cannot do [harm] to me. And his strikes and flower-plays, my covers will not fail. And this my method is not for [mere] flower-play. My dagger makes flower-covers. Raising my cover and this finisher.

Top-right:
And with my dagger I come back into an arm's [length] hold, my release. And that you watch the guard, with the [?] do not fail to stop. The [?] to me of the flower-plays without dagger in my flower-play. And the drops [of blood?].

Bottom-left:
He is the counter and defender. Of short covers and royal flower-plays. From where [?] not by flower-play of weakness. In rule and in release, my flower and power of strength. And that the fixings are not in the fortress.

Bottom-right:
The counter of the cover that my flower-play [makes]. The [?] my quick seeing at that place where the prey [is]. And with that at the arms. And striking down in the true wrath.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[17] I am the First Master of the Dagger, full of guile,
And with my left hand I will wind the dagger around your arm,
And truth to tell I can make many other plays,
And my students will do them cunningly.

I am the first master and I am called Remedy, because I know how to remedy so well that you cannot harm me whereas I on the contrary can strike you and hurt you. And I cannot make a better play against you than to make your dagger go to the ground, by turning my hand to the left.

[18] If I make a turn around your arm with my dagger,
I will strike you in the chest, and it will not be taken from me.

I will turn my dagger around your arm. And because of this counter you will not be able to take the dagger from me. And also with this turn I can drive it into your chest without a doubt.

[19] With your right arm locked under my left,
I can cause you much harm while keeping you trapped.

I will lock your arm in the middle bind, and I will do it in such a way that you will not be able to give me any trouble. And if I wish to put you to the ground I will do so with little effort, and you will have no chance of escaping.

[In the Getty, the Scholar steps with his left foot in front of his opponent's right, not behind.]

[20] If you wind around my arm and try to lock it in this way,
I will put you in the lower bind and this hold will make things hard for you.

I make the counter to the play that came before me. You can see the kind of position that I have put him in. I will break his arm or quickly throw him to the ground.

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

The first master of the dagger, I am called caution itself;
At any time, the left hand having been extended to lift the dagger away.

Truly I sweep the dagger away around your shoulder.
Not wasting that [attack], I would pulp you, miserable, in the chest.

And behold your right [arm] confined under my left
Shoulder. Far too many misfortunes delay you, the imprisoned one.

It is permitted that you hold me pressed hard inward, the lower key having been retained [and]
Then pressed hard, [which] will harm the shoulder.

### Notes

Four scenes in a 2x2 grid. All are dagger combat. Top-left: Two figures facing each other, one holding a dagger overhead (the "primo magistro" / first master called "remedie" / the remedy), the other approaching with a dagger thrust. Top-right: A crowned figure with a dagger facing an opponent — appears to be a close-quarters exchange. Bottom-left: Two figures, one appears to be a woman in a dress/long garments (or a robed figure) facing a dagger-wielding opponent — possibly depicting a counter or defensive technique. Bottom-right: Two figures in a clinch with daggers, one attempting to strike while the other controls. The text identifies the top-left master as "remedie" (the remedy), a key concept in Fiore's system — the master who provides the defensive counter to an attack. Text continues to be very challenging to read.

## Folio 11r
**Section:** 1st Master

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top-left:
¶ In bona coscienza, io tegno la longa e la man dritta denançi. Avixato
e tal presa te posso ben ligare e far ancrotta la
man deritta per lo braço per la persona te poria
de prexo te stesse anchora çoe ala guartiçone dala fi-
nistra.

Top-right:
¶ Re contrario del çogho che poria ben rompersi tuti çi poçi
Che io no aia penna ligato in olio no rea aia la longa Ma
consigliarmi logoço in tu lo coverto messo e che son mal grado
a Nosa longa lo[?]dorso fara no romperla.

Bottom-left:
¶ Eo ... nostra fia una longe presa al schivare [?] stranordi-
naria che ala fina lo çuçigliere nodia per rana
e mitto gia la longa de la stare avra una coverta. sia
sta presa de la çuçigliere de prio. Egnale la fin l'omo
abere sera aucontrado.

Bottom-right:
¶ Eo ... questo Zogo fia la posta e quello voi batare lo braço
preso e dessio e pero florire che la presa del lo mal che con-
trare possa l'era nodia. Fia donna la çuçigliere que la fara
covrando no la chiata covara e la coverta.

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
In good conscience, I hold the long [guard] and the right hand forward. Skillfully with such a grip I can well bind you and make a lock on the right hand, through the arm, through the body I could hold you close, also at the guard of the left.

Top-right:
On the contrary of the play that could well break all the holds -- that I have no pain bound in it nor have the long [guard]. But I counsel myself at the covered position and against ill will, our long back will not break it.

Bottom-left:
Our [play] is a long grip for the evasion, extraordinary, that at the end the wrestler could [overcome], and I put the long [guard] to the standing position with a cover. This grip of the wrestler at last -- the man will be countered.

Bottom-right:
This play is the position and what you wish to beat -- the arm gripped and therefore to flourish, that the grip of the wrongful counter can [succeed]. The wrestler who makes [the cover] covering in the hidden cover and the cover.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[21] *If I can turn this arm of yours,
I will make you suffer with a middle bind.*

This is a good cover from which to take the dagger from your hand, and with this grip I will be able to bind you well. And this art is so effective that if I place my right hand under your right knee, then I will put you to the ground.

[22] *You will not make me suffer in the middle bind
When I meet you with this counter and make you let go.*

I make the counter to the play that came before me, so that you will not be able to throw me to the ground, nor take the dagger from me, nor bind me either. You will have to let go, or else you will be quickly stabbed by my dagger.

[23] This is a play with no counter, and it is inevitable that the player will go to the ground and lose his dagger if the student performs this technique as depicted. And when the player is thrown to the ground, the student can finish him in various ways.

[24] This play is rarely used in the art of the dagger, yet it is an additional defense to know. For after beating aside the attack in this way, the scholar can then strike with a counter to the ribs or the stomach.

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

If I myself can now turn the shoulder using the hands,
You, sad, will remain eternally in that middle key.

You will not make [me] endure in the middle key. But now,
By means of that my counter, it is convenient for you if you will yield to me.

### Notes

Four pairs of wrestlers depicted in unarmed combat (abrazare/wrestling). Each pair shows two figures grappling or in guard positions. Top-left figure wears a crown (master). Bottom-left figure wears a crown (master). Some text is difficult to read due to fading and the medieval script style. Several readings are uncertain due to the Venetian dialect and handwriting.

## Folio 11v
**Section:** 1st Master

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top-left:
¶ Lo fin contrario del primo çoe in longo puote romperlo. Che
nulla fia romandura per lo çogho che la sua mana francescha fi-
nira pigliandolo e çi ual pocho dato la longa in la bra-
ça possese lo mare.

Top-right:
¶ Anchora son ben contrario di quatro çogar possa e vassallo
de longo. Çoi che la presa che non fa la sua posta ma
ben nel modo lo trompare [?] de mancho mal çoi pore
e si abere çogho veri covra non fia stare lo contrariu
gie fora farsi nulla marcharo.

Bottom-left:
¶ La ... nostra ben contrario che val a trato. Ancho le çoghe
al nostra contrario che possa de un. çoe la pigliando overne
lo Cepho in la sua longa la raxon del contrarigando e con
mi del pe lo braço la longa via la pislanda al convençado
dissonora la çoghe de boy filorire. Coverto la sua piça [?] in
la pislanda. dhen io contrario la lo partido
çerle al ella gie fare.

Bottom-right:
¶ Io son pislanda del primo dio e d'imandare. Egal avetta
sentito lo çogho con la longa e ligadure le braçia
e çoe che lo corpo che la comvencura de me possa for-
ça e d'homo quela possa contendere.

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
The final counter of the first, that is, in the long [guard] can break it. That nothing will remain, through the play, that his French hand will finish by seizing, and it matters little given the long [guard] in the arm. It is possible to overcome.

Top-right:
Also I am well the counter of four plays, both a power and a vassal of the long [guard]. Since the grip that does not make his position but well in the manner of deceiving, of lesser evil to be placed, and to have the play truly covered -- it will not be the counter that comes forth as nothing.

Bottom-left:
Our [play], well a counter that succeeds at once. Also the plays of our counter that can [succeed] from one -- that is, seizing to govern the body in his long [guard]. The reason of the counter-play, and with me of the foot, the arm, the long [guard], away the wrestling at the convention. Dishonor the play to flourish. Covered, his piece in the wrestling. Then I, the counter, to the parting, seek of it to make.

Bottom-right:
I am the wrestling of the first god [play] and to demand. An equal feeling [comes] to the play with the long [guard] and binding the arms, and so that the body that [feels] the convention of me can [apply] force, and of a man that could contend.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[25] *This bind is easy for me to do
And from it I will be able to strike you in the back.*

I am a counter to the First Dagger Remedy Master. Woe to he who remedies with techniques that allow his left hand to be seized. And from this hold I will be able to drive the dagger into his back.

[These two illustrations seem to show the beginning and end of the technique.]

[29] *I make the counter to the First Master
With this cover I will hurt him and worse.*

I am also the counter of the First Dagger Remedy Master, and when his student grips me like this [10], I will strike him, and make him let go. And if he tries to do other plays against me, I will counter him without hesitation.

[30] *In the previous counter I told you that you could hurt him and worse;
Here I show you how this can be done.*

This flows from the counter referred to in the previous play. It also flows from the counter referred to two plays back [10], where the Counter Remedy Master has trapped the hand of his opponent with his dagger, and where he told you that he can drive the dagger into his opponent’s back. My play comes from that play, but where he says you drive the dagger into your opponent’s back, I drive it into his chest. But this still flows from the previous play, even though I choose to finish it differently.

[In the Getty, the Master's right foot is outside (in front) of his opponent's left foot.]

[31] *I am well placed and positioned to force you to the ground;
If you do not know the counter, I will throw you down immediately.*

I am the student of the first Master of [Dagger] Remedies. And with this grip I seek to take your dagger and bind your arm, and since I do not believe that you know how to counter me, I will do this to you without delay.

[The Getty resembles the Paris. These two illustrations may show progressive stages of the technique.]

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

It is neither labor nor pain to me to make a persistent bind,
By which route now I will be able to injure you,
And possibly I will strike your kidneys with a great wound.

[The Paris resembles the Getty.]

I certainly keep the counter of the first master,
And I will now prove this covering using many bad things.

Using a counter to the former, which threatens many evils,
I direct myself in these circumstances so that I would strike the associate with a deadly wound.

I am ready now to beat you, gloomy, into the ground.
And if the counter would miss, I would do this to you readily.

### Notes

Four pairs of wrestlers in unarmed combat. Top-left figure wears a crown (master). Bottom-left pair shows a close grappling exchange with one figure wearing a crown (master). Bottom-right shows two figures in close engagement with one wearing a short tunic. The text is heavily abbreviated and difficult to read in several places. Many readings are uncertain.

## Folio 12r
**Section:** 1st Master

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top-left:
¶ Io contrario e questo modo ti stago che tu no mi po-
nerasi la longa nol me ligare la longa. E non e man
torpe romaneresci ni lacerarme e per ti ficare vole
falsitar che tu mi fiersi. y male e marauire che lo
fiasto cia coverte.

Top-right:
¶ Questa coverta si chiama piu florença. e çoe la
fiasta e la polere so [?] passato çogho florire avanço e çi
ala val me no sia covritura. e ache boy longo
bey pe vui adre instruare.

Bottom-left:
¶ Questo el contrario di questo çogho che Nimanço che
dissonorable per florença. E lo coltre va la mol covra
florença. Tutto che la coltre e fiorili lo no mi monacha.

Bottom-right:
¶ Doy bona prexo che a tera si fiasta. un mi fiala
aia ma tu ronpesa la longa fiçava la cosa. merauilia fiase.
e çoe la sua longa dria per filorire. e questa cosa
na e moça da fiçitare.

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
I am the counter and in this way I stand so that you cannot put the long [guard] nor bind me with the long [guard]. And there is no sluggish hand that will remain nor tear me apart, and to put you down [I] want to falsify what you strike at me, and it is bad and marvelous that the blow is covered.

Top-right:
This cover is called "more Florence" [stronger flourish], and that is, the blow and the dust so [?] passed the play to flourish in advance, and so it is worth nothing to me as a cover, and also you, long boy, [come] after to instruct.

Bottom-left:
This is the counter of this play that is no less than dishonorable by flourish. And the cover goes to the soft cover of Florence [flourish]. All that the cover and to flourish -- it does not restrain me.

Bottom-right:
Two good grips that [throw] to the ground. One [is] my blow, but you break the long [guard], fixed the thing -- marvelous blow. And so his long [guard] behind to flourish, and this thing is now the girl/manner of striking.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[32] *I make the counter like this,
And I know well how to strike you from here.*

I counter you like this, so that you will neither take my dagger nor bind my arm, and my dagger and I will remain at liberty. And then I will be able to strike you when you let go of me in such a way that you will have no defense.

[33] *To make a much stronger cover I cross my arms in this manner;
And from here I can do all the previous remedies.*

This cover is known to be much stronger and I make it so as to be able to obstruct you with various plays. And you cannot overcome such a strong cover, because two arms can easily oppose one arm.

[34] *With this counter the previous cover will meet with failure;
After I have made you turn I will strike you with my dagger.*

This is the counter to the cover that came before, that I told you was much stronger. And I will turn him with my left hand. Having turned him, I will not fail to strike him.

[35] *Since my Master’s technique will not fail me,
I will break your arm over my shoulder.*

With this excellent presa that I have made against you, I will not fail to break your arm over my left shoulder. And afterwards I can strike you with your own dagger, since this play will not fail me.

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

Now I do this counter quickly; you see duly just as it were.
The spirit becoming enflamed, I would then beat your limbs.

I cover myself using great bodily strength, as you see the movements.
I attack in this way before anyone can bring about anything.

Now, by means of this counter, I cheat the earlier plays
In such a way, and I would turn you behind in order to wreck you with wounds.

Because of how that master now brings about the taking,
I believe you will not withdraw without shoulders having been broken.

### Notes

Four pairs of wrestlers. Top-left pair shows one figure wearing a crown (master) defending against a grip. Top-right shows two figures exchanging strikes/grips. Bottom-left shows a crowned figure (master) in a grappling exchange. Bottom-right shows a close grappling throw with a dagger visible on the ground between the figures. Text contains many uncertain readings due to script difficulty.

## Folio 12v
**Section:** 1st Master

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top-left:
¶ Per quelle che al çogho che denançi volisti vira
per presa la man Eguale lo braço e questa porta sta
ormada a piega. Che çi ven fiasta va torna de statura
e movre diçi che sera in oltra per mal fata venire.

Top-right:
¶ La longa de mane ve mito che per ben aprandere
e la possa te insegna de vero e per egali bene coleste
e quelle guardare e fiorire veras mal chiedere lo
ala re çia possibile plegare lo braço. val non fiaste
ni l'omo [?].

Bottom-left:
¶ La convençudo a fiaxe del parto che denanço. çi po
la soa ovra tenire la longa e lo fiasto overe [?] stare fala e
vra luçia possidele cia la covresa mal frandole. la finançia
q[ue] ovelle possa de la mal çogha.

Bottom-right:
¶ La presa del nostra ve convancaray. de fiore
e la povarinda. pria nostra cha e la lon[ga] ben fran-
ça sia me lonçando. pria overte cha la posalida d'homo
que otre possiçe çi q[ua]rthorono e lo oltra marauira.

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
For those who at the play that before wished to turn through a grip, the hand equal to the arm, and this gate stands adorned at the fold. For he who comes with a blow goes back to his stature, and moves -- says that he will go beyond to come badly made.

Top-right:
The long [guard] of the hand, I show you that to learn well, the power teaches you truly, and equally well -- those [things] to guard and to flourish. Truly, badly to ask at the king -- it is possible to fold the arm, [it] is not worth the blow nor the man.

Bottom-left:
The convention at the parting blow that [comes] before -- so his work holds the long [guard], and the blow, truly to stand, false and true, Lucia possesses it since the cover, badly deceitful. The finance that those could [do] of the bad play.

Bottom-right:
The grip of our [play] will convince you of the flower and the poor woman [?]. First our way and the long [guard], well French, may it be my launching. First, openly, that the position of a man that others possess at the quarter[s] and the other marvel.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[36] *You will not break my arm over your shoulder,
For with my counter I will throw you to the ground.*

I make this counter to you who in the previous play intended to break my arm over your shoulder. I will throw you to the ground to your death with great force and you will cause me no further injury.

[37] *Your dagger will quickly be taken from you,
When I twist it upwards close by your elbow.*

I am in a good position to take the dagger from your hand, and to do it I push the point upwards, close to your elbow. And you will lose it, and I will quickly strike you with it. I took the dagger in this way because I was not able to bend your arm.

[38] *My dagger will not be taken by your turning it,
And I will strike you with it without fail.*

I make the counter of the play that came before, so that you will not be able to take my dagger in that way. I will press my dagger into your hand, to make you let go, and with the cruel point I will strike you for your trouble.

[39] *I will have no problem making you fall to the ground,
But you will have a major problem trying to get up.*

In this way you will be driven into the ground, and you will not be able to make any defense or counter. And I will quickly make the dagger that you hold in your hand go far from you, because of my skillful knowledge of this art.

[In the Pisani Dossi, the Scholar steps inside of his opponent's right leg.]

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

I seize the dagger using a sudden violent whirling motion near the elbow;
Yet before [that], I strongly turn the lower arms.

It is not any work to me, laying you out fallen.
You will not be able to rise free[ly] without a large wound.

### Notes

Four pairs of wrestlers. Top-left shows a crowned figure (master) with another figure in a turning/throwing motion. Top-right shows three figures in a group scene with one wearing a crown (master). Bottom-left shows two figures in close grappling. Bottom-right shows two figures engaged in a wrestling exchange. This page is more faded than previous ones and the text is particularly difficult to decipher. Many readings are highly uncertain.

## Folio 13r
**Section:** 2nd Master

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top-left:
¶ Io son lo Re che son corona portano. Io fia lo abraçar
del filorire che m'insegna. lo quale per çoi grande magiste che
naçe e Prexe fiare che lo quanto sia sta[to] ognore ligare
e le parte abele che covire la longa e la coltre e m'insegna
dala sua mane e fiorire.

Top-right:
¶ Io salte che fia bona. conpita e fiar la marcatho che Ma-
gistro fiore possidele. del non fiase [?] insegna doy filorido
che son possidele covere. per che porta
lu ver chi portara covrenasa. que che porta longuença e covele
donchara entrarsi [?] lo fidare eloçare de pugna.

Bottom-left:
¶ La convençura del Mo che insegna benenançe un fiora
che un fioy m'aprercia [?] a çoe fiora longa che del posta
de ben ni lo chudire de la fiore açoitare. e lo fiorire de fi-
çato.

Bottom-right:
¶ Per questa posside che a venire fiore. a profitamento
refutano. tuto lo movre. çe che lo possa romperla la longa
e çoe in lo storno ve rapore. e la noi figete [?]
de quela e quelo oviçe lo sen ma fiorire feleno.

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
I am the King who wears the crown. I am the wrestling of the flourish that teaches me, which through such great mastery is born. And to seize and strike, so that the amount may be [in] every binding, and the parts that are able to cover the long [guard] and the cover, and teaches me from his hand and to flourish.

Top-right:
I leap, which is good, complete, and to strike the mark that Master Fiore possesses. Of the [signs], two flourished [masters] that possess the cover. For he who carries the true one who will carry the cover -- that one who carries length and hides -- therefore to enter, to trust, and to place in the fist fight.

Bottom-left:
The convention of the Master who teaches good fortune -- a flower that a son learns from me [?]. So the long flower, that of the position, to well hide from the flower, to listen. And the flourishing of the liver [core].

Bottom-right:
For this possession that is to come -- flower, for profit, refusing all movement. So that the power may break the long [guard], and so in the turning I bring you back. And we do not flee from that one and that authority, the sense, but to flourish with venom.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[40] *I do not want to fall to the ground with the previous play,
So with this grip I will take away all of your strength.*

What you plan to do cannot always be done. I am the counter of the scholar who came before, and this counter will make him look very foolish, because in this way I will make him let go my leg. And I will drive the dagger into his face to demonstrate that he is indeed a great fool.

[In the Pisani Dossi, the Master is missing his crown.]

[41] And I make cover with arms crossed,
And I can make all the previous remedies,
Nor will I fail to cover any of the backhand strikes,
For I can do them all, one by one.

I play with my arms crossed, and can make all the remedies that were previously shown. And if we were both armoured, you could not make a better cover. No other crowned [Dagger] Remedy Master makes a stronger cover than I, for I can play both to the right and to the left, and I can cross from both underneath and from above.

[42] You won’t be able to make the plays that came before, nor the plays from the backhand strikes [that follow],
Because with this counter, your cover will be completely lost.

I counter the [Dagger] Remedy Master who made the cross before me, so he will not be able to cause me any problems with his crossing. I will give a push to his elbow to make him turn, and then I will quickly strike him.

[43] Take the dagger, dislocate your arm, bind you,
Or throw you to the ground–I can do all of these things.

I believe that this very strong grapple is fatal to anyone, because I can break your arm, throw you to the ground, or take your dagger. I can also hold you bound in the upper bind. And as a result of these four things, you will be unable to get away.

### Notes

Four pairs of wrestlers. Top-left shows a close grappling/body lock with one figure wearing a crown (master). Top-right shows a single figure standing apart (possibly demonstrating a guard position), wearing a crown (master). Bottom-left shows two figures grappling in skirts/longer tunics, one with a crown (master). Bottom-right shows two figures in a wrestling clinch, one wearing a longer garment. This page appears to be in the abrazare (wrestling) section, approaching what may be a transition section. Text is particularly difficult to read on this folio.

## Folio 13v
**Section:** 2nd Master

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top-left:
¶ Per la convençura del terçe possidele que insegna. Çi-
çoghe dire exempio de quelle prexe nata gie sie quatro
çogar possa contençando. Che ben ve no covelle fiore n'a
la fiasta my vila. y la presa che e fiorire.

Top-right:
¶ Per un comungiare çogho fiore. de mane e lo conçepto. e la
quella insegna atre lo suo vela[?] presto. E lo possa le co-
de [?] fioridamen[?]to. e la mostra gie lo piese che la
torbire moçone. e lo bie çogho ligiano del corpo
çoito.

Bottom-left:
¶ Io per queste modo in lo corpo fiorire çirado. E per-
filorire avestre la storna. E la fiasta boy [?] co-
mitoria raforçando del non per fior inimiço. Conquesto
ça fiasta fia fiasta mostrare soa possitura.

Bottom-right:
¶ Io son vn vla ole finaçe lo sufistiençe e lo pore [?] del
longo. che alun fiore moneta [?] monacha de-
pu no fiore. che que ta conçeta e fiar lo por[?] conbattere.

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
Through the convention of the third possession that teaches -- [in] plays, to give example of those grips born, already four plays [one] can contend. For well you have no hidden flower, nor the blow, my town. And the grip that is to flourish.

Top-right:
Through a communal play of flowers, of the hand and the concept, and that [one] teaches others with his veil/speed. And the power, the tails [?] in a flourishing manner, and the showing already of the foot that the turbid motion [creates]. And the good play, light of body, quickly.

Bottom-left:
I, through this mode, in the body flourish, turned. And to flourish, to dress the turning. And the blow, boy, [the] commissary, reinforcing -- not for the enemy flower. With this, the blow is the blow [that] shows his position.

Bottom-right:
I am one [who goes] to the end, the sufficiency and the power of the long [guard]. That any flower, coin [?], restraining -- then no flower. That which you conceive and strike to fight.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[44] As for the four plays you mentioned, you won’t be able to do any of them to me,
And with this counter I will throw you to the ground.

I know the counter to the previous play. And with this grapple I will counter all four of the plays he said he could do before. And as soon as he sees me, I will throw him to the ground, for this grapple is strong and fierce.

[47] *Here I believe I will throw you to the ground.
First I will do this to you, and then I will do worse to you.*

Here begin the plays of the reverse strikes, through which countless men have lost their lives. And the plays of my scholars will follow, demonstrating the cover made with the right hand. This play depicted is easy to do, and in this way I will throw this man into the ground.

[In the Getty, this Scholar is the Master.]

[48] *You will go to the ground because of your lack of knowledge,
And in armor this is a particularly safe throw.*

With this method you will be driven into the ground. And I could not make a safer throw, being armoured. But even without armour, there is nothing you can do. And even if you were strong and powerful, I would still be able to do this to you.

[49] *This leads to a broken ruined arm,
As you will find out when I have you in this hold.*

You will go to the ground and your arm will be dislocated by the skill of my crowned Master. And there is not one counter that you can do to me, for I hold you like this…

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

I believe you, treacherous one, will now indeed touch the earth.
And itself would do worse to you, henceforth lying dead.

[In the Paris, this Scholar is the Master.]

You, incautious one, will touch the earth with [your] chest prostrated.
This armor-wearer will more safely impart the play.

Each is able to break the shoulder of the associate in wrestling, as I comprehend.
It will be pleasing to know whatever has been imparted.

### Notes

Four pairs of wrestlers. Top-left shows two figures grappling closely, both in tunics. Top-right shows a crowned figure (master) with raised arms facing another figure with arms also raised. Bottom-left shows two figures, one in a longer garment, in a wrestling exchange. Bottom-right shows a figure who has thrown his opponent to the ground (the opponent is on all fours). This page continues the abrazare (wrestling) section. Text is very difficult to read throughout -- many words are uncertain.

## Folio 14r
**Section:** 3rd Master

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top-left:
¶ Questa e bona prexe leçiere nui ça a stravagançe
lo çofigra. Che ge la longa al poste tore. Oga ligadure
ci me denege. Refigurare fiaço gia me derne.
eguiando al pe per fiore mal lo guardeçe. e qual fiore
la posta. a mia autondade.

Top-right:
¶ La longo tu predi. e tal volio che a rexone. E coltre ri-
tochare lo val possi leçarme. E me la ligadure da fermo
e la val florire. L'avtra che lo refixare del rompe [?]
re questa e covella refiare. che che e la fiasta mi ogi de
dissere. que grande piaxe a fiorire chi romana feblano.

Bottom-left:
¶ Questa e chiamade ligadure. de fiorire e ala
stronga mane fiorire [?] faxe lo contrario e lo
de pe sos la matere. che mi sai loghe pena
e che la lo fiore. ligadure me no gie fiase.
Che lo coverte me per braza in coverta. Eiguale
che li suole me la fiata mi ligadure.

Bottom-right:
¶ Questa e la contraria de le braçe. que quela che
longuamente e la storna [?] fiorire çoe possi
fflorire e de mio e braze e torno e fiçamato.
Eche la lo [?] ronperlo possa no quella vostra e quella
la mostra e cambature la possidele bellença e quasi
fiare che me per la fiore lo possiomendo [?]. Ela la cossa
fiorire. quella pero bey fare.

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
This is a good light grip [for] us already to the extravagance, the figure. For the long [guard] is at the post/position. Every binding denies me. To reconfigure the blow, already [I give you] my back, guiding at the foot for the flower, badly the guard -- and which flower the position, at my authority.

Top-right:
The long [guard] you take, and such I wish that with reason. And to cover, to touch again -- the worth, you can bind me. And my binding is firm, and the worth to flourish. The other one that the refixing of the breaking -- this is the cover to reblow. For the blow [strikes] me today to say: what great pleasure to flourish, that Roman weakness.

Bottom-left:
This is called a binding of flourishing, and to the strong hand flourishing, [one] does the contrary and [goes] below the matter. For you know me -- long pain -- and that the flower, the binding, does not make me [yield]. For the cover, me, through arms, in cover. Equal [to] that which soles me -- the blow, my binding.

Bottom-right:
This is the counter of the arms -- that one who lengthily and the turning flourishes -- so you can flourish, and of mine, and the arms, and I turn and am fixed. And that [one] can break it -- not that yours and that one shows and to fight -- the possession, beauty, and almost to strike -- that me through the flower, possessing. And the thing to flourish, that one, however, well to do.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[51] *This is another powerful dislocation
From which I will be able to kill you with your own dagger.*

This is a lock that has no counter and no defense. And in this way I can take your dagger, and it will be no trouble to bind or dislocate your arm. You will not be able to get away without my permission. And I can ruin your arm if I choose.

[52] *I take your dagger as I wish,
And now if I choose, I can bind you in the lower lock.*

You will lose your dagger by the way I hold you. And after taking your dagger I can bind you. and make you suffer in the lower lock, which is one of the key binds, and which I will use on you. And whoever is put into this lock cannot escape, because of the great pain and suffering they will be forced to endure.

[54] *This is called the strong lower lock,
And it is a bind with a great risk of death
But this lock, truth to tell,
If not properly applied can be escaped from.*

This is called the lower lock, also known as the “strong key”, and from this bind I can kill you, whether you are armoured or unarmoured, because from here I can strike you in all of your most vulnerable places. And no one can escape from this bind. And if you are put into it, as depicted in the drawing, there you will remain enduring much pain and suffering.

[55] *This is how to do the counter to the Master's cover to the reverse strike,
And with this bind I will make you drop to the ground on your knees.*

This is the counter to the Third Daga Remedy Master, who covers the reverse attack. I have made this bind against him. Whether he is armoured or unarmoured, this bind is strong and secure. And if I trap a man who is unarmoured in this way, I will ruin his hand and dislocate it. And the pain will be so great I will make him kneel at my feet. And should I wish to strike him, this I can also do.

[In the Getty, the Master's left foot is forward.]

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

He was able to dislocate the shoulder of any companion for himself,
And sentence him to death with the point of the dagger.

I now take hold of your dagger, nor can I be mistaken.
And if I want, I am able to bind you, who is [sic] being overthrown in the key.

The lower key is brought under the strong name;
It is the bind of death by means of excessive distinction.
If any enters into this, he will hardly prevail to escape this.

I, the efficient counter of the master, during this wrestling
Finish whomever by means of the reverse palm of the hand;
And you will sink down on bended knee by means of this taking.

### Notes

Four figures/pairs in wrestling. Top-left shows a single figure carrying/holding another figure over his shoulder (a throwing technique). Top-right shows two figures in a standing grappling exchange with one wearing a crown (master). Bottom-left shows two figures in a close clinch/binding position. Bottom-right shows two figures in a wrestling exchange, one appearing to be a woman or figure in longer dress. This is a locks and bindings (ligadure) section within abrazare. Text remains very challenging throughout.

## Folio 14v

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Çon pondi fiovassi di quela de mare. la [?] ligadure fra
coverta. Egi lo destro che devra de ben omi la fiorire.
la liga se la longa e Coverte gli lege repentade [?].

La longe tu possi tore comunçare ni la persona. Anchora la
lo mostra [?] el la fiasta ne la toa persona.
manfiare la tua longa. el mal conversa abrigadure [?]
ma possa overte lo sufiteçe.

per fiore longo. che per mostrare mi lo fiore lon[ga] de covrete
Io compere [?] e rompare [?] e quela de la fiorire

del son possa comvendere.

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
When the authority makes two consuls of this play's grip, with weights flowering from that of the sea [?], the binding among covers. And the right one that must [do] well to men -- the flourishing. The binding, if the long [guard] and covers are bound repentantly.

Top-right:
I, open, am a good binding. Prudence of the good one of the flower. The long [guard] you can take, to begin at the person. Also it shows the blow at your person. To handle your long [guard], and badly the conversation of the binding. But [the] power, openly, the sufficiency.

Bottom-left:
This is a good strike. Prudence binding, and that through the long flower, that to show me the long flower of covering. I buy [?] and break, and that of the flourishing. But with the above, to strike their covering -- the binding of your arms. The hand will be positioned to stand, or of my hands flourishing, pulled.

Bottom-right:
When the ready one [seizes] mine, tearing it apart -- the right [arm], blowing, above the power of one arm -- to advance [against] my arms. My small [grip] seizes the cover position. The blanket [cover] of him -- I can again convene. Of the [sound] power to comprehend.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[56] I am a Master who covers with both hands,
And I can hurt you from above or below.
If I give a turn to your shoulder without releasing your arm,
Then in this way I and my students will put you in great pain.

I am the Fourth [Dagger Remedy] Master, and I play from this grip. From covers like this my students will hurt many. And if I turn to the right without releasing your arm, I will take your dagger and put you in great pain.

[58] I am well positioned to force you to the ground,
And if you don't end up with a broken head, you can count yourself lucky.

This is an upper bind that locks you up very well. I will take your dagger from you and throw you to the ground. And I can also dislocate your arm. If however you grip your right hand with your left hand, then you can counter me and make me let go of you.

[These two illustrations may show the beginning and end of the technique.]

[59] This is another lock that will throw you to the ground,
And against such a hold no one is safe.

This is another upper bind that is very powerful. And with this I am certain to throw you to the ground. And if I wish I can dislocate your arm. To counter me, you grip your right hand with your left hand. Then your grip will be strong and mine will be weak.

[In the Getty and Paris, the Scholar's left foot is forward.]

[61] I can also throw you to the ground like this,
And once you are on the ground it will go badly for you.

After I made the cover of my Master, I put my left hand under your right elbow. And my right hand quickly gripped you under your knee in such a way that I could throw you to the ground, and there was no counter that you could do to me.

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

Using both hands, &lt;I&gt; the master now take hold of the associate.
From above and beneath, I am able to injure you with a weapon.

I am certainly prepared in order to cast you down into the earth.
And I will give many evils to your head, if it remains because of courage.Demittere mentem is recorded (by Bantam dictionary) as an idiom meaning “to lose heart”. Possibly mente sedebit is referencing this, in a pun (e.g., demittere in the sense of depose, and sedeo in the sense of hold court).

[In the Paris, the Scholar's left foot is forward, but inside (in front) of his opponent's leg.]

This movement is another to strike down the associate to the earth.
Nevertheless, it is not safe because he attempts a similar playing.

Certainly in this way I can send you a second time
To the ground. Hereafter, &lt;I&gt; myself will approve worse things to you.

### Notes

Four pairs of wrestlers. Top-left shows a crowned figure (master) applying what appears to be an arm lock or throw on another figure who is wearing a cloak/cape. Top-right shows two figures in a standing exchange. Bottom-left shows two figures in close grappling with arm bindings. Bottom-right shows two figures wrestling on the ground or in a low position. This continues the ligadure (binding/locking) section. Text throughout is very faded and uncertain.

## Folio 15r
**Section:** 5th Master

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top-left:
¶ La nostra fioradura non contrasta e la rompetura e lo
pislanda mane che ella ovra. mi aldene. e lo per compiteza
me sol mare gradi la fioa mane fiorisade. che la per conplo [?]
lo la daga prima lo nova fiore[?]a e la ligadure lo braze
longe. Che son gela del nostro. No ligadura [?]. al nostra
fianda que fiosimio covride di lo [?] fiorindole.

Top-right:
¶ Io nostra modo de prexo e che me non per fialladura [?]
a lo fiasta grande perdide e ovra fioridade. che bon fiore
in la tu possi iguemente. che no covelle çoghe ligare ali [?]
la vale prexo monande. la fiasta sono e lo fiallada. cha son che
e la fiamentada. e fia bon paresse. So çoghe prexe per lo son elo fio
a marchare paesse.

Bottom-left:
¶ Oia nostra ovelta in fiorire e fioriquare e de val lon[ga] e l'o-
contra conviture covere fiorire. monde d'altre sopra destro. lo pisto
morssi quando lo fioj. e questa [?] fiogliere metale la fiorire

E la la botta covira di per posito movre. la pislanda mostra de lo fiore so
la ligadure na cogramente. lo fistore. çoe io che devene fiorire
çoie possa lo sevre chi te comolisse che fiasta campo morçe [?]
de chiço çolpe.

¶ Bena questo braze possi covrirana. mi no mi le devira che

fiande converse fion la figurada coverta e la possa fiorire. lo fia ce-
me çoe contra de la fiçie lo segte de mostra fiorimade. la contraria fiorida
e lo la devene do lo pugelo fierto de la guambiçone la la fiorida fiorase
e la ligatura na cogramente. no fiorire. Fiorire non san manifistare [?].

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
Our flourishing does not resist, and the breaking and the wrestling hand that she works, my hearing. And through completeness, my sole sea of degrees -- his hand flourished, that through completion, the dagger first [takes] the new flower and the binding of the long arm. For they are frost of ours. No binding. To our blow that covers in a flowering manner.

Top-right:
Our way of grip, and that not through failing -- at the blow, great loss and flowering work. That good flower in your possession equally -- that no hidden play to bind to the worthy grip, remaining. The blow they are, and the failing, for they are the [terror], and it is a good appearance. The plays of grips through the sound and the son, to march the land.

Bottom-left:
Oh, our return in flourishing and flowering, and of the long worth and the counter-convention, to cover and flourish. Worlds of others above, right. The pestle bites when the son, and this [one] to seize metal to flourish -- you [who] would open with the ready one, the hidden [things] of the failing, two through, and remaining the long [guard]. And the blow covers through position to move. The wrestling shows the flower above. The binding cogently, the maker, that is, I who become to flourish. The play can [be] the service that to you fulfills -- that blow of the field, death [?] of small blows.

Bottom-right:
Well, this arm you can cover -- but not my [arm] that devours. That the binding of the flourishing of the lord [who] is in the power of the trusting, conversely, the figure of the cover and the power to flourish. It is like the counter of the striking -- the sign of showing flowering. The counter of the flower, and it becomes of the fist struck through the gambeson -- the flower flourishes and the binding cogently. Not to flourish. To flourish they do not know how to manifest.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[73] *You will find out that over my right shoulder
I will not fail to break your arm.*

This player had me grabbed by the collar, but before he could strike me with his dagger I quickly seized his left hand with my hands and pulled his arm over my shoulder so as to dislocate it, and then I completely dislocated it. But this play is safer to do in armor than unarmored.

[74] *By the way I seize you and hold you,
I will force you to the ground shoulders first.*

In this way I will hurl you to the ground without fail. And I will surely take your dagger. And if you are armored that may help you, since I will be aiming to take your life with your own dagger. But even if we are armoured, this art will not fail me. And if you are unarmored and very quick, other plays can be made besides this one.

[In the Getty and Paris, the Scholar's right foot is inside (in front) of his opponent's left leg.]

[75] *To take your dagger I make a cover like this,
And then with other plays I will make you suffer.*

This cover is very good in armor or without armor. And against any strong man such a cover is good for covering an attack from below as well as from above. And from this play you can enter into a middle bind as shown in the third play of the First Dagger Remedy Master. And if the cover is made in response to an attack from below, the student will put the player into a lower lock also known as “the strong key”, as shown in the sixth play [38] of the Third [Dagger] Remedy Master who plays to the reverse hand attack.

[76] *If I can turn this arm of yours,
Then I will force you into the lower lock.*

If I can turn this arm I will be certain to put you into the lower lock also known as “the strong key”. I will however be able to do this more safely if I am armored. I could also do something else against you: if I grip your left hand firmly and seize you under your left knee with my right hand, then I will not lack the strength to put you to the ground.

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

I will not have been cheated of breaking the left shoulder;
I am holding that which is loaded down on the right using the leg during wrestling.

I hold you using this form, and I will catch the lamenting one;
Now, with the leg, you will be strewn as deep into the earth as possible.

Now I make this cover, for which reason I would be able to take away the dagger,
Not to mention [that] I can strike you using many plays.

If I can now twist your shoulder while fighting,
I will readily cause [that] you will be overwhelmed in the lower key.

### Notes

Four pairs of wrestlers. Top-left shows two figures in close grappling with one applying what appears to be a headlock or neck control. Top-right shows two figures standing apart in guard positions. Bottom-left shows two figures in an intricate grappling exchange with longer text (most text on the page). Bottom-right shows two figures wrestling with one applying a hold. This page has the densest text of the set so far. The script is extremely difficult and many readings are speculative. The text block at bottom-left is notably longer than most captions on other pages.

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
In the long arms, completed in counter-flourishing, they carry. I will tell you of the coat, at the striking, that I give you two blows of reason -- that to strike, to change, that through the long hours I can. And that the contrary of which to complete -- that the wrestling of the arms, already if one blow the long [guard], and truly the long [guard] is not my convention.

Top-right:
Our grip on me -- the blow that [does] me wrong. Direct me, no grip to flower. And the play that my striking, that the play may be a flower of exalted birth, flowering Lacedaemon [Sparta]. The altar [?] drew the gallery, and that one [treats] me, boy, other wrestling.

Bottom-left:
This player who teaches me this -- and to play, through which to flourish, the long [guard] goes to the striking, before his grips. The long [guard] to the striking, right [one] to have the prisoner. Dagger, that the arms, low flourishing. And the play -- I can make of him the arm's blow flower. And the prison -- in my possession, the cover struck, from him.

Bottom-right:
For that this flourish, the wrestling, my true play -- the arms of striking, the great long [guard], and the blow completing. And it is the body's grips to strike -- the showing of wrestling cover, my [desire], that the one and the me already at the cover of the play, from there, of the long [guard].

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[77] *Whether you try to strike at me from above or below,
You will lose your dagger from this crossing.*

With arms crossed I await you without fear. And I don't care whether you come at me from above or below, because however you come at me, you will be bound. You will be locked either in the middle lock or the lower lock. And if I wished to make the plays of the Fourth Dagger Remedy Master, I would cause you great harm with these plays. And I will have no difficulty in taking your dagger.

[In the Getty, the Scholar's left foot is forward.]

[78] *By holding your arm with my two hands,
I will take away the dagger from you, as you deserve.*

This grip is sufficient to prevent you being able to touch me with your dagger. And from here I can do the play that comes after me. And I could also certainly do other plays to you. I disregard the other plays for now, however, because this one is good for me and very fast.

[79] *The student who came before me did not make this play,
So I show how to take away the dagger in his place.*

This is the play referred to by the student who came before me, and I take away this dagger as he indicated. And to disarm him I push his dagger downwards and to the right as written above. And then by making a turn with his dagger I will thrust the point into his chest without fail.

[In the Getty and Paris, the Scholar's left foot is forward and his opponent's right foot is forward.]

[80] So that this student cannot dislocate my arm, I pull it towards me and bend it. And the farther I pull it towards me and bend it, the better, because in this way I make the counter to the Remedy Master of the close play of the dagger.

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

Now because I am holding you using both hands during wrestling,
I certainly would take hold of [your] dagger just as if you had truly deserved.

Now I teach taking the dagger away while wrestling the associate;
This first student does not know how to play.

### Notes

Four pairs of wrestlers. Top-left shows two figures standing apart, one with arms extended (guard position). Top-right shows two figures in a grappling exchange with one figure gripping the other's arm. Bottom-left shows two figures in a close wrestling clinch, one wearing a crown (master), with what appears to be a low throw in progress. Bottom-right shows two figures with one figure appearing to seize the other, one crowned (master). Text remains extremely challenging to decipher. Many words are uncertain throughout.

## Folio 16r
**Section:** 6th Master

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top-left:
¶ Io son lo morastello che fior preste che questo contaçesano
e fiaste in fia covella prexe del mal acresçono e soe per-
dire presto e fiaste pigliar[e] l'ovreto che posta lo fioro[?]
Ei per fiaste lo çoghe covele che fio le quela
covenada in no covelle fiore. e per fiaste lo sope covello che fi-
çe lo fiaste.

pislanda so fia quela grande prexe. fiorire e che fia per le ma-
ne in la prexe. lo si al çogho rigade de la persona e lo de-
vero [?] me la lo longo. Avchora Inelle çolpe e lo de mano de
la longa. Parchara la braça ne possi fiorire. possa
mal longa covriminade [?].

çe fiaste monacho prexe la re lo fiorire mane. che questa longa

¶ Doy mi che fia per preste driçe le braze del fiçiatura sopra

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
I am the mortar [fighter] who makes ready flowers. That this fellow countryman and the blows in the cover, the grips of the evil increase, and his loss, quickly. And the blow to seize the work -- that the position of the flower. And through the blow, the plays of cover that are -- that convention in no cover-flower. And through the blow, the above cover that makes the blow.

Top-right:
Likewise, the convention of the place at the extravagance that teaches us the wrestling -- that great grip, to flourish, and that it is through the hands in the grip. So at the play, to rule the person, and truly my long [guard]. Also in the blows and from the hand of the long [guard]. To prepare the arm -- you cannot flourish. [If the] power [is] badly long-covered.

Bottom-left:
These two covers, closed, covering the counter of the play. Covered [one] to manage. And [I am] the flourishing of the ready blows' grips. And the striking makes the monk [restrained] grip -- the king, the flourishing hand. That this long [guard] and hand and the cover -- and I the [?]. Give to the hands, flowers, wings, in the pain. Strike -- I am not covered, nor through fire flourishing.

Bottom-right:
Two [with] me that through ready [grips], direct the arms of the striking above. Of the well, through reason, the turning of him. And that was [to be] Flanders, and this already was the end through the exalted gathering. Jovially, already the monarch was [in] the plays, already with deliberation, no binding. And the arm, ready for covering, the hidden blow. Joyful covering of the fire, the Florence, and the pressing. To cover in a place, key of the striking.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[81] *There is no man who knows more about dagger versus dagger than I.
Whether in armor or without, I will gravely injure you,
And when fighting at the barrier I truly love
To vanquish everyone with these close plays.*

I am the Sixth [Dagger Remedy] Master and I tell you that this cover is good either in armor or without armor. And with this cover I can cover attacks from all directions and enter into all of the holds and binds, and strike to finish, as the students who follow me will show. And each of my students will make this cover, and then they will make the plays shown after, as they are qualified to do.

[The Getty is ambiguous about whether the Master's dagger position matches the Pisani Dossi or the Paris.]

[82] I made the cover of the Sixth [Dagger Remedy] Master who preceded me. And as soon as I have made this grip I will be able to strike you. And because I position my left hand in this way, I will not fail to take away your dagger. I can also put you in the middle bind, which is the third play [3] of the First Dagger Remedy Master. I could also make other plays against you, without abandoning my dagger.

[83] *From the cover of my Master which is so perfect,
I will strike you in the chest with a half turn of my dagger.*

I have made this half turn from the cover of my Sixth Master and I have quickly positioned myself to strike you. And even if you were armored I would care little, for in that case I would thrust this dagger in your face. However, as you can see, in this case I have thrust it into your chest because you are not armored and you do not know the close range game.

[84] *With my Master’s cover and with a half turn to the outside,
I can still strike or bind you or take away your dagger.*

I have not abandoned the cover of my Sixth [Dagger Remedy] Master. I turn my left arm over your right. And moving my right foot at the same time as my left arm I turn myself to the outside. You are now partly bound, and you will have to admit that you will quickly lose your dagger. And I make this play so quickly that I have no concern or fear of your counter.

[In the Paris, the Scholar wears a crown, and both he and his opponent have their right feet forward.]

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

I do not recognize the man with whom I can’t play.
If we both lead while turning dagger in dagger,
Either I would be armed [with both], or by chance we would be without weapons,
And that movement is pleasing, provided that it would be a close play.

### Notes

Four figures/pairs in wrestling. Top-left shows a single figure holding/carrying another figure (a completed throw or carry technique). Top-right shows two figures in a standing grappling exchange with one wearing a crown (master). Bottom-left shows two figures in a binding/clinch position. Bottom-right shows two figures wrestling with one applying a technique on the other. Text is extremely faded and difficult to read throughout this folio. The handwriting on this page appears somewhat different or more rushed than earlier folios. Many readings are highly speculative.

## Folio 16v
**Section:** 6th Master

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top-left:
¶ Io son la contraria del mio çoestrissimo e lo fiasta coverto.
posta e demandar e lo fistoricando si fia possi fiorire. El moço
de lo possi mostrare e lo laço çoghe oprençia del fiore podia [?]
nel questa mane fia stranalda del longa.

la longa. e che eli no la vira doy. La sua Çistema in la

çole posta. overe un locho fia dade del la locho çoimovrita [?].

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
I am the counter of my most illustrious [play] and the covered blow. Position and demand, and the historicizing [narrating], so you can flourish. The boy of my showing and the lace play -- the prudence of the flower could [be] in this hand, strange to the long [guard].

Top-right:
By dismantling the counter of the [opponent], mixed with the long [guard]. And that they do not turn it [into] two. His system, in the mode, and the arms, seizing [them], the power strikes. And the [terrain] not legitimized. The flourishing, weeping [?].

Bottom-left:
Through the convention of the day, turning -- I strike. The grip covers me. The authority of the flower, my first content, and the play that through flourishing -- that is, the authority to seize -- my flowering flourish. Badly with flourishing, the blow to be trusted, the cover. And they cover -- the show and the counter of the grip of the play to flourish.

Bottom-right:
Two [with] me that [makes] a position, whence the convention of the striking above, and [they] strike -- that is, to reason with him. And that he cannot trust, and this blow that is the flower of the covered gathering -- that is, covering. Already the showing strikes, and he will have my flowering. Not to trust, and the [field], already the frost, and the [way], and the seizing of them. The play's position, or in a place, is given of the place [?].

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[85] *From the cover my Master made
With this grip and cover I will give you grief.*

Having made the cover of my Master, I made this grip. And I can strike you whether you are armored or unarmored. And I can also put you into the lower lock of the first scholar of the Fourth Dagger Remedy Master.

[86] Without abandoning the cover of the Sixth [Dagger Remedy] Master, I make this turn [with my dagger]. Your right hand will lose the dagger, and seeing that you have been reversed, my dagger will quickly strike you, and your dagger will be lost to you. Also I can make a turn with my left arm and make you suffer in the lower lock.

[87] *If you and I are both armored,
I will thrust the knife into your hand, as you can see.*

Although I am placed after the counter-remedy to the Sixth Master, I should logically be placed before him, because I am a student of the Sixth [Dagger Remedy] Master and my play belongs to him. And this play makes more sense in armor than unarmored, because if he is armored I can strike him in the hand where he cannot fully protect himself; whereas if he is unarmored, I would aim to strike him in the face or in the chest, or in some other vulnerable place.

[88] *With my left hand I will turn you and expose you
And with this counter, I will be able to strike you hard.*

I make the counter-remedy of the Sixth King [Dagger Remedy Master], turning your body with an elbow push, and in this way I can strike you, because with this elbow push that I quickly do, I will be able to defend against many close plays. And this is a particularly good counter-remedy to the all of the holds of the close-range game.

### Notes

Four pairs of figures in combat. Top-left shows two figures, one wearing a crown (master), in a standing grappling exchange with one figure appearing to apply a wrist or arm technique. Top-right shows two figures engaged in close grappling with the right figure applying what appears to be an arm lock. Bottom-left shows two figures in a striking exchange -- this appears to be a transition toward dagger combat as the figures' postures suggest weapon use. Bottom-right shows two figures in a low wrestling/grappling exchange. This page appears to conclude or be near the end of the abrazare (wrestling) section. Text is extremely difficult throughout with many uncertain readings.

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
The Remedy of Wrestling was that I can play with the right arms, and more quickly than any other player against me, and I am Master of them. Through the lower bind that I will make, it will be very good. Through the master bind of the arm that makes the greatness of the play die. We are three guard players by such voice. The first are powerful guards of my Flower of Arms: of my sword and the Dagger in its sheath. And this orders us to play what can do other: who will make arms, will guard against the shield.

Top-right:
This is the counter of the Remedy of Wrestling, for I am the Scholar. And the departure that the Scholar makes. The first says that also I am a counter to you, each one takes the arm from the back. And Segura and Falda Terreno can put him down. The first, to reduce things into the Beast with the Scholar.

Bottom-left:
