Otherwise I can play the counter of the hand under the play, to kill the gate of his guard.

Bottom-right:
We are the counter of the long remedy, oh my skill, of the play and that the hands I can counter, who at the play escapes. The first and counter of I who am Arms and force: and escaping from that which other moves: that the arm and with the Mouth.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

| 
I am the Seventh [Dagger Remedy] Master and I play with arms crossed. And this cover is better made when armored than unarmored. The plays that I can do from this cover are the plays that came before me, especially the middle bind which is the third play of the first Dagger Remedy Master. Also I can turn you by pushing your right elbow with my left hand. And I can strike you quickly in the head or in the shoulder. And this cover is better for binding than any other cover, and is a very strong cover to make against the dagger.

| 
This is the counter remedy to the plays of the Seventh [Dagger Remedy] Master who came before me. With the push that I make to his right elbow, let me tell you that this counter-remedy is good against all close range plays of the dagger, the poleaxe, and the sword, whether in armor or unarmored. And once I have pushed his elbow I should quickly strike him in the shoulder.

[In the Getty, the Master's right foot is forward.]

[92]

 

I am the Eighth [Dagger Remedy] Master and I cross with my dagger. And this cover is good both armored or unarmored. And some of my plays are shown before me, and some are shown after me…

[94] I am the counter-remedy to the Eighth [Dagger Remedy] Master that preceded me, and to all of his students…

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

In this way, I carry my dagger while fighting during the cross[ing]. Any defense
Which the dagger offers does not oppose itself in the play,
But I will be strong to lay waste in playing using many moves.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[97] This is a guard that is a strong cover in armor or unarmored. It is a good cover because from it you can quickly put your opponent into a lower lock or “strong key.” This is what is depicted by the sixth play [54] of the Third [Dagger Remedy] Master who defends against the reverse hand strike and who uses his left arm to bind the Zugadore’s right arm.

[98] This cover that I make like this with arms crossed is good in armor or unarmored. And my play puts the Zugadore into the lower lock, which is also called the “strong key,” which the scholar who preceded me told you about, namely the sixth play [54] of the Third Master who defends with his right hand against the reverse hand strike. And this play is made similarly to the play that immediately preceded me, but is begun in a slightly different way.

And our counter–remedy again is the elbow push.

[The Master in the right image is missing both garter and crown.]

[100] *From this grip that I have I can do many plays.
Take away the dagger, break, strike or bind.
And the quickest is to take the dagger from your hand,
so as to avoid any risk of harm from the player.*

I am the Ninth King [and Dagger Remedy Master] and I no longer have a dagger. And this grip that I make from the low attack is similar to the grip made by the Fourth King [and Dagger Remedy Master], only this one is made against the low attack instead of the high attack, and my plays are not the same as his. This grip is good whether in armor or unarmored, and from it you can make many good strong plays, as shown below. Whether in armor or unarmored there is no doubt of their effectiveness.

[101] *If I rotate the dagger close to your elbow,
Your dagger will be mine for certain.*

I have followed on from the presa of the Ninth [Dagger Remedy] Master. Taking my right hand from the grip, I seize your dagger as shown and I rotate it upwards close to your elbow. And I will then thrust the point into your face for certain, or I will deal with you as the next student will demonstrate.

### Notes

- Four pairs of wrestling (abrazare) figures continuing the wrestling section
- The crowned figure (Master) appears in the top-left pair
- Text is quite faded and difficult to read in several places
- Many abbreviations and ligatures make precise reading challenging
- The figures show various grappling techniques and counters

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[102] *The first student of this Master
Takes away the dagger and makes this play.*

I complete the play of the student who came before me, and from his grip this is how he should finish his play. Other students will make different plays from his grip. Watch those who follow, and you will see their techniques.

[103] *I can dislocate your arm like this,
And I can also bind you in the lower bind.*

My Master's grip has already been demonstrated. Here my right hand leaves his grip. And if I grip you under your elbow, I can dislocate your arm. And also from this grip I can put you into a bind, namely the “strong key” [lower bind], which is one the third King and [Dagger Remedy] Master showed in his plays In his sixth play [38] he shows you how this one is done.

[In the Getty, the Scholar's right foot is forward.]

[104] *If I can give your arm a half turn,
You will quickly find yourself in the lower bind.*

I have arrived at this position from the grip of my Master [Ninth Dagger Remedy Master], and I do not remain in this grip but move into the lower bind, also known as the “strong key.” This I can do without difficulty, and I can then easily take your dagger.

[In the Getty, the Scholar's right foot is forward.]

[105] *Without releasing my grip I enter underneath your arm,
And from behind your shoulder I will hurt you grievously.*

I have not abandoned the grip of my Master [the Ninth Dagger Remedy Master], but I have quickly entered under his right arm, to dislocate it with this grip. I can do this whether he is wearing armor or not, and once I have him held from behind and in my power, I will show him no mercy as I hurt him.

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

The student will perhaps be able to make this play of that master [of yours],
And would have snatched the powerful dagger away.

[In the Paris, the Scholar wears a Master's crown.]

I can truly dislocate your shoulder in this same way;
Furthermore, I can lead to using the lower key.

[In the Paris, the Scholar wears a crown.]

I prepare to take away your life using the 
Lower bind, if by chance I can twist your shoulder.

[In the Paris, the Scholar wears a crown.]

Behold! I crossed beneath the shoulder during play,
And furthermore, I left behind the taking. But I will burden the back.

### Notes

- Page numbered "20" in upper right corner (old foliation)
- Four pairs of wrestling figures, continuing the abrazare section
- Top-left pair shows a close grappling technique with one figure behind the other
- Top-right shows a lifting/throwing technique
- Bottom pairs show standing grappling exchanges
- Text heavily abbreviated and difficult to decipher in many places
- Several readings are highly uncertain due to fading and scribal hand

## Folio 18v
**Section:** 9th Master

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top-left:
¶ La presta del mio remidio del sbandezamento /
noi foy chi nardo [?] zugadore sozo allo: che noi la forza /
la [?] Scolaro presta in foa mane lo la mia mane manugle [?] /
lo soma de foy grande. ¶ E lo morire la foy soma destrier /
scolaro della che lo foy de poi de posste [?] Soma golpara /
lo lo poste della La mane suma /
Quello soma che la foy buoy [?] Somo. La mano alcuna /
della buoy [?] golpari e per la lo corpo e remidio e /
forza e possa [?] del nostro andare: e a dosegla la buona un /
toto poy morire forza.

Top-right:
¶ Questo Scolaro che ohe mio Scolaro: e come lo presa /
e lo foy del e Scolaro la Soma: de mandritore [?] e le /
lo brazo. ¶ Vedemo morire che noi possa lolo botalori [?] goze.

per se dare soma: e la buona e lo foy soy /

e forza la scolaro.

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
The quickness of my remedy of the banishment, we the play that Nardo, player filthy to that: that we the force, the Scholar quick in the hand the my hand gauntlet, the sum of the great play. And to die the play sum destrier, scholar of that the play of then of the guard. The sum strike, the guards of the hand sum. That sum that the play good Sum. The hand some of the good strikes and through the body and remedy and force and power of our going: and to pursue the good one, all then to die force.

Top-right:
This Scholar who oh my Scholar: and as the grip and the play of the Scholar the Sum: of right-hand strikes and the arm. Let us see to die that we can the combatants' joys.

Center-right:
The cover of the sum and this that when the gander and grips the left hand: and the good that is for giving sum and through the sum and his timing and I can quickly and harangue sum with the sum and the play and force the Scholar.

Bottom-left:
Of my master I can play his counter and Scholar and my sum destroyed. And the mastery knot and my good: And the play sum the taker, the master. And if the good way of the play sum taker. Like this play to me and sum and the little one.

Bottom-right:
And this simple play of this Scholar that my baptism. That Wrestling the play good sortie and all his good. Of Use of the play dagger blow of the that restraint.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[106] *Although this play is not often employed,
It can be done well, if you practice it.*

I did not abandon the grip of my Master [the Ninth Dagger Remedy Master] and the Zugadore saw that he could not break my grip on his arm. And as he pressed downwards towards the ground with his dagger, I quickly reached through his legs from behind and grabbed his right hand with my left hand. And once I had a good grip on his hand, I passed behind him. And as you can see in the picture, he cannot dismount his own arm without falling. And I can now also do the play that follows me. If I let go of the dagger with my right hand, and I grab his foot I will send him crashing to the ground, and I cannot fail to take his dagger.

[107] The student who preceded me performed the first part of this play, and I make the finish by driving him into the ground, as has already been explained. Although this play is not commonly performed in the art, I wish to show you that I have a complete knowledge of it.

[108] I made the cover of my Master [the Ninth Dagger Remedy Master] and then quickly I gripped him in this way with my left hand. And then I drew my dagger and thrust it into his chest. And if I do not have time to draw my dagger, I will make the play that follows me.

[109] With this play I complete the play of the student who preceded me, who left his [sheathed] dagger where it was and instead decided to take your live dagger. I have already explained how this play is performed.

[110] The Counter-remedy to this Ninth [Dagger Remedy] Master's play is as follows: when the Zugadore with his left hand has seized your right hand that has the dagger, then you should quickly seize your dagger near the point and strongly draw or pull it back towards you so that he has to let go of it, or alternately press the dagger point into his elbow to make him think twice.

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

It is granted that this play could scarcely be learned by this art,
Yet this one honestly succeeds by means of the practiced man.

### Notes

- Page has five text blocks with four pairs of wrestling figures plus an additional bottom pair
- Top-left shows a dramatic throw or takedown with one figure being lifted
- The crowned Master figure appears in multiple positions
- Text continues the abrazare (wrestling) section
- Very challenging paleography throughout; many readings uncertain
- Some text blocks appear to be partially repeated or related counters

## Folio 19r
**Section:** Dagger vs. Sword

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top-left:
¶ Lo mio remidio Spada e daga e zugatore: e la /
segureza ogunimanera e chi lo foy Scolaro. ¶ E mogiste [?] ispada /
denista guardara. ¶ Alla guardara de resistente morira /
¶ Zugare buoga e morta che lo braza [?] non foy /
guardadore. ¶ Lo foy prioro sono [?] redurra [?] e la posta solamente /
¶ A miro e mia redurra la guardare la /
non mire foy le guardare de dui portadori [?].

Top-right:
¶ E nui soma cerchi: la primero chi lo ¶ E la choglia /
italano [?] foy la le scolaro merito lo la possa: ¶ Chi dare /
conrisa Spada: Pretendo michi soma pertanto e lo le Doy /
preiode e la posta fizurare e scolaro e de le le con /
non pertano.

Bottom-left:
¶ Io so lo zugadore chi mio denastro accosto portado soma /
noi destriza. ¶ Che ello moriso la chogadore scolaro al italano /
porta e a questo mia portia e [?] lo batagliadore la [?] soma chi /
mortiero: del mio lo magistero e mio lo lo [?] conrisa la [?] /
morire che fio de la buoy [?] Spada.

Bottom-right:
¶ Io allo magistro che foy soma [?] de la buogo [?] di lo bruogo foy /
mortelo le [?] Scolaro e gli coltra: ¶ Che presta moriti [?] /
portare e gostario e chi de foy /
posta e de lo [?]. ¶ A questo che soma [?] poy la soma [?] /
remezo [?] alo soma [?] posta buona mi de portare [?] /
moriri alle lo soma [?] zuego broca [?] ogunimaniera [?] morira /
le [?] la la chiave foy portare al mio soma [?].

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
My remedy is Sword and dagger and player: and the security in every manner and who the play Scholar. And master sword, straight guard. At the guard of resistance will die. Play well and dead that the arm not the play guardian. The play prior are reduced and the guard only. To my aim and my reduction the guard, not my play the guards of two carriers.

Top-right:
And we are circles: the first who the, and the gathering Italian was the scholar merit the power: Who gives counter Sword: Pretending to me sum therefore and the two periods and the guard to figure and scholar and of the with not therefore.

Bottom-left:
I am the player who my sinister close carrying sum we skill. That he died the player scholar at the Italian carries and at this my carrying and the combatant the sum who mortar: of my the mastery and my the counter the death that was of the good Sword.

Bottom-right:
I to the master who the play sum of the place of the arm play, kill the Scholar and the others: That quick dead carrying and hosting and who of the play, guard and of the. At this that sum then the sum remedy to the sum guard good to me of carrying, die to the sum play thrust every manner die, the the key play to carry to my sum.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[1] *I know how to cover cuts and thrusts with my dagger.
Come one by one, that this play will not fail.
And my Scholar will show the proof:
Doing it according to what you find depicted.*

Here begin sword against dagger plays, and you will have a significant advantage if you know how to do these. The Master waits in a guard named Boar’s Tusk, a guard that will protect me from both cuts and thrusts. As I beat back my opponent’s sword, I pass backwards with my right foot, for I know the Narrow Play so well it cannot fail me. Attack me one by one as you wish. None of you will escape as I destroy each of you with this turn of my dagger.

[2] *The proof is found depicted here:
You see that I can strike you without difficulty.*

I have made the cover against the thrust that my Master showed you, and now I quickly strike my opponent in the face or the chest. With dagger versus sword you should always aim to close with your opponent. Here, since I am at close range I can strike you effectively, and like it or not, you will have to endure it.

[In the Getty, the Scholar's left foot is outside of (behind) his opponent's.]

[3] *The sword has won against the dagger here,
Because I have turned you and pushed you.*

If the attacker in the previous picture had known how to defend himself, he would have reached across with his left hand and seized the opponent behind his left elbow, turning him in the manner shown here. Then he would have no need of a counter to the remedy of the dagger Master.

[4] *If someone would attack me with a sword to my head,
I would make this cover with a quick catch;
I would turn him with the left hand
And then I would strike with a dagger in his back.*

If the dagger Master is attacked with a downward strike to the head, he passes forward immediately making the cover shown, turns his opponent by pushing his elbow, and then strikes him immediately. He can also bind the opponent’s sword with his arm, as shown in the fourth play of the sword in one hand. You will also find this Middle Bind shown in the third play of the dagger, which is made a hands-breadth from the face.

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

Here the sword and the dagger begin to play. The advantage is great to he who knows how to do it. The Master awaits in this guard with the dagger, and the guard is called Boar's Tusk. Come cuts or thrusts, I know how to guard myself from these: I will withdraw my right foot as I deflect. I understand the narrow play so well that I cannot possibly fail. Come one by one whoever wants to work against me, and if you don't flee from me, I will waste you in one turning.

[In the Morgan, the Master is missing his crown.]

This is made plain in the picture, constructed with great care, having been pointed out by a witness.
And henceforth you will see how I am able to subdue utterly with the dagger.

My Master makes this cover against the thrust and immediately strikes to the face or to the chest. And with dagger against sword, I always seek the narrow play. Here I am in the narrow and I can strike you well. Like it or not, you must suffer.

[In the Paris, this Scholar is the Master and his left foot is outside of (behind) his opponent's.]

Your dagger is not strong; I set your back in motion so that I have compelled [you] to turn
Around. You will not be able to reveal to me [your] sad face.

If the Player that came before me had known to do this defense, he would have put his left hand to the Scholar behind his elbow in this way, turning him in the way that is demonstrated here. Then I should have had no need to make the counter to the Master who is in guard with the dagger.

[In the Morgan, the Master is missing his crown. In the Paris, the Master is missing his garter.]

And whoever would have struck the sword into me and under the crown of the head,
I will have made this covering, the elbow having been seized by the left [hand];
And using my own hand, the back of the one playing would be turned.
Thence the dagger would have struck, his kidneys having been penetrated.

If, to the Master that stands in guard with the dagger against the sword, someone comes attacking with a downward blow to his head, he steps forward and he makes this cover quickly, and from the turn [the Master] pushes his elbow and then he can immediately strike him. Also, he can bind the sword with his arm in the way that the fourth play of the sword in one hand is done, and you can also find the Middle Bind in the third play of the dagger (where the hold is a hand's width from the face).

### Notes

- Page numbered "21" in upper right corner (old foliation) and "21" at bottom
- This page marks the transition from wrestling (abrazare) to sword and dagger (spada e daga) combat
- Four pairs of figures, now armed with swords and daggers
- Top figures appear in guard positions; bottom figures show engaged combat
- The crowned Master figure is visible in the top-left pair
- Text references "Spada e daga" (sword and dagger), "Remedio" (remedy/master), and "Scolaro" (scholar)

### Translation — Claude Vision

Of this other close play of the arm, another play of and of every gathering of the arm and mine and I killed with the Sword that the death. We carried high arm to die, I the arm and he of the Scholar thus. And the killing of the chasing to death with the dagger, to two because and to the sum high the arm: that the Sword was the killing that sum of the first death, power of my the arm and I we call the Sword, power the play arm and at this that great gulf of force: pardon me and his guard Sword of my arm.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[6] *This match is one of dagger to sword:
The sword invites against the dagger that he holds,
And he will demonstrate through his Scholar
The way in which this play may be done.*

This is one way to defeat dagger against sword. The man with the dagger grabs the man with the sword by the collar and warns: “I will strike you with my dagger before you can draw your sword from its scabbard.” The man with the sword says “Try and strike me then, for I am ready.” And as the man with the dagger attacks, the man with the sword responds in the manner shown in the next picture.

[7] *In this fashion the sword defends against the dagger:
I will strike you with the sword; the dagger can do nothing.*

When the man with the dagger raises his arm to strike me, I immediately press the sheath of my sword against his dagger arm in such a way that his arm is jammed. I then quickly draw my sword, and I can strike him before he has a chance to even touch me with his dagger. I could also take the dagger from his hand using the method of the First Dagger Remedy Master, or I could put him into the middle bind, using the third play of the First Dagger Remedy Master.

[8] *This is another odd match:
The sword makes an invitation against the dagger.
The sword will make the play of the Scholar
And will demonstrate that the dagger can do nothing.*

Here is another way for the sword to defeat the dagger. In this one I hold my sword with its point on the ground, as you see drawn here, and I say to the man with the dagger, who has grabbed me by the collar: “Go ahead and attack me with your dagger from this position. And when you try I will strike against your arm with my sword still in the scabbard, then I will draw my sword as I pass backwards with my right foot, and in this way I will be able to strike you with my sword before you are able to strike me with your dagger.

[9] This is a similar defense to the one shown before, although it is done slightly differently. As the man with dagger raises his arm to strike, I quickly raise my sheathed sword up under his dagger, aiming the point of my sheathed sword into his face, while at the same time passing back with my lead foot. From here I can strike him as you see drawn in the next picture.

[This Scholar wears a crown.]

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

This is a match which is of the dagger against the sword. He who has a dagger and holds the swordsman by the chest says "I will strike with my dagger before you draw your sword from the scabbard." He of the sword says "Attack, for I am ready." And with that, the swordsman does that which is depicted hereafter.

[In the Morgan, the Master is missing his crown.]

When he lifts his arm to give it to me with the dagger, instantly I put my scabbard on his dagger arm in such a way that he cannot give me grief. And quickly I draw my sword, and I can strike before he can touch me with his dagger. Also, I can take the dagger from his hand in the same way as does the First Master of dagger. And again, I can bind him in the Middle Bind, which is the third play of the dagger (of the First Master who is Remedy).

That best moving of what will have been played, and [is] careful in the art.
I would neither cover nor likewise strike the point to that left unprotected.

### Notes

- Four pairs of sword and dagger combat figures
- Crowned Master figure visible in the top-left position
- The text extensively discusses sword and dagger techniques, grips, and counters
- Bottom-left figures show a disarming or binding technique
- Text is extremely challenging to read; extensive abbreviations and faded ink
- Many readings are tentative due to the scribal hand and condition of the manuscript

## Folio 20r
**Section:** Sword in One Hand

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

foy [?]. ¶ Che chi soma buono che nui lo buozo [?] Moy non poy fir /

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top:
This play was of the fourth that Paolo carries. The first. That the first and who the play and who died the play. That who sum good that we the arm. I cannot play, not anyone, scholar.

Middle-left:
I am firm, three Players who only this Scholar, and we the sum of guarding. The Other the sum Scholar that the sum the guard and the play Sword, sum the blow and play: And that blow sum at guarding Scholar that the play dead: that was the Sword wrestling sum and three.

Middle-right:
We are against: who guardful: Scholar carrying. That the and force that the carrying that at good side. The acquisition of the sum of the Scholar: Who at my not right sum and that of Power. And guard that of that long and Scholar and sum play of everyone of death sum of Scholar and the Scholar in the carrying that the sum of the. And this death goes sum completion the play sum. ### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[10] *I will strike the eye in your face with my scabbard
And I won't refrain from striking you with the sword.*

This is the continuation of the play of the Master who made the preceding defense. And I am performing it exactly as he said to do it. And as you can plainly see, you will give me no trouble with your dagger.

[1] Here are three opponents who all want to kill this Master. The first aims to kill him with a thrust. The second intends a cut. The third will throw his sword at the master like a spear. If the Master can perform a mighty deed and avoid being killed, then God will have indeed blessed him with great skill.

| 
[2] Whether throwing the sword or striking cuts or thrusts,
It amounts to nothing because of the guard that I hold.
Come one by one whoever wants to go against me
Because I want to contend with you all.
And whoever wants to see covers and strikes,
Taking the sword and binding without fail,
Watch what my Scholars know how to do:
If you don't find a counter, they have no equal.

You are cowards and know little of this art. You are all words without any deeds. I challenge you to come at me one after another, if you dare, and even if there are a hundred of you, I will destroy all of you from this powerful guard. …

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

We are three players that wish to strike this Master. One would strike with the point, another the edge, and another wants to throw his sword against the aforesaid Master, so that it will be a great feat indeed if this Master is not killed. May God make him suffer.

If a wild one throws a sword, or if
The other would prepare to cut to pieces, still that one would only favor me with the point;
This caution teaches, in order that I would not be ridiculed or alarmed.

You are wicked and of this art you know little; you do things that words cannot describe. Come one by one whoever knows what to do and is able, and even if you are a hundred I will waste all of you with this guard (which is so good and strong). …

### Notes

- Page numbered "22" in upper right corner (old foliation) and "22" at bottom
- This page has a different layout: a wide text block at top, then two text columns in the middle, and two pairs of sword combat figures at the bottom
- Top pair shows two figures with longswords in a crossing/binding position
- Bottom pairs show longsword combat with what appears to be half-sword technique
- This appears to be a transition page, possibly introducing the sword (spada) section
- The large block of red and black text in the middle-right is one of the longest text passages in this section

## Folio 20v
**Section:** Sword in One Hand

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top-left:
¶ Qui nello cho a soma lo morire: che la lor soma chi /

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
Here in this at the sum the death: that their sum who my quick sum of Scholar. The played sum to die who that the long player sum the chasing: And of sum guarding thus sum me sum the death guard. And of my Scholars knot guard. That the sum Sword quick me was to guard.

Top-right:

Bottom-left:

The Scholar sum died the guide the good. Through the inside and play that of all we my good the guard sum. And of my death the guard Scholar: and Sum.

Bottom-right:

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[6] Again I have struck your head without stepping
Because of the good cover that I knew to make.

I have rendered you completely unprotected, and now I will easily strike you in the head. And if I choose to pass forward with my rear foot, I can perform close range techniques against you, such as locks, dislocations and grapples.

[7] I uncover you in this way to strike you with my point
To avenge myself on you for every manifest neglect.

I have done what my teacher told me to do. That is to say I stepped off the line making a strong cover. And having rendered my opponent unprotected I now easily place a thrust into his face. And with my left hand I will demonstrate that I can take his sword, and send it to the ground.

[8] Because of the hand that I have put beneath your hilt,
If your sword doesn't go to the ground, call me squint-eyed.
 

From this position I can easily strike or stab you. And if I advance my front foot forward, I can lock you in the middle bind, as shown in the third play of the first Remedy Master of the dagger. Alternately I can do the play shown next, and strike and lock you as shown there.

[In the Getty, the Scholar's opponent has his right foot forward.]

[9] With my left arm, I have bound your right
And will you be presented with many strikes.

Here both your sword and your arm are effectively trapped, and you will not be able to escape before I strike you as described, because you have shown you know nothing of this play.

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

I have found you completely uncovered and I have struck you in the head for certain. And if I want to step forward with my rear foot, I can make many narrow plays against you (that is, the binds and breaks of grappling).

I uncover you in order to strike a bargain with the point extended. I will avenge after this;
The soul having been ground into small pieces, we will be perfected.

I have done that which the Master has said—that is, I stepped out of the way making a good cover. And I found the player uncovered such that I certainly want to thrust my point in his face. And I want to try this with my left hand, to see if I can make your sword hit the ground.

[In the Paris, the Scholar wears a crown.]

You would mock me with your voice, and I shall call you blind;
If your sword, when I catch it by the hilt openly,
Will not fall to the ground, your weakness will remain henceforth.

### Notes

- Four pairs of longsword combat figures
- The crowned Master figure appears in the top-left position
- Top-right pair shows what appears to be a binding or crossing of swords technique
- Bottom-left shows a grappling technique with swords (close-quarters combat)
- Bottom-right shows another close engagement with swords
- Text continues to reference "Scolaro" (Scholar), "Remidio" (Remedy), and "Spada" (Sword)
- The figures are now fully armed with longswords, marking the sword combat section

## Folio 21r
**Section:** Sword in One Hand

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
Here I am in good Scholar guard: and my Sword turned sum Scholar. I who the my turning the hand who sum to master: and much short Sword of sum place Lucifer.

Top-right:
This in Scholar Remedy guard: and this who my blow through the scholar Scholar. Guard who the Scholar and Scholar blow mouth: that the Sword and the sum and Scholar this who the sum in every manner.

Bottom-left:
I am in this play that my the sum and this dead, was of other: And Scholar the Sword my the good killing. That the sum who the takes the Sword. Of the guard who the Sword sum and Scholar manner.

Bottom-right:
And my this sum Sword and the scholar and who the that the was: the Scholar and thus the guard Scholar. Again in killing scholar and Scholar guard. That the good the Scholar in that dead.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[10] *Because of the way in which I have caught your sword,
I will quickly have hollowed out your hand.*

Here I can easily strike you while taking your sword, and by rotating it in your hand I will make you drop it as the only way to prevent yourself being thrown to the ground.

[11] I will make you turn with the left hand
And in that, I want to give you a great blow.

Here I can strike you from the front, but this is not enough. By gripping your elbow I make you turn away, then I wrap my sword around your neck from behind, and you will have no defense to this.

[12] Because of the turn that I have given you by your elbow
I believe I have cut you across the throat.

In the previous drawing I told you I would turn you and then quickly wrap my sword around your neck, as shown here. And if now I fail to cut your throat, then I am a pathetic fool.

[The Getty resembles the Pisani Dossi.]

[13] *This is a good break of the point on the ground
And in this fashion you will come to be in the narrow.*

You aimed a thrust at me and I beat it to the ground. Do you see how you are now unprotected and can be struck? And I can also turn you and do you even more harm, by striking you from behind.

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

I decide to pluck the sword out of your slow hands;
This more-clever hand snatched that of yours in such a manner.

At any time, with the hand, I would have turned the elbow, turning my sword around.
I make you covered in blood. I cannot be deceived.

Now I consider cutting the middle of your neck using the sword;
Then, therefore, I am superior because I throw this forearm back.

### Notes

- Page numbered "23" in upper right corner (old foliation) and "23" at bottom
- Four pairs of longsword combat figures
- Top figures show guard positions with longswords held high
- Bottom-left shows a disarming technique where one figure appears to be taking the other's sword
- Bottom-right shows a close-quarters engagement
- The figures wear short tunics and colored hose (yellow/gold garters visible)
- Text continues the longsword (spada) section with references to guards (posta) and scholars (scolaro)

## Folio 21v
**Section:** Sword in One Hand

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
See the turn that I make outside, chasing you then to reduce. You at this power of and taking well the king, power: and who your Scholars the sum and who killing was: we and my everyone sum.

Top-right:
This my shears and the sum: and the we turn the sum quick. Of the remedy and Scholar the guard carrying. Again sum the turn: and carrying of the flat Scholar. That the Sword the death at the blow: and sum was I dead.

Bottom-center:

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[4] In order to wound you again with this, my point,
I have added my left hand to the sword.

This is a play where if you wish to make this kind of thrust, you should be armoured. If your opponent strikes at you with a thrust or a cut, you first make your cover, and then quickly counter attack as shown.

[The Getty resembles the Pisani Dossi, including the lack of armor.]

[14] *I have sending you to the ground in my thoughts:
Again, you are uncovered so that I can strike you.*

Because I turned you by pushing your elbow, I have quickly come to this position and from here I can throw you to the ground, where you will no longer be able to fight me or anyone else.

[15] *Either your sword is bent or it is broken
And I can strike you from above or from below with mine.*

This opponent struck at my head, and I beat his sword to the ground, coming to the position you see depicted here. Now after forcing you to turn away I will aggressively wrap my sword around your neck.

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

In order to do it again, I will strike a bargain with you using my gloomy point;
Then the left hand retains that sword with strength.

This is a play in which he who wants to thrust the point wants to be armored. When someone strikes at you with the point, or with the edge, make the cover and immediately thrust this in the way that is depicted.

[The Paris resembles the Pisani Dossi, including the lack of armor.]

- Top-right pair shows a similar engagement with swords
- Bottom-center shows a single pair of figures in close combat, one appearing to grapple while armed
- The bottom half of the page has significant empty space
- This may be a transitional page or the end of a subsection
- Page appears to continue the longsword section

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top:
At the sum two guards: Sword one stands: who the takes and to me and Counsel of Scholar and the Scholar other guard that their of killing Scholar: of the sum Scholar the the killing of sum the carrying: that the first long: and the sum: and my carrying the Scholar of the sum and the sum and who the guard: and through the sum and the Sword. And the sum of the Sword who the dying sum Scholar of the good playing sum Scholar the play of sum. And the in this and the sum the sum the sum killing death dead sum the and the sum: and who the sum Scholar sum quick sum the dying: and who of dead the things that the sum Scholar. And the arms sum the sum the dying: And the sword that sum things the Scholar was sum and carrying: and sum sum. Again sum: Pretending sum sum and the Sum the good carrying: and sum sum. Carriers these.

Bottom-left:
I who sum sum guard: and sum who at Sword Scholar. And the was the flat that was and sum who Scholar: my Sword this and my of scholar: The other sum carrying of the play sum arm. Sum the Scholar the sum sum and sum dead.

Bottom-right:

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[1] We are two guards that are similar to each other, and yet each one is a counter to the other. And for all other guards in this art, guards that are similar are counters to each other, with the exception of the guards that stand ready to thrust—the Long Guard, the Short Guard and the Middle Iron Gate. For when it is thrust against thrust the weapon with the longer reach will strike first. And whatever one of these guards can do so can the other.

And from each guard you can make a “turn in place” or a half turn. A turn in place is when without actually stepping you can play to the front and then to the rear on the same side. A half turn is when you make a step forwards or backwards and can switch sides to play on the other side from a forwards or backwards position. A full turn is when you circle one foot around the other, one remaining where it is while the other rotates around it.

Furthermore you should know that the sword can make the same three movements, namely stable turn, half turn and full turn.

Both of these guards drawn below are named the Guard of the Lady.

Also, there are four types of movement in this art, namely passing forwards, returning, advancing, and withdrawing.

[Text spans both images.]

[2] Six Masters we are, and we dispute one to another
Each does something that the others do not:
And every one of them holds his sword in guard;
We will explain and demonstrate that which they are.

For throwing I am well-prepared,
On a big step I will strike a bargain.

We are six guards and each of us is different from the other, and I am the first to speak of my purpose. My method is to throw my sword. The other guards follow after me. I believe they will tell you themselves about their particular virtues.

[3] Against the grip of hand and also the throw,
With this guard, I know well how to ward.

I am a good guard, in armor or without armor, and against a spear or a sword thrown from the hand, I am confident I will not be harmed, because I know how to beat them aside and thus evade them.

### Notes

- Page numbered "24" in upper right corner (old foliation) and "24" at bottom
- This page has a large text block at the top taking up roughly a third of the page
- Two pairs of longsword figures at the bottom
- The top text block appears to be an instructional passage covering multiple guards and techniques
- Bottom-left pair shows two figures in guard positions with longswords
- Bottom-right pair shows two figures similarly posed with longswords
- All figures appear to be wearing crowns/coronets, possibly indicating masters
- This is one of the more text-heavy pages in this section

## Folio 22v
**Section:** Sword in Two Hands

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
The two guards of the sum long guard sum: that my sum sum and the Sword of the sum killing. And sum good Scholar sum in of Scholar was and oh the sum guard and my sum carrying of sum the my play sum regarding.

Top-right:
The play sum long guard counter Sword sum that sum Scholar guard: and the sum the Sword. Who the and sum Scholar was and guarding sum arm at sum. The play and sum sum the good: my play of the other sum guard.

Bottom-left:
And guard and this of Scholar sum of sum my sum: who the guard scholar this of sum and who of sum Scholar: that my who who at that Scholar the and sum who at play: that the dead sum the sum Scholar sum the Sword and the the sum the Scholar and sum.

Bottom-right:
The this Sword sum the Sword Dagger. And who at sum the sum and Scholar sum death. Who the right and sum the death the Scholar: that the who the play and sum and Scholar sum the play sum and the sum the of sum to die. That who the sum and the Scholar sum of the sum the arm and the sum sum carrying and sum of sum and sum and who sum the the Sword. At these good sum carrying who the the carrying.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[4] In order to strike farther and to step stronger
Against armor I want to stand in this way.

I am the guard to use if you want to extend a long thrust, because my grip on the sword increases its reach. I am good to use against you if you and I are armored, because I can make a quick thrust to the front which will not miss you.

[5] Against dagger and against sword, armored
And unarmored, I want to be found in this way.

I am a good guard against sword, axe and dagger if I am armored, because I grip the sword with my left hand at the middle. And this is particularly useful against the dagger, which can do more harm to me at close range than the other weapons.

[6] This grip is the noble Stance of the Queen:
She defends against cutting and thrusting in every way.

I am named the Guard or Post of the Lady, and I am different from the four sword guards that came before me, even though they are themselves different from each other. And although the next guard that opposes me seems to be my guard also, you will note that I am not using my sword reversed as a poleaxe, whereas that is how he is using his.

[7] This sword I use for a sword and an axe:
With harness and without, whoever can take me, go ahead.

This sword is being used as both a sword and a poleaxe. And its great force can stop any attack from a lighter weapon. This guard is also the High Guard of the Lady, who with her skill can fool the other guards, because you will think she is going to attack you with a strike, but instead she will attack you with a thrust. All I have to do is raise my arms above my head, and I can then quickly launch a thrust at you.

### Notes

- Four pairs of longsword combat figures

- Top-right shows a similar guard position, described as a counter
- Bottom-left figure pair shows a different guard with swords held lower
- Bottom-right figure pair shows a technique involving both sword and dagger reference
- All figures appear to wear crowns or coronets
- The text references "guardara" (guards), "longa" (long), and "contrara" (counter)
- This page continues the systematic presentation of sword guards and their counters

## Folio 23r
**Section:** Sword in Two Hands

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top-left:
¶ Lo primo fendente si e questo che si chiama fendente mandritto, e questo si e lo primo colpo dela spada, e questo colpo taglia per lo traverso dela testa in lo brazo sinistro, e questo colpo si fa riverso. Questo giurador[e] che si fa riverso lo fendente per la parte riversa. E manda per lo ponto de la spada al volto e ala testa. ¶ Quel colpo fendente lo magistro fece cosi che [fece] guardare delle porte al scolaro quando che manchava la guarda de posta tal mago.

Colpo fendente.

Top-right:
¶ Colpo sottano sono questi e chiamansse colpi sottani perche naschono e vegna da la parte de sotto e va ala testa e alla faza. ¶ E la prima si comenza dalo brazo dritto e va per la via del fendente per roverso. ¶ Ancora de qua li colpi sot[t]ani sono per roverso e vanno fendimento in posta longo.

Colpo Sottani.

Bottom-left:
¶ Colpi mezani sono altri nomi[n]ati e che son colpi meza[ni] e mezo[s] colpi sopra mano e sotto mano e sono boni e grandi sforzo e portano, e devanno essere fati come lo fendente dela[?] ponta breva. Quella questa colpi[?] manchano de la posta veguda. ¶ Che nostro chamane questo bello tondelo ella sopra.

Colpo mezani.

Bottom-right:
¶ De ponto le punte sonno chiamade e armade. ¶ Ella ponto tutta le cose la e rompono ella dela fiancho. ¶ Che ponto portano e voy le armadure ello son dei brevi. E lo lor nomo portan falze, ponto falzare de falzi. ¶ E lo lor sforzo e molto mandar tutta flore contra e desloda. ¶ E tutte le punte me son ogn[i] do posta. E vanno de posta longo e breve.

Le punte.

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
The first fendente is this one which is called the forehand fendente, and this is the first blow of the sword, and this blow cuts across from the head to the left arm, and this blow is done in reverse. This oath-taker who does the reverse fendente on the reverse side. And it sends the point of the sword to the face and head. This fendente blow the master made so that [he made] the student guard the gates when the guard of the position failed.

Label: Fendente blow.

Top-right:
The sottano blows are these and they are called sottano blows because they are born and come from below and go to the head and face. And the first begins from the right arm and goes by the path of the fendente in reverse. Also from here the sottano blows are in reverse and go cutting into the long guard.

Label: Sottano blows.

Bottom-left:
The mezzano blows are named differently and these are half-blows, forehand and backhand, and they are good and carry great force, and they should be made like the fendente of the short point. Those blows that fail from the seen guard. Our [master] calls this beautiful circling from above.

Label: Mezzano blows.

Bottom-right:
Regarding the point: the thrusts are so called and armed. The point breaks through all things from the flank. The points carry and you the armatures and they are short. And their name carries false, false point of the false edge. And their force is much to send all the flower against and dislodge. And all the thrusts come from every guard. And they go from long and short guard.

Label: The thrusts.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[8] Downward Blows

We are downward blows and we dispute
By cleaving the teeth with proper intention:
We have not delayed in wounding
And we return to guard from move to move.

We are the downward blows. In this art our method is to cut with precision from the teeth down to the knee. And we can easily end up in any of the low guards. We are highly effective in breaking the other guards, and with each blow we leave a trail of blood. We downward blows strike fast, and thereafter we return to our guard back the way we came.

[9] Under Blows

We are the blows called Under,
Who always seek to strike the hands;
And we dispute from the knees up
And we teach a lesson by returning with downward blows.

We are the rising blows, and we go from the knee to the middle of the forehead, following the same path that the downward blows follow. And we return down the same path as we ascend, unless we choose to remain high in the Long Guard.

[10] Middle Blows

We middle blows go crossing;
We go wounding from the knee and up;
And we beat thrusts out of the way
And strike a bargain by redoubling the blow;
And if we of the middle blow enter cleaving,
We waste many people with such blows.
 

We are the middle blows, and we are so-called because we go crosswise through the middle of the path of both the downward blows and the rising blows. And we strike with the true edge of the sword from the right, and with the false edge of the sword from the left. And our path could be anywhere between the knee and the head.

[11] The Thrusts

Thrusts we are, of greatest offense
And we make our dispute to all strikes;
Venomous we are, more than a serpent,
And we kill more people than any strike;
And our thrusts to the blows do say:
So little do you cut that we sew like a needle.

We are the cruel and deadly thrusts. Our target is the body’s center line, and we can strike anywhere between the groin and the forehead. And we thrusts can be made in five ways: two of us can be made from high guards, one from each side, and two can be made from low guards, also one from each side. The fifth one comes from a center line guard, and can be made from Middle Iron Gate, the Short Guard, or the Long Guard.

### Notes

- Four figures demonstrating the four basic sword attacks: fendente (descending cut), sottano (rising cut), mezzano (middle cut), and punta (thrust).
- Each figure holds a sword overhead or in a striking position, with a crown indicating master status.
- Page numbered "25" in upper right corner (old foliation) and "25" at bottom.
- Some text is quite small and partially faded, making certain words uncertain.

---

## Folio 23v
**Section:** Sword in Two Hands

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top-left:
¶ Qui cominzano le guardie che de spade a doy mane e grande. La posta si e comp[i]ta e bona de spade. ¶ De queste guardie si e tredese Longha e curta e mezo le posso io fare de ogni guardie e non del tuto bono sopra uno e mezo fare. ¶ Ella Frontale de le poste ella matrice. ¶ Perche de posste lo primo acresser ello fa volto e le certo redole ella da mesura. ¶ Questa sia tuta falle de lo largo grande. Posta de ferro pulsativa.

¶ Posta de ferro pulsativa.

Top-right:
¶ Questa si e posta de donna che per tuta mane e elle e la piu bella. ¶ E de tute coste ella si e de grande avantaze. ¶ E guarda lo scolaro de lo ben fare. E la pontta destra Longha e la sinestra e curta. ¶ Ella fendente de spada punta ella blasturra. ¶ Ella cominza lo soprammano e soppra che fiancho ella pulsativa.

¶ Posta de donna destra e pulsativa.

Bottom-left:
¶ Questa si e posta de fenestra che e fenestra che manchar[e] fenestra la pugna. ¶ Ella manaza de fare alle elle poste. E non stare grande de breve alle poste. E chi se non guarda[re] quello che la fenestra e ello le Speranza. ¶ Ella sforzo quello de bono que muda e covertte le posste e ferischa e traversa e quello le vado ella per luy posta.

¶ Posta de fenestra instabile.

Bottom-right:
¶ Questa si e posta de donna la fenestra che e la donna e la Rosa ella e brazal posta. ¶ Ella chiama e e certa le fenestre e altro perto. ¶ Le lanze drette e le doy punte voce companie [?]. ¶ E sop[r]a dol guardare de loy posta.

¶ Posta de donna la fenestra pulsativa.

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
Here begin the guards of the two-handed sword, and they are great. The guard is complete and good for swords. Of these guards there are thirteen, long and short and half, I can make them from every guard, and not entirely good to do one and a half. And the Frontal of the guards is the matrix. Because from the guard the first advance makes the turn and the certain rules give measure. This is the whole deception of the broad great. Guard of the iron, striking.

Label: Posta de ferro pulsativa (Striking Iron Gate guard).

Top-right:
This is the posta de donna (woman's guard) which for the full hand is the most beautiful. And of all sides she has great advantage. And the student watches the good work. And the right point is long and the left is short. And the fendente of the sword point is the striker. And it begins the forehand and above the flank, striking.

Label: Posta de donna destra e pulsativa (Right woman's guard, striking).

Bottom-left:
This is the posta de fenestra (window guard) which is the window that the window's fist fails. And it threatens to make all the guards. And do not stand broadly at the short guards. And whoever does not guard what the window is and the Hope. And the effort of that good one who changes and covers the guards and strikes and crosses and that goes for his guard.

Label: Posta de fenestra instabile (Unstable window guard).

Bottom-right:
This is the posta de donna la fenestra (woman's window guard) which is the woman and the Rose and is a brazen guard. And it calls and is certain of the windows and other openings. The straight lances and the two points voice companions. And above two guards of his guard.

Label: Posta de donna la fenestra pulsativa (Woman's window guard, striking).

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[12] The Iron Gate (Powerful)

We are called stances and guards by name,
And we are each one similar and contrary to another;
And following the way we stand and are positioned,
We will demonstrate how to make one against another.

The Full Iron Gate, I am low to the ground
So that I always restrain cuts and thrusts.

Here begin the guards of the two handed sword, of which there are twelve. The first is the low Iron Gate, which is a very strong guard, and a good guard in which to wait for an attack by every kind of hand-held weapon, whatever its length, as long as you have a good sword that is not too long. And from this guard if you make cover with a passing step you move to the Narrow Game. Or you can exchange thrusts, striking home with yours. Or, as you step, you can beat the opponent’s thrust to the ground. And this guard can cover attacks from all angles.

[14] The Stance of the Queen on the Right (Powerful)

I am the Stance of the Queen, noble and proud
For making defense in every manner;
And whoever wants to contend against me
Will want to find a longer sword than mine.

This is the Guard of the Lady, from which you can make all seven of the sword’s strikes and cover them too. And from this guard you can break the other guards with the strong blows you can make, and you can also quickly exchange thrusts. Advance your front foot offline, and then pass diagonally with your rear foot. This will take you to a position where your opponent is unprotected, and you will then be able to quickly strike him.

[15] The Stance of the Window (Fluid)

I am the royal Stance of the True Window
And I am always quick with the whole art.

This is the Window Guard who is always quick, skillful and deceptive. She is a master at covering and striking. She threatens all opposing guards, whether high guards or low guards. She moves quickly from this guard to other guards to confuse her opponent. And she is a very good guard from which to make powerful thrusts, break the opponent’s thrust or exchange points.

[22] The Stance of the Queen on the Left (Powerful)

I am the left Stance of the True Window;

I am just as swift in this one as from the right.

This is the Guard of the Lady on the left, and she is always quick to cover or strike. She generates powerful blows and easily breaks the thrust, driving it to the ground. Also, because of her skill in traversing, she can quickly enter into the Narrow Game, a game she is very familiar with.

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

Each one calls the position by name, and the deceptive guard.
The second is similar to another, and indeed to the counter.
And just as this has been placed, so we understand similar action.

I am called the Iron Gate, equal to the earth from all;
I always prepare something again, chop and strike with the point.

Here commence the guards of the sword in two hands, and there are 12 guards, and the first is the Full Iron Gate which stands in great strength and is good for awaiting all hand-held weapons, both extended and withdrawn, as long as she has good sword (not one of too much length). She steps with a cover and goes to the narrow, she exchanges thrusts and she delivers her own; she also beats thrusts to the ground and always goes with steps, and against all blows she makes a cover. And whoever joins a brawl with her will make great defense without fatigue.

Brave, elevated, I am the Lady’s Position, high,
And in any quarter by this manner I defend limbs with fury.

This is the Stance of the Queen, which can make all seven blows of the sword and can also cover all blows. She breaks the other guards through the great blows that she makes, and she is always ready for the exchange of thrusts: the foot which is in front advances out of the way and the one behind steps to the side. And her companion is made uncovered, and that one can immediately strike him for certain.

I am this the regal Position, certainly, of the True Window,
And fleeting, I always disclose in accordance with my clear art.

This is the Stance of the Casement Window on the right, which is always ready with malice and trickery, and she is the Master of covering and of striking and with all the guards she makes her disputes (with the high and with the low). She often goes from one guard to another in order to fool her companion, and she throws great thrusts, and knows how to break and to exchange them—those plays she can make very well.

I am called the On the Left Position itself, and Of the True Window.
Thus, I am certainly fast on the right, just as this way on the left.

This is the Stance of the Queen on the left, and she is always ready to cover and to strike. She makes great blows and breaks the thrusts, beating them to the ground, and she enters into the narrow play by knowing how to thwart. Such a guard knows how to make these plays well.

### Notes

- Four figures showing sword guards (poste): posta de ferro pulsativa, posta de donna destra pulsativa, posta de fenestra instabile, and posta de donna la fenestra pulsativa.
- Each figure is crowned (indicating master status) and holds a longsword in different guard positions.
- This begins the section on two-handed sword guards (guardie de spade a doy mane).
- Text is in red and blue ink for decorated initials.

## Folio 24r
**Section:** Sword in Two Hands

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top-left:
¶ Posta longa, chiamo[r]e, prima instabile. ¶ Ella si e posta molto da fare e da fare quello che la magistra de lagarda[?] e de lo mettere e lo levare la spada. E lo Pu[n]to fare, tosto la puntta e po finire, tutta le posste granda. ¶ Ella tolta la Frontale e la prima sop[r]a. Ella la cabeza e la testa el la p[r]imo erta [?] della la cabeza domandar. ¶ La Pulsativa de opra.

¶ Posta longa instabile.

Top-right:
¶ Questa e mezana posta deffensiva e che son cinque. ¶ Che son forte e quatro, ella che fa e posta che fa e destro porta e di man revexa[?], ella lo stabelle in la erta, Arma[?] e la fondatura e la volta, e la tuta. ¶ Ella che stare a[?] la ponta de lo magistro Fiore, grande che fallo lo mal de la spada muove, e lo volto. ¶ E quello de punte manzi [?] tuto manzi de Fiore.

¶ Forma di ferro mezana stabile.

Bottom-left:
¶ Questa si e po[s]ta longa che se vede longe. Posta e maistro e lo ello lo fendente e la pulsativa. ¶ Mastro fenyr lo po cantar de pugna, e no possi ella la companya. ¶ De pos[ta] e grande de la guarda e la cassa de la conquesta.

¶ Posta breve stabile.

Bottom-right:
¶ Questa ella donna de compagnone per ella delle granda poste longe lo nodo de trame. ¶ Ella con grande fallo e fallo e dello stagno ella de lo maestro tostano. ¶ E ella de apresso volto che ne quella bona de le fianchi accosta allo pe de guarda[?] manestra. Posta e le donna de la Frontale de lo fiancho la ponta delle erta [?] de delle poste. De la che trovasse e cosi ella lo ella la partia [?] del opra. Ella altra delle porte. De la che lo pe che e lo deffendere.

¶ Donna di cong[i]unture stabile.

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
Long guard, called the first unstable. And it is a guard much for doing and for doing what the mistress of the guard [teaches] and for putting and lifting the sword. And to make the point, quickly the thrust and then finish, all the great guards. And taken the Frontal and the first above. And the head and the first rise of the head demand. The striking above.

Label: Posta longa instabile (Long guard, unstable).

Top-right:
This is the middle defensive guard and there are five. Which are strong and four, and she who makes a guard makes a right carry and backhand, and the stable in the rise, armed and the foundation and the turn, and all. And she who stands at the point of master Fiore, great one who does the harm of the sword's movement, and the turn. And that of thrusts forward, all forward of Fiore.

Label: Forma di ferro mezana stabile (Middle iron form, stable).

Bottom-left:
This is the long guard that is seen from afar. Guard and master and the fendente and the striking. Master to finish can sing of the fist, and cannot the company. From the guard is great of the guard and the case of the conquest.

Label: Posta breve stabile (Short guard, stable).

Bottom-right:
This is the woman of the companion for her of the great long guards the knot of the plot. And with great failure and failure and of the tin and of the swift master. And she who is near the turn that to that good one of the flanks comes close to the foot of the left guard. Guard and the woman of the Frontal of the flank the point of the rise of the guards. Of the one found and so she the part of the work. And the other of the gates. Of the one the foot that is the defense.

Label: Donna di congiunture stabile (Woman of the joints, stable).

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[16] The Middle Iron Gate (Stable)

The Middle Iron Gate, I am strongest
For giving death with thrusts and downward blows:
And by extending my sword, I feel that
From the narrow play I always defend myself.

This is Half Iron Door, because it stays in the middle and is a strong guard. But, she wants a long sword. She throws strong thrusts and beats with force the swords upwards, and returns with a downward blow for the head or arms, and returns to its guard. But it is called Door, because it is strong and it is a strong guard that badly it can break without danger, and without coming to the close.

[17] The Extended Stance (Fluid)

I am the Extended Stance with my short sword
And I often strike the throat with cunning.

This guard is the Long Guard, which is full of deception. She is skilled in probing the guards to see if she can deceive her opponent. If she needs to strike the opponent with a thrust, she is well-suited to do it. As for the opponent’s blows, she knows how to avoid them and then strike back with blows of her own. This guard employs deception more than any other guard.

[20] The Wild Boar's Tusk (Stable)

I am the strong Stance of the Boar's Tusk.

My tactic against all the guards is to probe.

This is the Boar’s Tusk, because it strikes the way the wild boar strikes. Sometimes it makes powerful thrusts from below up into the face, without stepping forward, and it returns along the same path with a downward strike to the arms. Other times as it thrusts the point of the sword high into the face, it advances the front foot forwards, then returns to its guard with a downward strike to the head or the arms. Then it quickly launches another thrust with another advance of the front foot. And this guard can mount a good defense against the Narrow Game.

[21] The Shortened Stance (Stable)

I am the Shortened Stance and I have the length of the sword;
Often I thrust the point and then return with cunning.

This is the Short Guard that is more effective with a longer sword. It is a deceptive guard but it is risky to wait in. It is constantly moving, trying to see if it can enter with a thrust and a step against the opponent. And this guard is more effective in armor than without armor.

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

I am the strong Iron, and named Door in the Middle,
And I give heavy blows, and I seek death with the point.

This is the Middle Iron Gate because she stands in the middle, and she is a strong guard but she doesn't want her sword extended. She throws strong thrusts and beats swords upward with strength, and returns with a downward blow through the head or through the arms and then simply returns to her guard. But she is well-named "gate" because she is strong; she is a strong guard that cannot be broken easily without danger and coming to the narrow.

I remain the short sword, but, however, in this Position I am called
Long, very often cutting the neck using this clever device.

This is the Extended Stance which is full of deceit; she probes the other guards to see if she can deceive a companion. If she can strike with a thrust, she knows how to do it well; she voids the blows and she can wound when she is able. More than any other guard, her tactic is deception.

I am the Position of the Wild Boar, brave and immoderate of strength,
Well-proved to extend with all precautions.

This is the Wild Boar's Tusk since the tusk of a wild boar has a similar method of striking. She attacks with great underhand thrusts ending in the face and doesn't move a step, and then returns with a downward blow down to the arms. And sometimes she throws her point to the face and goes with the point high, and in that throw of the point she suddenly advances the foot which is in front and returns to her guard, and immediately throws another thrust with an advance of the foot, and thus defends well against the narrow play.

I am this, the Shorter Position, and I go back over the long sword.
I often threaten with the point; nevertheless, thenceforth I return thither.

This is the Shortened Stance, which wants a long sword, and she is a malicious guard which doesn't have stability. Also, she always moves and watches to see if she can enter with her point and with a step against her companion, and more appropriate is this guard in armor than without armor.

### Notes

- Four figures showing sword guards: posta longa instabile, forma di ferro mezana stabile, posta breve stabile, and donna di congiunture stabile.
- Page numbered "26" in the upper right corner.
- The figures show progressively lower guard positions, from extended long guard to close guards.
- Some text is difficult to read due to fading and the small script.

## Folio 24v
**Section:** Sword in Two Hands

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top-left:
¶ Posta [?]
¶ Questa si e posta de coda longa, che si torpe in la man e lo colpo per mandar punte e lo braco per riverso e vene[?] e dentro. ¶ E la posta riversa sente posta falmente. E ello del maestro centura le cossa frondea e potente, e allo da qualita si e la destrina [?] e la qualita e ella la instabile per intrero.

¶ Posta di coda longa stabile.

Top-right:
¶ Questa si e posta de bicorno che finire. ¶ Ella Frontale dove la prima e meza la frontale. ¶ E la de posta porta longe de punta. Ella manaza. ¶ E quello mastra chos[a] la posta di posta frontale.

¶ Posta de Bicorno instabile.

Bottom-left:
¶ Questa si e posta frontale, e donna[?] de la ballazana dimandata, posta de corona. ¶ E de ponta la grande e ella e forte e le ¶ de la ponta alto le mure. ¶ Ella la monta e ella la testa e la pontta frontale e la ponta venire. ¶ La possta parte[?] finire de tutta finir[e]. La partia una tuta. ¶ E con questa lo posta si e la fenestra e ella la longe e talle ella la[?] pulsativa della corona[?]. La possta della posta di fenestra e la qualita gra quista ella la corta e deffendere.

E lo fata dalla e che la ponta e mandata ella la possta frontale ella e da la tutta [?] la erta la mandata e non cade le corpo elle questo longo.

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
This is the posta di coda longa (long tail guard), which twists in the hand and the blow to send thrusts and the arm in reverse and comes inside. And the reverse guard feels the guard falsely. And of the master the belt the things leafy and powerful, and to that quality is the dexterity and the quality and the unstable within.

Label: Posta di coda longa stabile (Long tail guard, stable).

Top-right:
This is the posta de bicorno (two-horned guard) that finishes. And the Frontal where the first and half the frontal. And from the guard carries long thrusts. And it threatens. And that mistress the guard of the frontal guard.

Label: Posta de Bicorno instabile (Two-horned guard, unstable).

Bottom-left:
This is the frontal guard, and the woman of the ballazana called, guard of the crown. And of the point the great and she is strong and of the point high the walls. And she rises and the head and the frontal point and the point comes. The guard partly finishes of all finishing. The part a whole. And with this guard is the window and the long and such the striking of the crown. The guard of the window guard and the quality of this and the short and defending.

Label: Posta frontale di una corona instabile (Frontal guard of a crown, unstable).

Bottom-right:
This is the woman of company who puts of the mistress. And she is the guard of [?] and that the hand and the long guard and such she changes the face. Of the strong and then the hand. And the made from and that the point is sent and the frontal guard and is of the whole the rise the sent and the body does not fall and this long.

Label: Posta di donna guardare mezana stabile (Woman's guard watching, middle stable).

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[18] The Frontlet Stance called the Crown (Fluid)

The Frontlet Stance, I am called the Crown;
I pardon no one, not from the edge nor from the point.

This is the Forehead Guard, called by some instructors the Crown Guard. She is a very good guard for crossing swords, and is also very good against thrusts. If she is attacked with a high thrust, she crosses swords while stepping off line. If she is attacked with a low thrust, she also steps offline, but this time she drives the opponent’s sword to the ground. She can also do other things. For example, in response to a thrust she can pass backwards with the front foot and respond with a downward strike to the head or arms, ending in the Boar’s Tusk, then she can quickly throw a thrust or two with advancing steps, then deliver a downward strike, ending in that same guard.

[23] The Stance of the Long Tail (Stable)

Stance of the Long Tail, I am extended to the ground
Forward and backward, I always make offense:
And if I step forward and enter with a downward blow,
I come to the narrow play without fail.

This is the Long Tail Guard Guard that extends behind you down to the ground. She can attack with a thrust, and can also move forwards to cover and strike. And if she passes forward while striking downwards she can easily enter the Narrow Game. This is a good guard to wait in, because you can quickly transition from it into other guards.

[24] The Two-Horned Stance (Fluid)

I make myself called the Stance of the Anvil
If I have enough deceit, I will not challenge it.

This is the Two Horned Guard, which is held so strongly locked in position that its point cannot be moved off the center line. And this guard can do all of the things that the Long Guard can do. And the same is true of the Window Guard and the Forehead Guard.

[In the Novati, the left hand is not reversed; this may be due to an error on the part of the artist hired to clean up the Pisani Dossi manuscript images before publication.]

[25] The Stance of the Boar's Tusk in the Middle (Stable)

This is the Middle Boar’s Tusk, so named because the boar has two tusks, one low and this one in the middle, by which I mean this one stays on the center line. And the Middle Boar’s Tusk can do whatever the Low Boar’s Tusk can do. Just as the wild boar strikes diagonally with its tusks, so you strike diagonally with your sword, in such a way as to displace your opponent’s sword, from which position, having uncovered your opponent, you can launch thrusts, or destroy his hands, head or his arms.

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

I am called the famous Crown, the Frontlet Position itself.
I don’t spare each one, destroying with cuts and the point.

This is the Frontlet Stance, and some Masters call her the Stance of the Crown. She is good at crossing, and she is also good against thrusts because if a point comes attacking upwards, she crosses, stepping out of the way. And she also steps out of the way if a point comes attacking low, beating the thrust to the ground. Again, she can do it differently, such that in the attack of a thrust she returns her foot behind and comes with a downward blow through the head and through the arms and goes to the Boar's Tusk, and then suddenly throws a thrust or two with an advance of her foot and returns with a downward blow from her own guard.

Behold! I am dragged forward into the ground, the Long Tail Position. And before
And after I very often drive, piercing blows to blows.

This is the Stance of the Long Tail which is extended toward the ground. She can thrust the point behind and she can cover and strike in front, and if she steps forward and attacks with a downward blow, into the narrow play she enters without failure. And such a guard is good for waiting, because from her someone can enter into the others quickly.

I am surely called by all the actual Two-Horned Position.
Don’t even ask how deceptive, how clever I would be according to you.

This is the Stance of the Two-Horned Anvil, which is so strongly enclosed that she always remains with her point toward the middle of the way. And she can do that which the Extended Stance can do, and this can similarly be said of the Stance of the Window and the Headband Stance.

### Notes

- Four figures showing additional sword guards: posta di coda longa stabile, posta de bicorno instabile, posta frontale di una corona instabile, and posta di donna guardare mezana stabile.
- The figures show diverse guard positions including rear guards (coda longa) and high guards (corona).
- Text marked "Posta [?]" at the very top of the page in red ink appears to be a section header.
- Several readings are uncertain due to faded ink and archaic spelling.
