## Folio 25r
**Section:** Wide Plays

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top (full width, header text):
¶ Spada doy mane aqua comenza mettere in lo zogo del ditto magistro de lo Arte, chi de la spada a doy mane sopra uno colpo. Longo e curta me posso fare, e uno acrescho e un passo allo sopra fendente e punte allo tuo e posta e volte abrazare e una certa de finale posso de tuto me posso fare. ¶ E chiamasi de [fio]re posste lo magistro quello la che tutta e destino e de lo arte. Ella me guardasse fissicha come mette e lo stato Y armizare, de fiore soma compo [?].

Bottom-left:
¶ Qui comenza zogo de spada a doy mane sopra longe. ¶ Che questa mancha si ella mancha per alla posta de lo fendente e la guardia elle porte de spada. Finire a lo volto ale mie spada e me posso fare tutte le posste balatre e pinta lo le ordine e lo fendente, e lo punte e la le porte, e finire lo scudo. ¶ Ello che qui me vene[?] una punta le le orde e come, e me salire, per[?] lo bello de punta.

Bottom-right:
¶ Con posta una punta. ¶ Ella se ordena cossa la matrise de balatrare [?] mi[?]. ¶ Questa possa cossa fronte la orde la spada delle ella la sua mancha dretta cossa ella[?] finire la compagnia delle gre[?] blatre. ¶ Adelante, per que ella la pfata ella le voltare ordene per la mia spada la mia braze la Rechiamandr[?] e la su le porte e che[?] lo cossa le braze, cossa e dello la sua manestra delle braze.

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top (header):
Two-handed sword here begins to put into play of the said master of the Art, who with the two-handed sword upon a blow. Long and short I can make, and one advance and a pass above the fendente and thrusts to yours and guard and turns embracing and a certain finish I can do everything. And the master who is all destiny and of the art calls himself of Fiore's guards. And she who guards me physically as she puts the state and arming, of Fiore's complete field.

Bottom-left:
Here begins the play of the two-handed sword from long range. This failing hand fails at the guard of the fendente and the guard of the sword's gates. To finish at the turn with my sword and I can make all the crossing guards and paint the order and the fendente, and the thrusts and the gates, and finish the shield. And here comes to me a thrust of the orders and how, and to rise, the beauty of the thrust.

Bottom-right:
With a guard a thrust. And it orders the thing of the matrix of crossing. This thing the front orders the sword from her right failing thing, finish the company of the crossings. Forward, because she the said turning orders for my sword my arm the recall and to the gates and that the thing the arms, thing and of his left of the arms.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[26] I am the sword, deadly against all weapons. Neither spear, nor poleaxe, nor dagger can prevail against me. I can be used at long range or close range, or I can be held in the half sword grip and move to the Narrow Game. I can be used to take away the opponent’s sword, or move to grapple. My skill lies in breaking and binding. I am also skilled in covering and striking, with which I seek always to finish the fight. I will crush anyone who opposes me. I am of royal blood. I dispense justice, advance the cause of good and destroy evil. To those who learn my crossings I will grant great fame and renown in the art of armed fighting.

[27] *By crossing with you at the tip of the sword
I have settled my point in your chest from the other side.*

Here begins the Wide Play of the sword in two hands.

This Master who is crossed at the point of his sword with this player says: “When I am crossed at the points, I quickly switch my sword to the other side, and strike him from that side with a downward blow to his head or his arms. Alternately, I can place a thrust into his face, as the next picture will show.”

[In the Pisani Dossi, the player is wearing a crown.]

[28] *With the strike of which the Master spoke who came before,
I have quickly put the point of my sword in your throat.*

I have placed a thrust into his face, as the previous Master said. Also, I could have done what he told you, that is, when my sword was crossed on the right I could have quickly switched sides to the left, striking his head or arms with a downward blow.”

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

I hold the sword constricted in the cross[ing] with the point.
Of the others I am first; I burden the chest with the point.

Here begins the wide play of the sword in two hands with a little crossing; the honor will be to whoever will know to make it.

This Master who is here crossed with this player says "When I am crossed at the tip of the sword, I quickly give a turn to my sword and thus I strike with a downward blow from the other side (that is, through the head and through the arms), or I thrust the point into his face as you see hereafter in my depiction."

[In the Paris, both Masters have their right feet forward. In the Morgan, the player is wearing a garter and crown.]

Now hear my discussion of the earlier master:
The impatient point of the sword approaches the juicy throat.

I have set my point in his face as said my Master who came before. Also, I could have made the [other] play that he said—that is, to have attacked with my sword immediately when I was beside the crossing of the right side: from the other side (that is, from the left) I should have immediately turned my sword into a downward blow to the head and to the arms, as has said my Master that came before.

### Notes

- This page marks the transition from guards to actual sword plays (zogo de spada a doy mane).
- Two pairs of figures shown at the bottom, each pair depicting two swordsmen engaged in combat from long range.
- The top text serves as an introduction to the sword plays section.
- Page numbered "27" in upper right (old foliation).
- The figures wear no armor and fight with longswords.

### Translation — Claude Vision

Again this comes to me as a long blow and half-sword. Again if you make the blow to me, and the touch below searches the thing across the arms and thrust toward the short foot river by the command and above the guard. A thing to guard a good thrust in the guard that runs from point to thrust.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[29] *By crossing at mid-sword, I will strike your left arm;
I will do this quickly because time is short.
 *

I too am crossed in the Wide Play, but this time at the middle of the swords. And immediately after making my cross I let my sword drop down to slide forwards and backwards over his hands. Or, if I choose to pass forward with my right foot and move offline, I can then make a thrust into your chest, as you will see drawn next.

[In the Pisani Dossi, the player is wearing a crown.]

[30] *From the Master who crosses at mid-sword,
I strike you a bargain with that which he has said.*

Here you see me completing the play of my teacher. I have made his cover, and then immediately I do what he said to do, that is I strike first to my opponent’s arms, and then I continue with a thrust into his chest.

[31] *Also from this same crossing
I have grasped your sword in this way:
And before your sword escapes my hand,
By striking I will deal with you like a foul villain.*

My master previously instructed me that when I am crossed at mid-swords with my opponent, I should immediately advance forward and seize his sword as shown, and then strike him with a cut or a thrust. Also I could destroy his leg as you see drawn next, by stomping with my foot against the side of his knee or under the kneecap.

[32] *There is no question of the saying of the earlier Master,
And I make with intent the play that he has said.*

As the previous student told you, our Master taught us this technique. Here I show you how it’s done, and as you can see my opponent can do nothing to stop me.

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

I, the clever one, holding the sword now in the middle, with the sword
As if in a cross, would certainly beat your left shoulder
So much this time, however greatly briefness would be recommended.

Again I am crossed here for the long play, at mid-sword. And immediately when I am crossed, I allow my sword to run off over his hands, and if I want to step out of the way with my right foot, I can thrust my point into his chest as is depicted hereafter.

[In the Morgan, the player is wearing a garter and crown.]

I strike a bargain with you just as that earlier master before said.
He who holds back the sword in the cross[ing], he would be able to deceive.

The play of my Master I have completed, in that I have made his cover and I have quickly executed his saying: I have struck first his arms, and then I have placed my point in his chest.

My Master who came before has taught me that when I am crossed at the mid-sword, I should immediately advance forward and grab his sword (as in this match) in order to strike him with edge or point. Also, I can waste his leg in the way that you will be able to see depicted hereafter by striking with my foot over the back of his leg or under his knee.

The Scholar who came before me says of his Master and mine that he has taught this play, and I do it to crumple [my opponent]. Without a doubt, to do it is little trouble to me.

## Folio 26r

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top-left:
¶ Questa si e posta che se chiamade colpo de villano ella de foy posta. ¶ Ella che la sola e lo colpo e la [?] magistro lo meyo ella de sopra metera. ¶ De questa si e la corage ala torpe[?] e mettera per la volta e grase[?] e metra le pe el pe chiostre[?] le pe dela spada posste di la sop[r]a e le tramezar della la Fronte posste de la coschenzia[?] della Frontera. ¶ E falsso soppra le la sforza della appare. ¶ La lo fior posta de la grassa valse bayonne. ¶ Della lo que e questa sopra foy alle la fiera delle la veye e acro e ponta tutta bracia spada al opra.

Top-right:

E lo esto me per nome entrar[e].

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
This is the guard called the peasant's blow and from the guard. And she who is alone and the blow and the master the middle and from above will put. Of this is the courage to twist and will put through the turn and the fat and will put the foot to the foot closed the foot of the sword's guard from above and the crossing of the frontal guard of the conscience of the frontier. And false above the force that appears. The flower guard of the fat valse bayonet. Of that which is this above was at the beast of the old and sharp and thrust all arm sword to the work.

Top-right:

And the right failing hand is the death thrusted as to do and the contrary, and the right above the right the contrary as. And the beating accepts the right and the guard as of the flower above the flower. And this for me by name to enter.

Bottom-left:
To demand light but by the great hand. But by shadow the foot the two forces and that our rupture and old second bayonet. He who sword that I alone from failing was the force our master will make the student die against. And clear that his guard is the crossed points. Master this hawk mode no. Master by the point thrust and great and here such this great I will make beautiful and as crossed sword.

Bottom-right:
This thrust that I want to make, and the foot I put there through the flank and his right. And it was the guard she makes the calling and the counter-measure. And it was the one above the guard the gates and of that the crossing I the right the thing. And it is the flower the foot the beating.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[33] *I have uncovered you well by stepping out of the way
And I will surely strike your arm while turning.*

This play is named “The Peasant’s Strike” and you do it like this: take a narrow stance with your left foot forward, and wait for the Peasant to attack first with his sword. When he launches his attack, immediately advance your left foot to the left off the line, and step diagonally off line to the left with your right foot, receiving his strike in the middle of your sword. Now let his sword slide off yours to the ground, and then quickly counter-attack with a downward strike to his head or arms, or a thrust into his chest as you see drawn in the next picture. This is also a good play if you are fighting sword versus poleaxe, or against a heavy or light staff.

[In the Getty, the Master is missing his crown.]

[34] *The strike to your arms, that play I make,
And from the narrow play I will cause you other trouble.*

In the previous drawing you saw the Peasant’s Strike, in which you saw a thrust well-placed into the attacker’s chest. And alternatively he could have struck a downward blow to the opponent’s head or the arms, as I explained previously. Also, if the opponent seeks to counter me by striking back up with a rising blow to my arms from the left, I quickly advance my left foot and place my sword over his, and from this position he can do nothing to me.

[35] *When a sword flies for your leg
Make a downward blow to his face or around to his throat:
His arms will be wasted more quickly than his head,
Because the distance is manifest for a shorter time.*

If your opponent strikes to your leg, withdraw your front foot, or pass backwards and strike downwards to his head, as shown in the drawing. With a two handed sword it is unwise to strike to the knee or below, because it is too dangerous for the one striking. If you attack your opponent’s leg, you leave yourself completely uncovered. Now, if you have fallen to the ground, then it is all right to strike at your opponent’s legs, but otherwise it is not a good idea, as you should generally oppose his sword with your sword.

[36] *When I am crossed with someone and come to the narrow,
I strike his testicles with my right foot.*

This play, where I strike you with a kick to the groin, is made to hurt you so much that your cover will falter. When you make this play you should do it quickly, to prevent your opponent from being able to counter it.

The counter to this play must be done quickly, and is made by the player grabbing the student’s right leg with his left hand, and then throwing him to the ground.

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

This play is called the Villain's Strike, and is made in this way: that is, that one should await the villain in this way until he strikes with his sword. And he who awaits the blow should stand in a small stance with the left foot forward. And in that moment when the villain attacks to harm you, advance your left foot out of the way against the right side. And with your right foot step out of the way to the side, catching his blow at the mid-sword and allowing his sword to run off toward the ground, and then quickly respond with a downward blow (through the head or through the arms) or with your point in the chest as depicted here, this is also good.

[In the Morgan, the Master is missing his crown.]

This is the Villain's Strike which appeared here before me, so that I have put my point into his chest well. And so I could [also] have made a cut through the head and through the arms with a downward blow as was said before. Also, if the player wanted to come against me such that he would strike me with a backhand blow under my arms, I would immediately advance my left foot and thrust my sword over his, and then he cannot do anything to me.

When one strikes for your leg, withdraw the foot which is forward or return it behind, and throw a downward blow to his head as depicted here. Note that the sword in two hands should not attack from the knee down, because the danger to he that attacks is too great. He that attacks for the leg remains wholly uncovered, unless he would drop to the ground—then he could strike the leg well, but otherwise [he could] not when fighting sword to sword.

In this match I strike you with my foot in your testicles, and I do it to give you pain and to make your cover waver. Thus, in making this play I want to do it suddenly so that the counter is doubtful.

The counter of this play wants to be made quickly, such that the player should catch the Scholar by the right leg with his left hand, and then he can throw him to the ground.

### Notes

- Four combat pairs showing two-handed sword plays.
- Page numbered "28" (old foliation) in upper right.
- Top-left describes the "colpo de villano" (peasant's blow) - a powerful descending cut.
- The figures show engaged pairs with swords crossing at various points.
- Some figures wear golden arm bands/garters indicating student (scholar) status.
- Text is particularly dense and difficult to decipher on this page.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[37] *This is a cruel exchange of thrusts:
In the art, a more deceptive thrust than this cannot be made.
You attacked me with the point and I have given you this;
And I can make more secure it by voiding out of the way.*

This play is named “The Exchange of Points”, and it is done like this: when your opponent thrusts at you, quickly advance your front foot off the line, and with the other foot step to the side, also moving off the line, crossing his sword with your hands low and with your point high into his face, or chest, as you see drawn here.

[38] *Because of your hilt, which I hold in my hand,
I will make you bleed with my point in your face.*

This play comes from the exchange of points that came before me. If you make the thrust, and your opponent fails to immediately position his point either into your face or into your chest, perhaps because you are in armor, then you should quickly pass forward with your left foot, and seize his sword as shown here. Then strike him hard with your sword, since you have his sword gripped and he cannot escape.

[39] *Here we stand crossed near the ground:
And more knowledge of plays will be given.*

This is another defense you can make against the thrust. When someone thrusts at you as described in the “Exchange of Thrusts”, two plays before me, then you must advance and step off the line. You should do the same thing in this play, except that in the “Exchange of Points” you thrust back with your hands low and your point high, as I explained earlier. But in this play, which is named “Breaking the Thrust”, you proceed with your hands high and as you advances and step off the line you strike downwards, crossing the opponent’s thrust at mid-sword, and driving it to the ground. Then you quickly close to grapple.

[40] *I beat your point to the ground very quickly
And in this way, I strike you without a doubt.*

The student who preceded me beat his opponent’s sword to the ground. Now I am going to complete his play, as follows: after I beat my opponent’s sword to the ground I stomp on it with my right foot. This will either break it or prevent him from being able to lift it. But wait—there’s more. As soon as I have pinned his sword to the ground with my foot, I strike him with the false edge of my sword under his beard or into his neck. And then immediately I will return with a downward strike of my sword to his arms or his hands, as you see drawn here.

[In the Pisani Dossi, the Scholar stomps with his left foot and his opponent's right foot is forward; the Scholar's opponent is also left-handed.]

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

If, suddenly, we turn our sword by means of the play,
Thus we have the strength to injure the head using the palm during the play.

This play, which is called the Exchange of Thrusts, is made in this way: that is, that when he attacks with the point, quickly advance your forward foot out of the way and with your other foot step to the side (also out of the way), crossing his sword with your arms low and with the point of your sword up in his face or in his chest, as is depicted here.

Although you hold me with hands, anything is overthrown. I would
Strike you in this way, [your] dripping face having been split with the point.

From this exchange of thrusts that came before me comes this play. Given that the Scholar who came before me did not immediately thrust his point into the face of the player, or that he failed such that he could not thrust into [the player's] face nor into his chest, or that the player was armored, then immediately the Scholar should step with his left foot forward, and he should grab [the player] in this manner, and his sword should throw a good strike because the player has his sword caught and he cannot flee.

We remain in the form of the cross now in this playing.
The knowledgeable one will always have more conquering plays.

[The Paris resembles the Pisani Dossi image.]

Now your wicked hand would suddenly drag the point through the 
Earth. Henceforth, I would strike you immediately with a high wound.

### Notes

- Bottom figures show figures with gold arm bands (scholars/students).
- Text heavily references "frontale" (frontal) techniques throughout.
- The handwriting on this page is particularly cramped and difficult to parse.

### Translation — Claude Vision

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[41] *From the crossing at the ground which the Scholar makes
I come to cut your face because of my swiftness;
And your sword will end up bent or broken
And it will no more be able to work or deal.*

Here is another drawing of the “Breaking the Thrust” play, that you saw first two drawings previously. After I have beaten his sword to the ground I quickly pin it to the ground with my right foot, and then strike him in the head, as you see shown here.

[In the Pisani Dossi, the Scholar stomps with his left foot and his opponent's right foot is forward.]

[42] *From the play that came before, I enter into this one:
I make it quickly and cut your face.*

This is another play that flows from the “Breaking of the Thrust” play. After I break his thrust, if he raises his sword to cover as I strike upwards, I quickly drop the hilt of my sword inside his right arm, near his right hand, then I grab my blade near the point with my left hand, and then strike him in his face. Or alternatively, if I chose, I could drive my sword edge into his neck, slicing him across his throat.

[43] *I will make you turn by pinching your elbow
And with that, I will strike you without any delay.*

Also, after I have beaten aside or crossed my opponent’s sword, I can press my left hand to his right elbow and push strongly. This will turn him and leave him unprotected, after which I can strike him.

[44] *Because of the turn that I have given you by the elbow
I have quickly struck your head from behind.*

The student who preceded me spoke truly when he told you that he could turn the opponent and cut to his head. In addition, before you could turn back to make cover I would give you a major wound in your back with the point of my sword.

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

So, of course, quickly I would tear open your face by means of this action.
The student teaches [that] by means of this cross[ing], the leading sword [was] covered
By the ground. But your sword will depart either bent
Or broken, and never will you be able to wield that sword .

### Notes

- Four combat pairs showing two-handed sword plays.
- Page numbered "29" in the upper right (old foliation).
- Top-left and top-right show plays involving the breaking of thrusts (romper de ponta).
- Bottom figures show follow-up techniques after the initial exchange.
- All figures are unarmored, wielding longswords.
- The crowned figures (masters) appear to be demonstrating successful techniques against scholars.

---

## Folio 27v
**Section:** Wide Plays

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top-left:
¶ Che nostro zogo Rebatuda, punta falza e punta curva. ¶ E lo dire como le faze, lo ma[?] esto in le volte restare e cossa ella pe volta non mandar[e]. ¶ E lo falo[?] ella la contremessa, e sopra delle foy Coscha le foy ponta morte. ¶ E lo faro detto la spada mia si ella la mano dreta. El primo de movir[e] Frontale la golla e la ella la gamba, lo magistro questo zogo in arme che forza.

Top-right:
¶ Mastro ella la contremesa del coszo che me donanty de ponta falza contra de pontta curva. ¶ E lo per la mia possta, ella la cabeza drit[t]a e la ella per le foy grossa. ¶ E lo destro ella la mia contremeza e la ponta grossa le la contremeza. ¶ De lo Rebatitura. ¶ E lo per possta e io ponto la contraria la e lo che a e piaza. ¶ E lo volter. ¶ Ella questa contremeza l'arme e la piaza.

Bottom-left:
¶ Denanti finesse zogo longo della spada, a doy mane, che per sova zogo vero chi mane con zogo le per veza contremessa, de per ve breve, e lo per volta, e di turmenta e divercament[e], e di tutta che se queste contremess[e] fovero e lo quandra e che tute le grossa[?] frontale menbar[e] el per ponto.

Bottom-right:
¶ Denanti comenza zogo de spade a doy mane, gostilisemo e bello e de foy lo per spada. ¶ E lo fiore vore e lo se breve de posta. ¶ E lo per la pontta di modo e contremeza de per lo forza. ¶ E la perforante raptore[?] ella le frontale a offendere e la deffendere, ello la compania ella lo Rebatimento ella le forza contra e lo frontale contra, la de lo contremeza la cossa de per lo posta contremeza ella spada.

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
That our play the rebatting, false thrust and curved thrust. And to say how to make the face, this in the turns to remain and thing and the foot turn not to send. And the feint and the counter-measure, and above the thigh was the death point. And I shall make said my sword and the right hand. The first to move the Frontal the throat and the leg, the master this play in arms that is force.

Top-right:
Master and the counter-measure of the blow that you give me of false thrust against curved thrust. And through my guard, the right head and through the thick. And the right and my counter-measure and the thick point the counter-measure. Of the Rebatting. And through the guard and I point the contrary and that which pleases. And the turning. And this counter-measure the arms and the plaza.

Bottom-left:
Before this finishes the long play of the sword, with two hands, that through his true play who the hand with play sees the counter-measure, through the short, and through the turn, and of torment and diversely, and of all that these counter-measures were and the square and that all the thick frontal remember the point.

Bottom-right:
Before this begins the play of the two-handed sword, most just and beautiful and of the sword. And the flower wishes and the short of the guard. And through the thrust in manner and counter-measure through force. And the perforating raptor and the frontal to offend and to defend, and the company and the rebatting and the force against and the frontal against, of the counter-measure the thing of the guard counter-measure and sword.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[45] *I appear to come from the right, but I enter on the left
To give you this thrust with great pain and harm;
I make myself called Deceitful Thrust by name;
And I am so cruel as I exchange the point of the sword.*

This play is named “The False Point” or “The Short Point”, and I will explain how to do it. I make it look like I am making a powerful attack against my opponent with a crosswise strike to his head. As he makes cover I strike his sword but only lightly. Then I quickly turn my sword to the other side of his blade, gripping my sword with my left hand at about mid-sword. From there I can quickly make a thrust into his throat or chest. This play is however better in armor than without armor.

[The Getty resembles the Pisani Dossi image.]

[46] *To the Deceitful Thrust that you wanted to strike at me,
I have struck the counter by turning myself and my sword 
Such that I have positioned my point in your face,
In this way I have removed all of your plays.*

This play is the counter to the previous play, the False Point or the Short Point. And this counter is made like as follows: when the student strikes my sword lightly and then turns his sword around to the other side, I turn my sword around his in exactly the same way, stepping sideways to the left as I do so to gain his unprotected side. From here I can make a thrust into his face. And this counter is good both with or without armor.

[47] Here ends the Wide Play of the sword in two hands, made up of plays that are all connected to each other, including remedies and counters from both the right and left sides, and counter-thrusts and counter-cuts to each situation, with breaks, covers, strikes and locks, all things that can be easily understood.

[48] Here we begin the Narrow Play of the two handed sword, in which you will see all manner of covers, strikes, locks, dislocations, sword disarms and throws to the ground. There will also be the remedies and the counters needed for each situation, whether you are attacking or defending.

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

I steal in on [you] in the sly part from the honest part;
Therefore, you will quit this sorrowful life by means of the point.

Here begins the play of the sword in two hands, the narrow play, the method of breaking all thrusts and cuts, in the which will be every method of covering, striking, and binding, and dislocations, and grapples, and takings of the sword, and beating to the ground in diverse ways. And there will be remedies and counters of every category that should offend or defend.

### Notes

- Four combat pairs showing two-handed sword plays.
- Top section deals with "rebatuda" (rebatting/parrying) techniques including punta falza (false thrust) and punta curva (curved thrust).
- Bottom-left text explicitly mentions the end of the "zogo longo della spada" (long play of the sword).
- Bottom-right text introduces a new play section described as "gostilisemo e bello" (most just and beautiful).
- The transition between long sword play and a new section is notable on this page.

### Translation — Claude Vision

Again I who wish to cover, and of the master's manner through and the most [plasters?] ours that above through his payment to me and that to go guards to come and of the guards and all of the great of the flowers, and Roses so those who pay good of the three count lady and flower.

Of so the masters, who made the hands, and through crossing the body leg that. ### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[49] *Because of the way that we stand here crossed,
The play is given to whoever knows more and is swifter:
But since many plays are made from such a crossing,
We will only be making the strongest counters.*

We stand with crossed swords, and from this crossing either one of us can make all of the plays that follow. And as I told you earlier, these plays will follow one after the other.

[In the Pisani Dossi, the Scholar is wearing a crown.]

[50] *Because of your hilt which I hold in my hand,
I will strike you and your sword will be pledged to me.
 *

Using the crossing my Master made with his right foot forward, I now complete the first play as follows: I pass forward with my left foot, and I reach over my right arm with my left hand, seizing his sword-grip in the middle, between his hands. And from here I can strike him with either my edge or my point. This grip can be made when fighting with the two-handed sword or the one-handed sword. And I can make this grip by reaching either under or over the crossed swords.

[51] *Because my sword has received a blow
And because of this catch, my pommel strikes you in the face.*

This is another play that flows from the crossing of my Master. And from that crossing I can make this play and all of the others that follow. In this play I grip my opponent at the elbow as shown, and then strike him in the face with the pommel of my sword. After that I can also strike him in the head with a downward strike before he has a chance to make cover against me.

[52] *This is another strike with my pommel,
Following the quickness of the art and the Masters.*

This is another pommel strike, which is effective against a man with or without armor. Make this strike quickly if his face is unprotected, and you will certainly hurt him. I can tell you from experience that with this strike you’ll have him spitting out four teeth. From here, if you wish, you can also throw your sword around his neck, as my fellow student will show you next.

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

We stand here crossed and from this crossing that we make, all the plays that follow us can be made, and by one of us as easily as the other. And all of these plays will follow, one after the other, as was previously said.

[In the Morgan, the Scholar is wearing a crown.]

I would strike, and I will hold your sword; restrained by no
Pledge, you conduct yourself so disgracefully
By laws holding me, [which] pierced, you will now die.

From the crossing that the Master has made with his right foot forward, I complete the first play—that is, I step with my left foot and I pass my left hand over my [right] arm and grasp the hilt of his sword in between his hands (in the middle of the hilt), and then I could strike with edge and point. And this catch can be made as easily with the sword in one hand as with the sword in two hands, and this catch can be made as easily crossing under the hands as over.

[In the Paris, the Scholar's sword is in front of his arm.]

I strike to your face using this hilt, obviously ferocious.
This because you had knocked the sword using the deepest touch.

This is another play that comes from the crossing of my Master, and as he is crossed, he can make this play and the others that follow after—that is, he can make or grasp the player in this way to strike him in the face with the pommel of his sword. Also, he can strike him in the head with a downward blow before [the player] could make a cover ready.

This second blow is striking the companion in return using the hilt,
While yet in this place [both] the art and master himself would be speeds.

This is another strike of the pommel and it can be done quickly; if his face is uncovered then do it without fear, because it may be done armored or unarmored. You will bore four teeth from his mouth with this play (as has been proven), and if you wanted, you could throw the sword to his neck as does the Scholar who is after me.

### Notes

- Four combat pairs showing two-handed sword plays.
- Page numbered "30" in upper right (old foliation).
- Top figures show masters (crowned) engaging with students.
- Bottom figures show close-range techniques including what appears to be grappling with swords.
- Text is very difficult to read throughout this page; many readings are uncertain.
- The yellow/gold arm bands on some figures mark them as scholars.

## Folio 28v
**Section:** Close Plays

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top-left:
¶ Ello lo zogo che me domanty, e cossa le podare[?] e longo e lo per lo fiy la spada, al ostro. ¶ Ella cossa per che me ponta la spada al cosza e che e lo possta lo podare. ¶ E per le volta e de per la volta lo per le grande[?] de per lo volter[e].

Top-right:
¶ Quando la per me conbare de per le posste e alchone[?] per le per el per el per ello prima e ponta[?]. ¶ Ella pero e grossa per e la per che ella per la per grandere[?] e la Porte[?] priminendar[e] le batayre[?] la batayre[?]. ¶ E le cosza de la per posste che le per la lo batayre la cosza, e le fiore la magistra.

Bottom-left:
¶ Che possta che dette le podare che me donanty, questa ella e lo foy. ¶ Drevo mane ella la possta drita impente. ¶ E le lo batayr ella le Fronte le cosza e la le forza[?] ella la cosza le mane ella la de lo posste e per le posste per e lo per postte[?] contremeza possta. ¶ E lo per che le posste possta deffesa fiochare[?] cosza elle le posste fronte.

¶ Questa lo foy marcapede e la soper[?] e cosza fiancho, ¶ e Che la per le mane posst[e] della la par e la pontta[?] maner[e] cosza[?]. ¶ E lo per che e lo per la mane cosza. ¶ Che la mane forte e pontta per le per la lo per e le pontta. ¶ E lo per mane batayre grande che delle la fior posste, le le la per posste e cosza le per batayre.

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
And the play that you ask me, and the thing the pruning and long and through the sword, to the other. And the thing through which the sword is thrust at the thigh and that is the guard the pruning. And through the turn and through the turn through the great of turning.

Top-right:
When through me to fight through the guards and some through the first and point. And yet thick through and through that she through the great and the gates commanding the beating the beating. And the thing of through the guards that through the beating the thing, and the flower the mistress.

Bottom-left:
That guard that said the pruning that you give me, this and it was. Right hand and the right guard pushed. And the beating and the front the thing and the force and the thing the hand and of the guards and through the guards and counter-measure guard. And through the guard the guard defense weakening the thing and the frontal guards.

Bottom-right:
This was the foot-mark and the above and the thing flank, and that through the hands of the guards of the pair and the point manner thing. And through the hand thing. That the strong hand and thrust through and the thrust. And through the hand great beating that of the flower guards, and the guards and the thing and beating.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[53] *I send you to the ground in this match;
I have not failed to thrust my sword to your neck.*

As the student who preceded me told you, after doing the previous play I now put the sword-edge into your neck. And from here, if I discover that you have no neck armor, I will easily cut your throat.

[63] When I am crossed I move to the Narrow Game, and I place the hilt of my sword between your two hands. Then I push your two hands upwards so that your sword is high. From here I throw my left arm over your arms from the left, binding them with your sword pinned under my left arm. Then I will strike multiple times until I am exhausted. The student who follows me will show you what happens next.

[67] *I have hindered your sword with my arm,
And I have fixed the point of mine in your face:
And I counter the takings of the sword
And all the various other narrow plays.*

When I am crossed, I pass with a cover, and I injure you in your arms in this part. And this thrust you get in the face. And if I advance the left foot, both your arms will be bound. Or, that in the other play after me of grabbing you, you are bound at the sword by the hilt's retention.

[68] *I have trapped your sword by the hilt,
And I will strike you a great bargain with edge and point:
Also, I am the counter to the sword in the raised hand;
I can strike you and you cannot touch me.*

This is the grip that the student before me said to do to you. I can injure you without danger. I retain your sword's hilt, I will give you cuts and thrusts cheaply (with no risk?). And this play breaks all sword-disarms, and doing it immediately spoils the narrow play.

[In the Getty, the Master is missing his crown.]

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

I, the great one, throw you to the ground, you anticipating something,
I am not cheating to put the sword to your neck using this action.

From the play that came before me and as the Scholar has said, I have placed my sword at your neck and I could cut your throat well because I feel that you do not have an armored collar.

[The Morgan and Paris resemble the Getty, but the Scholar's right foot is inside of (behind) his opponent's right foot.]

From the crossed swords I pass with cover and bind your arms as shown, then I thrust my sword into your face. And if I advance the left foot forward I can bind both your arms. Or alternately I can do the play shown next, where I bind your sword by gripping your cross-guard.

[In the Paris, the Master is missing his crown. In the Morgan and Paris, the Master has his hand in position but hasn't grabbed his opponent's arm.]

I, the very strong, seize your sword in the cross[ing].
From here I would pierce you, already gloomy, with the 
Cutting point. And I am called “counter of the sword”, raising
The hand higher. And I prevail to strike a bargain openly with your limb.
You will not be able to touch the sword with any violations.

Here I am making the bind that the previous student told you about, and from this position I can strike you with impunity. I have your sword bound by its cross-guard, and from here I can strike you with both cuts and thrusts. In addition, if done quickly this play can defeat all attempts to take my sword, and if it is done quickly enough it will defeat the opponent’s Narrow Game.

[In the Paris, the Master is missing his crown.]

### Notes

- Four combat pairs showing two-handed sword plays.
- Top-left and top-right show standing sword engagements with swords crossed.
- Bottom-left shows what appears to be a disarm or bind technique at close range.
- Bottom-right shows a grappling/close-quarters exchange where one figure appears to be striking or controlling the other at close range.
- This page continues the "zogo de spada a doy mane" (two-handed sword plays) section.
- Text is extremely difficult to read; this page has some of the most uncertain readings in this batch.
- Gold arm bands visible on scholar figures throughout.

## Folio 29r
**Section:** Close Plays

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top-left:
¶ Del zogho del Scholaro che me domanda su sinistra parte.
Quello che tu ay in posta breve la serpentina. Lo scolaro
te vegna co lançe. Io lo passo contra. Guarda
che li fazi col pe sinistro. Guarda la vera manera che per
le Fior Fioretto.

Top-right:
¶ Del zogho che domanda si fo in questo zogho che
mesurava lo scolaro e doy Poste la cinquedea respondo
de fendoy e de le poche ben farate. E per fior bracciadura
de la tua Posta che ben el colpo a metter i quattro par-
te. Che el simile fazi de la poste per la manere.

Bottom-left:
¶ Soy uno Scholaro de la posta mandritto. per lo ala del
fior suo Fioretto che de la presa streta. che lo foy Fior
game de la fior posta. eprimay en breve. per foy lo
simptado hoy le poy fiore.

Bottom-right:
¶ Soy uno Scholaro de la posta d'aviso. posta. chi la
posta mesurava de la fior posta. a quatro mano. e d'aviso
lo posso aver de le mane. e de manto te raxo de la
fior Posta. Guardo entro la volta e la formay che segura.
Fandome quello io so ben Scholaro poste abbandonar. lo
mano posta. e queste la vera. ghe sia de la postone
chi era de dredo.

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
Of the play of the Scholar who asks me on the left side. You who are in the short serpentine guard. The scholar comes at you with the lance. I pass against him. See that you do it with the left foot. See the true manner for the Flower of the art.

Top-right:
Of the play that asks — so it was in this play that I measured the scholar and two Guards respond from the cinquedea, of the blows and the few well-struck. And through the arm-lock of your Guard, the blow lands well to four sides. Do the same from the guards in this manner.

Bottom-left:
I am a Scholar of the forehand guard. For the wing of his art from the close grip. What was the art's guard. And first in short. And so the art followed from the guard.

Bottom-right:
I am a Scholar of the alert guard. The guard measured from the art's guard. With four hands. And alert I can seize the hands. And I reason from the art's Guard. I enter the turn and the form that is secure. Making that which I know well as a Scholar, abandoning the guards. The hands guard. And these are the true ones, from the rear guard.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[56] If he covers from his right side, seize his sword with your left hand as shown and strike him with a thrust or a cut. Then after striking him hard, if you wish, you can drop your own sword and cut his face or neck with his own sword, in the manner shown by the student in the next picture.

[61] If he covers from his left side, grab his left hand including his pommel with your left hand, and pull it upwards and backwards. From there you will be able to strike him with thrusts and cuts.

[64] *I enclosed your arms with my left arm,
And this play is better armored than unarmored:
Also, I counter the taking of the sword,
According to where Master Fiore put me.*

The student who is before me has completed the play which I said to do. Your arms have been bound in ligadura mezana (middle bind). Your sword is prisoner, and it can not help you. And with mine I can cause you a lot of injuries. Without doubt I can put my sword to your neck. I can immediately do the play which is after me.

[65] This play follows on from the previous one, where the student struck his opponent multiple times while using his left arm to keep the opponent’s arms and sword pinned. Now I throw my sword at my opponent’s neck as depicted. Then I throw him to the ground to complete the play.

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

This is another method of throwing someone to the ground, and it is done in this fashion: the Scholar crosses with the player on the right side and comes to the narrow. He grasps the sword of the player with his left hand (passing the middle of the sword), …

[Text accompanies subsequent image.]

### Notes

Four combat pairs showing sword plays (likely two-handed sword / spada a due mani). Each quadrant shows two combatants engaged. The page number "31" appears in the upper-right corner. The text is densely written in Venetian dialect and somewhat difficult to read due to the manuscript hand. Some readings are uncertain due to faded ink and cursive abbreviations.

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
Of the counter of the Scholar from my Scholar in the forehand cover that is your guard. The turning half-blow in the thick. Well I show you that we do no less.

Top-right:
In this play I draw from the play of the short. For the great remedy of the art. That you extend to the manner, making the arm-lock turning. And reversed. The art that greatly guards the manner. Rise from the blow. Stop the blow.
Of the counter-play that from the short. The forehand cover of the brave short barbarian. That I have well from two, this art.

Bottom-left:
I make the contrary — who acts against the scholar that I counter the forehand, that was covered in time. For that which is to me the grip, making the play in this from the ground. This is well a remedy indeed. For all of the grip.

Bottom-right:
For the firm counter. We of the player together in pair. In the sixth. The reason the wise one makes to enter this play, to make secure. That also the play of which then this. Against the quick barbarian. Against such, this art.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[57] *I have in hand the catch that I have sought with you
For throwing you to the ground with your sword.*

Following on from the play of the student before me, I cut my opponent’s face with his own sword, then force him to the ground. Here I am demonstrating just how effective this art really is.

[69] This play is taken from the first play of the First Dagger Remedy Master, who places his left hand over the opponent’s wrist to take the dagger from his hand. In similar fashion the student here places his left hand over the opponent’s right forearm, rotating it outwards to remove the sword from his right hand…

[72] *You wanted to enclose my sword under your arm
But the counter brings you to an evil end here.*

I am the counter to the student who preceded me, if he tries to use the second play of the First Dagger Remedy Master against me that you heard about previously, and this is how I am done. And when I do this play I doubt you will be able to remain on your feet holding your sword.

[73] I am also a counter to the student who tried to use the second play of the First Dagger Remedy Master against me. From the previous picture, if I now start to cut into his throat, he will stand up a little, and then if I move quickly, I can throw him backwards to the ground.

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

I undertake with my hands the special taking for a
Long time, so that I am able to pitch you, miserable one, into the earth.

…and immediately he throws his sword to the ground and thrusts the player's own sword to his neck, grasping the hilt in the middle—that is, between the hands of the player—with his right foot behind [the player's] right. And in this fashion he throws him to the ground with his own sword.

Using the sword to strike back, I become acquainted with your very own shoulder,
Or I would pierce you, or even at once I would confine this arms.

You wanted to confine the sword under your very own deceptive
Shoulder. I am the counter, and this overthrows you to the greatest extent.

### Notes

Four combat pairs continuing the two-handed sword section. The bottom-left illustration shows one combatant being thrown to the ground (a wrestling/grappling technique from the sword). The bottom-right shows close-quarters grappling with swords. Text is heavily abbreviated and difficult to read; several readings are approximate. The script shows typical features of the Venetian chancery hand.

## Folio 30r
**Section:** Close Plays

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top-left:
¶ Soy una coverta de la persona. De una Ochanza
per questo presto. le che vi al cor de spada ver al
lato Riverso. aloy un aco offeso. Ancora le posso
pigliar de le presa de le mane presto passando.

Top-right:
¶ Questo Scolaro se fè de tal modo pare. che una mano
cum l'altra bechando. cercha la mczana de la garada
de lo scolaro. e chossi. In certo la spada
al collo del compagnone como qui e depento. Duncha io po-
mo strenzi senza pelliçamente.

Bottom-left:
¶ Questo al con de spada. la primerara et lo medesmo
de una spada. im posta manere. e poi la mia manconeza
che a el formazo ali per longo. grande posta commizara-
bia. lo scolaro che me drede aquesto. Segne una
presa contra la guida del segnalare. e tuti soy al
posta.

Bottom-right:
¶ Per la presta del Scolaro che domanda. ven a fiatem
la spada. mi trovo in cuberto. com la foy pandara. al più
Presto ho presto faze Orsa meandrare.

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
I am a cover of the person. From an opportunity with this quick one. That strikes to the heart with the sword or to the left side. I have not been struck. Also I can seize with the hand grips, passing quickly.

Top-right:
This Scholar did it in this manner. That one hand with the other hooking. He seeks the middle of the guard of the scholar. And thus. Certainly the sword at the neck of the companion as depicted here. Therefore I can press without [danger].

Bottom-left:
This at the pommel of the sword. The first and the same with one sword. In the guard manner. And then my left hand at the form long. The great guard begins. The scholar who follows me in this. Makes a grip against the guide of the signal. And all are in guard.

Bottom-right:
For the readiness of the Scholar who asks. He comes to strike with the sword. I find myself covered. With the parry. The quickest I quickly make to turn back.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[55] 

This play is performed as follows: against a crosswise strike from his left, you meet it with a crosswise strike of your own from your left. Then you quickly move to close range under cover, and then throw your sword around your opponent’s neck, as you see drawn here. From here you can easily throw him to the ground.

[In the Pisani Dossi, the Scholar's right foot is outside (in front) of his opponent's right foot.]

[62] *In this fashion I have bound you so well
That you would be trapped in armor or without:
And your sword will be useless against me;
I make this counter of the taking of the sword with certainty.*

If I am crossed at Narrow Play with someone, I can quickly make this move to prevent him from attacking me by taking my sword, or a lock.

[75] *This taking of the sword is called Above;
Which was made a thousand times and more by Fiore Furlano.*

This is a high sword disarm. With my left hand I pin his hands, while at the same time I press forwards against his blade with the grip of my sword so that he loses his grip on his sword. Then I will deal him several good strikes. The student who comes after me will show how this play finishes with the opponent’s sword lying on the ground.

[76] Following the disarm performed by the student who came before me, you will feel your sword fall to the ground. And now there is no question as to whether I can strike you.

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

During a similar play, we bring you down into the deep earth.
I will accomplish this also; nevertheless I myself &lt;I&gt; remain on my feet.

This is another catch to throw someone to the ground, sword and all—that is, that this Scholar crosses with the player on the right side and steps into the narrow; he pinches the right elbow of the player with his left hand, and then quickly he throws his sword to [the player's] neck, grasping his own sword at the middle (his right foot behind the right of the player). In this way, he throws [the player] to the ground with little honor.

This is another counter against the taking of the sword, and it can be made readily and quickly in this fashion. When one comes to cross with you and steps or advances close with his left foot forward, then grasp his hand at the wrist with your left hand (from under his hilt) and turn his sword toward your left side in such fashion as is depicted here. And thus you strike him in the chest, or in the belly, or wherever he likes least.

That movement by which I rob the man during the playing with the sword
Is called by nearly all “the high on the right” in close fighting weapons,
Which I, Florius, myself demonstrated by many exchanges.

This is the taking of the sword from above: with the hilt of my sword I push forward and with my left hand I grasp his arms in such a way that it would serve him well to lose his sword. And then the Scholar who is after will strike a bargain with great strikes. This play he demonstrates as the sword of the player is positioned on the ground.

[In the Paris, the Scholar reaches in front of his opponent's sword, and his foot is outside of (behind) his opponent's.]

Because of the catch of the Scholar who came before me which I have made, your sword has fallen to the ground. You can feel that I could thus make you truly wounded.

### Notes

Page number "32" appears in the upper-right corner. Four combat pairs showing sword techniques with close-range work including pommel strikes, arm locks, and blade-at-throat techniques. The top-right pair shows a sword placed against the opponent's neck. Several red initial letters are visible (decorated paragraph markers). Some words remain uncertain due to scribal abbreviations and ink fading.

## Folio 30v
**Section:** Close Plays

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top-left:
¶ Ay questo è meçano tor de spada. ay la foy fare.
Tal scolaro to spada e sey in quarto qual al fior
Scolaro chi la poy del foy aquello.

Top-right:
¶ Questo a maistro tor de spada. domando de Pozarma
per Scolaro miser. Si trdo questo. como fio la persona al falso
per posta rotto. e de sustamento. lo scolaro voltare.
contra. Che la mano Voltare. e responder farono eno soldo
a la manere. Che voltar fran de che
seguire.

Bottom-center:
¶ Tal scolaro essi fiamo tor de spada. che quando
ala di Fioreto maistrado. lo scolaro ho lo maistro. e
la foy mano do mano. e doy le foy de mesonata e
seder. e fisar. lassar la foy averte al gazo doy
mano Scolaro le presto la foy scolaro metere. chi la
gran fazare. e donqua. la volta faneta como iovnezare.
e fisar lo coyntello mano la foy spada porta.

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
In this is the middle disarm of the sword. At the art's making. This scholar takes the sword and is in the fourth [position] which is at the art's Scholar who can do this.

Top-right:
This is a master disarm of the sword. I ask of the armor-bearer Scholar sir. This third one. How the person at the false [edge] through the broken guard. And in support. The scholar turning against. That the hand turns. And they respond together in the manner. That he turns freely to follow.

Bottom-center:
This scholar and we are at the sword disarm. That when at the mastered art. The scholar or the master. And the hand from hand. And two of the half-blow and sit. And fix. Leave the art open at the gaze, two-handed Scholar, the quick art scholar to put. Who makes greatly. And therefore. The turn made as to play. And fix the dagger hand to the sword's port.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[77] *Here I make the taking of the sword in the middle,
And I will give you grief with my sword or yours.
 *

This is how you do the middle sword disarm. The rotation of the opponent’s sword is the same as in the first disarm, but the grip on his arm is not the same.

[In the Getty, the Scholar's left foot is outside (in front) of his opponent's.]

[78] *This is the taking of the sword from below:
It will be made well by whoever is a gifted Master in the art.*

This is another sword disarm, named the low disarm. The low disarm is performed in a similar way to the high disarm, with the same rotation of the opponent’s sword, following the same path. With your right hand you press his blade forwards, making his sword handle rotate upwards, and you must keep your left hand on his handle as it turns.

[79] *I take this sword for my own:
I will do you villainy with a rotation and a taking.
 *

Here’s another way you can take his sword if you are crossed at close range: put your right hand above his and grab his sword at mid-blade keeping it upright, then immediately drop your sword to the ground. Now with your left hand you grab your opponent’s sword under the pommel, and turn it to his left. Then immediately your opponent will be forced to release his sword.

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

I immediately execute the “middle sword taking” 
Blow, pressing your limbs using a raging sword,
Either with my own, or by chance yours, which you rely on to be present.

This is the taking of the sword from the middle: whoever knows how to make such turnings of the sword makes this one just as the first is made, save only that the catch is not the same. The first taking of the sword binds both of the arms, but I do not want to have such trouble so I separate one arm and hand from the other. He is not so strong that he could hold [his sword] and keep it from falling. As was said above, I am the taking of the sword from the middle, which was made a thousand times by Fiore Furlano.

[In the Morgan, the Scholar's left foot is outside (in front) of his opponent's.]

The sharp sword is seized thus in the lower position,
Because a skilled person in this art would make anything endure.

This is another taking of the sword, called Below: make this one in the same way as you do those from above and from [the middle]—that is, with a turn of the sword. This one follows the way of the others, with the right hand carrying forward a full rotation with the hilt, and the left hand should follow with a full turn.

I consider the sword to be mine, which you certainly see.
And by means of turning, I would certainly provide shame for you.
And also I would draw back using my very own hands, unless the fates disagree.

This is another taking of the sword, and it is done in this way: that when one is crossed in the narrow, the Scholar should thrust his right hand under his [sword] and grasp that of the player at the middle or above, immediately releasing his own sword to hit the ground, and with his left hand he should grasp under the pommel of the player's sword and give it a full rotation to the right, and then suddenly the player will have his sword lost and the Scholar, righting [the stolen] sword with a half turn, can strike the player.

### Notes

This page has only three combat pairs instead of four — two in the upper register and one centered in the lower register. All three show disarming techniques (tor de spada / taking of the sword). The bottom section has a longer text block. The page relates to the "tor di spada" (sword disarms) section of the treatise. Some readings uncertain due to damage and heavy abbreviation.

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
This art is a company of the scholar, to two this master. And this of the great other master. He passes openly. He throws then wide. Of the master. The person of the art the valor advances two. That to two returns. I am the valor, the tongue well positioned, mad in passing. As one disperses.

Top-right:
The Joined one comes quick in guard. That in honor of the companion. And a great [action]. Guard against which one can be in the middle art guard and flower. And I command the two of this question. Scholar of much manner in straightness to make in honor in drawing. For all through the valor — I am at the hard and the scholars' punches. Against which I can through the mind — all beautiful of his lance-art. The art of two to give the covered good, perhaps the guard. And the flower-art is the [action]. O and this much art makes one of the art. This is the contrary Scholar art of the flower.

Bottom-left:
This master guards these two. And their lance this comes to me, the ready manner makes. And Scholar I color the guard against this. The left of companion and the wound. And the low one. Where strong of both that in the time the art is his lance. The stab wound from the spear tip. Toward the Scholar. With guarding who acts in the truth. The contrary the adversity of the art. With the show. And how the [blow] and the art are the poor and the lance, remaining willingly at the best and making. The spark of the art as finely. From these two the line.

Bottom-right:
Against my need. You slave and this master the setting of the arms my [guard]. That therefore my art is strong. Of the long [guard]. And of the art. And of measure and the windmill. The sword. Scholar I [act] Scholar the guard. I let the will finish Scholar long. The guard art thrown. It was at the strong art and the long. And this art.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[1] Here are three friends who seek to kill this Master, who is waiting for them with his two handed sword. The first intends to throw his sword at the Master like a spear. The second aims to strike him with a cut or a thrust. The third intends to throw two spears he has made ready, as you see drawn here.

| 
[2] I wait in this guard—the Boar’s Tusk—for these three to attack, but I could also wait in other guards, for example, left side Guard of the Lady or left side Window Guard, and with any of these I would be able to defend just as well as I can with the Boar’s Tusk. Each of these guards uses the same method of defense. I wait unperturbed for them to come at me one after the other, and my defense will not fail against cuts, thrusts, nor any handheld weapon they throw at me. I advance my right front foot off the line and with my left I step obliquely, beating the incoming weapon aside towards my opponent’s left side. And in this way I make my defense, first by making cover and then quickly counter attacking.

[11] *In this way I wait with the dagger and with the staff:
The staff will make a cover, the dagger will strike you in the chest.
And that which I do with a staff, I could also do with a sword,
Although I could find much stronger plays with the sword.*

This Master awaits these two men with spears. The Master, who's waiting with a staff and a dagger, sees that the first intends to attack with an overhand strike, while the second intends to strike underhand. In each case, before either opponent attacks with his spear, the Master tilts his staff to the right, like the guard Low Iron Gate, turning himself without moving his feet or lifting the staff off the ground. And so the Master waits in this guard. As either opponent attacks, the Master pushes the spear aside with his staff to the left, using his dagger too if needed. Following that cover, the Master steps and strikes. Both attackers with their spears will discover that he has a good defense.

| 
[12] We were both planning on striking this master, but as you heard we can no longer do that, unless we deceive him [when he blocks our strike] by rotating our spear so that the steel blade is at the rear and then striking him with the spear-butt. Then, if he blocks the butt-strike, we'll rotate our spear again and strike him on his other side with the steel blade. That's how we will counter him.

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

In the same way, holding fast to dagger and staff,
I delay you while the staff offers cover to me, and that
Dagger strikes the breast. Nevertheless, I drive out everything the sword would have accomplished, 
Using the staff. However, we can use the better
Plays herein easily, exercising the nimble shoulders.

For this play, which is of staff and dagger against a lance, the Scholar awaits him of the lance in the Full Iron Gate, turning his body but not moving his feet from the ground nor his staff toward the right side. And as he of the lance attacks the Scholar with his lance, the Scholar advances his left foot out of the way, beating and voiding the lance. And he steps close to him without delay and with the dagger he can strike him. This play he wants to make without fail.

### Notes

Page number "33" in upper-right corner. Four combat pairs continuing two-handed sword and possibly lance/pollaxe techniques. The text on this page is particularly dense and difficult to read. Both upper-right and bottom-left text blocks are unusually long compared to other pages. Many readings here are approximate due to the small, compressed handwriting and abbreviation marks.

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

posta ben chi nel longo. e maistro e Scolaro trono
questa longa. e doy terra lo Scolaro ala forta maistre
lo a foy foy in la Provincia. e sapi te mescre supra
ben maistro posse ben fare la questo ponta de la
forma. foy provesto. Ma chi facesse laso

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
This is the play of the master who was waiting. Guard well in the long [position]. And master and scholar find this long [guard]. And from two the ground — the Scholar at the strong master's. He acted in the Province. And know, sir, above — well the master can do the thrust of the form. The art was foreseen. But who would let go that the tight one, the lance we opened well for the truth. Thus the lance art we cannot heal. But we, sick, would pay for the art.

Top-right:
This master art of delight. Of this of the good [one]. He counts then the master's manner. That words — this Latin lance for the art of the wing. That of the Scholar. He draws with the goodness. The grip. In the guard of the lance. Of the striker. Of the four Scholars I have the good — the need. I love the art, the beasts, the art and the price of the need. To one art of art.

Bottom-left:
I make the grip of the master here who commands me. And the push with the art's guard. Reversed for the art. And I command and Scholar — unshoe the hands of the short long grip — the low one. And then much manner to make four arts. From this art I expect nothing of the art.

Bottom-right:
[Two standing figures without text]

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[13] *In that way that the previous Master has said,
I strike that one in the chest with the dagger.*

This is the play of the Master who waits for those two with spears. The Master has in his right hand a dagger, and he keeps a stave straight in front of him in his left hand. He can do the play in this way. And I do it for him in his place. But if this player knew how to do well, he could have avoided the dagger’s point. If he had lifted his hands with the spear, and with the rest of the spear which remains at the rear-end, he had covered under my dagger, which means crossing, it would not have happened to him. And he could have damaged me with his spear, if he knew how to do this counter to me.

[14] *I wait here with two sticks and a dagger:
I will throw the one at you and I will cover with the other, coming to the narrow,
And I will quickly strike you in the chest with my dagger.*

This Master defends with two cudgels against a spear, as follows: when the spear man approaches to attack, the Master with strikes at his opponent’s head with the cudgel in his right hand. Then he quickly strikes with the cudgel in his left hand so as to make cover against the spear, and then he strikes his opponent in the chest with his dagger, as is shown next.

[15] *That which the Master has said, so do I do,
I have placed the dagger in your chest without trouble.*

I show the play of the Master before me. If he had known the counter, he would have obstructed me as follows: he would have lifted my hands with his spear by rotating it under my dagger, and in that way he would have been able to obstruct me and destroy me. So take this, since you know nothing.

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

Here, I delay you with two clubs at the same time and also a dagger.
But I will throw the first [club], and I will hold the remaining
Covering limb in that place, when we will both press in.
From here I would strike you quickly, but with the dagger in the opening of the breast.

This deed having been done, [that] which the master now says will be told,
And however I affix this particular dagger in the breast.

The bottom-left pair shows a grappling/wrestling technique at close quarters.
