## Folio 32r
**Section:** Seven Swords

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top (header text across page):
¶ Ay questo magistro di questo guado. Sette son gli Sette colpi de la Spada. Elli son
quatro li Animali. Segno fon como. presteza. forteza. e Ardimento. Ello vole chi
queste arme commo de lor medesme piere.

Center-top (above the crown, between two swords):
¶ Aquello che noi breve commençare. sul colpo
Altitudo. Che poselle nostre parte pur de petto
e a mczana.

Left (by the lynx with the compass):
¶ Lo segno ramparo foy della.
La prudentia del modo che
una parme avançamente.

Left label:
Presteza.

Right (by the lion):
¶ Piu de mi breve chi va fella
porre avdire. Per lo la mesonata [?]
possa avançamento.

Right label:
Ardimento.

Bottom-left (by the elephant with the tower/castle):
¶ Elli efanduy foy qui
e sia chi me e voy comigno.

Bottom-left label:
Forteza.

Bottom-center (between swords at the waist):
Un chastel lo porto. gi chesuno
chi gha pote matare [?].

Bottom-right label:
[No separate bottom-right animal; the fourth animal — likely the tiger/wolf — may be integrated]

Sword labels (around the figure, seven swords pointing in various directions):
Aupis mcrdes.

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top (header):
In this master of this ford. Seven are the seven blows of the Sword. And four are the Animals. They signify thus: Quickness, Strength, and Boldness. And he wills that these arms be as their own stones [foundations].

Center-top:
That which we briefly commence. On the blow from above. That our parts place it at the chest and in the middle.

Left (Lynx / Prudence):
The rampart sign of the art. The prudence of the manner that one advances in defense.
**Quickness.**

Right (Lion / Boldness):
More of me shortly, who goes to place boldly. For the half [blow] can advance.
**Boldness.**

Bottom-left (Elephant / Strength):
And the elephant is here and let those who are with me be my companions.
**Strength.**

Bottom-center:
A castle I carry. That each one who can kill [?].

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

| 
This Master with these swords signifies the seven blows of the sword. And the four animals signify four virtues, that is prudence, celerity, fortitude, and audacity. And whoever wants to be good in this art should have part in these virtues.

| 
Prudence/Wisdom

No creature sees better than me, the Lynx.
And I always set things in order with compass and measure.

| 
No creature sees better than I the Lynx, and I proceed always with careful calculation.

| 
Celerity/Speed

I, the tiger, am so swift to run and to wheel
That even the bolt from the sky cannot overtake me.

| 
I am the Tiger, and I am so quick to run and turn, that even the thunderbolt from heaven cannot catch me.

| 
Audacity/Daring

None carries a bolder heart than me, the lion,
But to everyone I make an invitation to battle.

| 
No one has a more courageous heart than I, the Lion, for I welcome all to meet me in battle.

| 
Fortitude/Strength

I am the elephant and I carry a castle as cargo,
And I do not kneel nor lose my footing.

| 
I am the Elephant and I carry a castle in my care, and I neither fall to my knees nor lose my footing.

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

Prudence

Everything born under the sky will be discerned with [my] eyes; I, the lynx,
I conquer [by] measurement whatever it pleases [me] to attempt.

Quickness

I am quick in the hunt and roll the quick ones back in their orbit,
Nor in my running will the lightning overcome the tiger.

Courage

I am the quadruped, the strong crown; my brave things,
For instance, are the foundation of every axis. Now conquers the lion of the heart;
[...] Therefore, we call whomsoever to arms.

### Notes

This is the famous "Segno del Magistro" (Master's Diagram / Segno page) -- folio 32r (numbered "34" in upper right). A central crowned figure stands with seven swords radiating around him and four animals at the cardinal points: the Lynx (presteza/quickness) at the left with a compass, the Lion (ardimento/boldness) at the right, the Elephant (forteza/strength) at the bottom-left bearing a castle/tower, and a fourth animal (likely the Tiger, representing prudentia/prudence) which appears integrated or partly at the top. The seven swords represent the seven blows of the sword. Some text labels are very small and partially illegible. This is one of the most celebrated pages in all historical European martial arts manuscripts. The word "Aupis" near one sword may be a label for a specific blow type. Several readings are highly uncertain [?] due to the small script around the figure.

---

## Folio 32v
**Section:** Sword in Armor

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top-left:
¶ Per fior Fiore. doy Magistro. che lo Primano ben
demostrar. lo qui spuntare che ma per quelledre ceri-
foy fiore. e la compria medesma. che sono lo posta
lo per gratio breve. la Superiora. ma e lo faza-
foy. lo per presto breve. la Superiora. meste lo
foy. gionto alla compri. e Alo una posta lora qui
parevite sia represa.

¶ Posta breve la Serpentina.

Top-right:
¶ Posta de una croce. che mi contrario. lo contria finte. [?] fia che
lo per posta te per comtrare. De la mia conzar e lo postone di-
foy mio per fiere. rotsse de foy fiare. la contrario che aya per
lo fallare. la concepitura. che la per la. e la verso. ayo
e soy la fiore. L'erra nolo questo a noy fallore.

¶ Posta di una croce.

Bottom-left:
¶ Simonetta e la pregontare. Per lo Soperana. ala
modo da la prenomi guarda come forte posendo che
foy en certo. avento al posta. vera fiore guarda
lo la ferire. e chi lo contra apte de foy fio-
foy fiore.

¶ Dente serpentina la Soperana.

Bottom-right:
¶ Posta de ferre la meçana. che chamamento. e de fiorome
fesse la che e alo guista fiore. la postura fiore. e dondo
al le la guarda. la meçana de la venta brevi. e nomada
foy possono possa. e rimanamente.

¶ Posta de ferre la meçana.

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
For the art of the Flower. Two Masters. That the first one well demonstrates. He who thrusts here but for those ceremonies of the art. And the purchase itself. That they are the guard for the brief grace. The Superior. But make the art. For the quick short. The Superior. The art comes to the purchase. And to one guard then here it appears to be the counter.

**Guard: Short Serpentine (Posta breve la Serpentina).**

Top-right:
Guard of the Cross. That my contrary. The contrary feints. That for the guard against him. From my preparation and the large guard of the art, I strike. Broken from the art's strike. The contrary that has the failing. The conception. That for her. And the verse. I have and I am the art. The error — not this, to us failure.

**Guard: Of the Cross (Posta di una croce).**

Bottom-left:
Simonetta and the question. For the Superior [guard]. In the manner of the forenamed guard, how strong being that the art was certain. Coming to the guard. True art — guard against the strike. And who the contrary is apt to the art.

**Guard: Serpentine the Superior (Dente serpentina la Soperana).**

Bottom-right:
Guard of the Iron at the Middle. That called. And of the art split that is to this just art. The posture of the art. And giving to the guard. The middle of the coming short. And named art they can guard. And remaining.

**Guard: Iron at the Middle (Posta de ferre la meçana).**

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[1] *We are six guards for armored fencing,
Which art we know how to perform in its completeness.
And this art concludes everything in the right truth:
It applies poleax, sword, and dagger to great extremes.
And here we'll explain how the art can come:
Masters and students will do it without lying.*

We are 6 masters who are very knowledgeable in the art of armed fighting, and each one of us is an expert in this art. Hand-held weapons do not worry us, because we know how to defend against any cuts and thrusts that may come our way.

[2] The Shortened Stance, the Serpent

*I am the Shortened Stance, the Serpent,
And I have a fine point for passing through armor.*

I am the Short Serpent Guard, and I consider myself superior to the other guards. And when I thrust those I strike will be well-marked.

[3] The Stance of the True Cross

*I am the stance called the True Cross
And cuts and thrusts mean nothing to me.*

I choose to use the True Cross Guard against you. And your thrust will fail to strike me. I will make cover to your attack as I make my step, and my thrust will strike you without fail. Neither you or the other guards concern me, because I am so well versed in the art of armed fighting that my crossing cannot fail me. Step, cross and strike, and this art will never fail you.

[4] The Raised Serpent

*I am the Serpent, the Sovereign;
I shoot great thrusts down low.
I also cover against cuts and thrusts,
Those strikes amount to little to me.*

I am the High Serpent and I am well positioned to give great underhand thrusts, since I begin high but end low. I will throw a great thrust into you as I step. That is my skill and I do it well. Your cuts do not concern me in the slightest, because when it is time to hand out great thrusts, you’ll get a large portion from me.

[5] The Middle Iron Gate

*I am the Iron Gate in the Middle:
Throwing great thrusts, I am always deceitful.*

My name is Middle iron Gate, and whether you are armoured or unarmored I make strong thrusts. I step offline with my left foot and I put a thrust into your face. I can also place my point and blade between your arms in such a way that I will put you into the middle bind, as depicted and identified earlier.

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

We are in deeds of arms six greatly skillful acts.
Whatever master of arms will acquire these, 
He will surpass sword or dagger and then two-edged ax.

Here begins the sword in armor, and great will be the Master who can make these plays. These Masters are six and each one is in guard; they will not delay in covering and striking. And whoever knows most in this, their art, will have a part in all of the following plays.

I am the Short Position itself. I am called by the proper name Serpentinus;
I am skilled at penetrating with a point.

In the Shortened Stance, the Serpent, do I want to come. If you are not well-armored, I will make you feel it! I hold myself better than any other guard for striking with the point. Because of my edges, I sign myself with the cross, and nothing can you do to me. In armor and without I want to prove it.

In this Position of the Leopard, I truly observe the Serene One,
And always checking the deepest cuttings of the point.

I am the Stance of the True Cross which I want to make to counter you. Your thrusts will not enter into me. I cover myself from you in the step that I make and my thrust will injure you without fail, so that you and the other guards can do little to me. I know armored fencing so well that I can never fail in the crossing (for stepping, crossing, and striking, the art wants these things without fail), and I break all your thrusts and I certainly will not fail: I come over and through, I go under the point and upward.

I am called Serpentinus, and Raised. And, point high,
I put my members below the lowest flat [of the sword].

I am the Serpent and I am High and well-armored. I quickly make great underhand thrusts because I am high and return down low. I'll drive a strong thrust to you with a step: this is my art and I know how to do it well. I have not a care for your edges, for I know the art and I will give you the better part of my point.

I am the guard in the formerly established Middle Iron Gate.
I do not do much harm with the point, and I am always frightful.

Of Iron, I am called the Middle Gate, because in armor or out I give strong thrusts. And I will step out of the way with my left foot and thrust my point in your face, or I will enter [with] my point and with the edge between your arms and force you into the Middle Bind (which was previously depicted and named).

### Notes

This page begins the section on the guards (poste) of the sword in armor (spada in arme). Four figures are shown, each in a named guard position. Each figure wears a crown (indicating master status). The guard names appear in red ink below each figure:
- Top-left: Posta breve la Serpentina (Short Serpentine Guard)
- Top-right: Posta di una croce (Guard of the Cross)
- Bottom-left: Dente serpentina la Soperana (Serpentine Tooth the Superior)
- Bottom-right: Posta de ferre la meçana (Middle Iron Gate Guard)
These are the opening guards for armored sword combat. Text is written in red and brown ink.

---

## Folio 33r
**Section:** Sword in Armor

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top-left:
¶ Posta Sagitaria. foy qui mie coverte. domanda de oy ale
postate e come passimento e foy mie la fronte. Chi la mesarize.
bontanose amagio. in gucho ben vero coverte e l'erta.
ne si cosa de una coverte. Guarda de un arce che l'alte
de Gusto.

¶ Posta Sagitaria

¶ Posta de croce bastarda. foy. di possa croce. io che la
la foy posta advenir lo cor. la mostra. e Dove avanzo e c' l'or-
naçada longo e de campo. Persona. Che mancha ale colpo
io esso. Che vado orge prigio meço che lo fiore.

de lo posta. per la posta fiore a contra que poy contro

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
Sagittary Guard. The art here, my cover. I ask of the guards and as passing, and the art was my front. Who measured it. Good in mastery. In play well true, covers and the art. Nor such a thing as a cover. Guard of an arc that is high of this art.

**Guard: Sagittary (Posta Sagitaria).**

Top-right:
Guard of the Bastard Cross. The art. Of the cross guard. I who the art's guard to come to the heart. The showing. And where I advance and the ornament long and of the field. Person. Who lacks at the blow, I am. Who goes now seized in the middle of the art.

**Guard: Bastard Cross (Posta de croce bastarda).**

Bottom-left:
Of the guard of the cross. And well I confess in this that the Scholar's guard. Guard of the vale, that of this cover. Guard that art, grace of art. For then covered cross. In completion. These are the guards of the cross. Guard and covered guard. Against the mystery guard. Guard.

Bottom-right:
Against the great guard of the master who asks me. This short of the guard of the cross. Of the guard of the cross. The art the Sagittary guard me not there — seeming to be at the command of the guard. For the art's guard against which then against the middle and for the Scholar — lost but free, at which I am mine. This is of the art's contrary master. See the art's thread, mystery. That of the Superior.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[6] The Archer's Stance

*I am the Archer's Stance, the sentinel,
And I am always ready to strike and cover.*

I am known as the Archer’s Guard, and I throw great thrusts as I step offline. And if strikes or thrusts come against me, I make a strong cover, and then immediately I strike with my counter. This is my skill, and I never vary from it.

[The Getty resembles the Pisani Dossi image.]

[7] The Stance of the Bastard Cross

*Of the Stance of the Cross, I am the Bastard,
And I will not delay in making her plays.*

I am the Hybrid Cross Guard, and I am related to the True Cross Guard, in that anything it can do, I can do also. I make strong covers, thrusts or cuts, usually avoiding your strike by stepping offline, and my strikes are my greatest asset.

[8] *With this cover I believe that I can waste anyone,
Following that which you will see the Scholar do to you.*

This cover is made from the True Cross Guard, when I step diagonally offline. And so that you can see what can be done from this cover, my students will show the plays that follow it, and since they are experienced in mortal combat, they will show these skills without hesitation.

[In the Pisani Dossi, the Master is missing his crown.]

[9] *This thrust exits from the Master's cover,
And the other plays hereafter may well arise.*

I am the first student of the Master who came before me, and I make this thrust from his cover. You should also know that you can make this thrust from the True Cross Guard and from the Hybrid Cross Guard. As the opponent makes his thrust, the Master or his student who is waiting in one of these guards (or posts) keeps his body low and steps offline crossing the opponent's sword, with his point high into the opponent's face or chest, and with the hilt of his sword kept low, as shown here.

[In the Getty, the Scholar's opponent's right foot is forward.]

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

Here the sword will shift into a Malignant Position by penetrating;
Now I cover with my arm, for I hold my limb strongly upright.

The Archer's Stance, by this name I am called. Great thrusts I give while stepping out of the way. And if you come against me with a blow of the edge, I make a good cover and quickly I strike my counter. This is my art and it does not change.

I am the Position consecrated as the True Cross by many masters.
The point is not a nuisance to me, nor will the cutting edge harm [me].

Of the True Cross I am the Bastard Stance (Posta di Crose Bastarda); that which she can do, I also choose to do. For my strategy, I make good covers, thrusts, and cuts, always while voiding blows out of the way, and with my blows I make my greatest bargain.

By using this covering, you would now be sure to repel whomsoever,
Just as you will see the students play at any time.

I come with this cover from the Stance of the True Cross, stepping out of the way to the side. And you will see what I can do from this cover; through my Scholars I can show it, because they make my complimentary plays (those that are for combat to the death). The art they will show without a doubt.

I would do other plays if ever it will be pleasing;
This point escapes from the deepest covering of the master.

I am the first Scholar of the Master that came before me, and I make this thrust because it is from his cover. Also, I say that this thrust could quickly be made from the Stance of the True Cross and from the Stance of the Bastard Cross, and I say that immediately as the player throws a thrust to the Master (or Scholar) who was in the aforesaid guards (or stances), the Master (or Scholar) should move lower with his body and step out of the way, crossing the way and thrusting upwards to his face or chest (the cross of the sword held low) as is depicted here.

### Notes

Page number "35" in upper-right corner. Four armored figures showing guards for armored combat. Each has a red-ink guard name label:
- Top-left: Posta Sagitaria (Sagittary Guard) -- a figure in armor with a sword held behind
- Top-right: Posta de croce bastarda (Bastard Cross Guard)
- Bottom-left: continues cross guard variations
- Bottom-right: a counter-play showing two combatants engaged, with one being grappled
The bottom register shows two combat pairs rather than single guard figures, suggesting a transition from guard demonstrations to plays/techniques. Text is in brown ink with red guard-name labels.

## Folio 33v
**Section:** Sword in Armor

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top-left:
¶ Per quando io entro che la una pontta. nor me contraro.
in la ponta me in lo voltar. e la volta. per tuta la voltara.
e poi metto la punta in la volta. Ello posito esso uno
voy ferido un mie maistre dala altra zugar foy fiore.

Top-right:
¶ Quando le mane ale ferro in questa ben siego.
A pur lo urtado de la bisogno. a a li e armato. mio doy-
voy. ello voltar foy per saldo. per che lo fiore. soy in
la foy arme. faremio di tratto fiore. tute in fiore.

Bottom-left:
¶ Quando le mane ello con la guida. scolaro di la possa luma
fiore. Guardo no presto avolta. mudo che le far do
lo arme. acuelo. che la maistre e la Fiore finamente done-
maistre. che io lo voltar e la fiore ficando lo fiore
tristiçiade chi meço brede posta. foy la fiore fiore scolaro
e la possire.

Bottom-right:
¶ Per la bontanor de fiore. e contano fiore. lo fiore le
fiole voltar. che lo foy de lo refilare. fiore lo possto
voy maistre. germanto lo possto. noy averto a la posta breve.
maistre Scolaro. fermando. ello le valo posta fioreto
que voy alo fiore informamente de trista. fin un posto-
guarda. e fiore arme fiore.

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
For when I enter with a thrust. Do not counter me. In the thrust, in the turning. And the turn. For all of the turning. And then I put the point in the turn. And in this position, one of you is struck. My master from the other play art.

Top-right:
When the hands are at the iron in this well secure. Even the impact of the necessity. And he is armed. My two of you. And the turning art was for firm. For the art. I am in the art of arms. We will make from the art. All in the art.

Bottom-left:
When the hands and with the guide. Scholar of the possible light art. I guard, not quickly turning. Changed what makes the arms. That the master and the art finely give — master. That I turn and the art, fixing the art of sadness that is middle short guard. The art of the Scholar and the ability.

Bottom-right:
For the goodness of the art. And counting the art. The art turns the threads. That the art of refinement. Art, the posited you master. Germinating the posited. We open at the short guard. Master Scholar. Fixing. And the valor of the art's guard that you in the art in the form of sadness. Until one post guard. And art arms art.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[10] If I see my thrust cannot penetrate his chest, or his face due to his visor, I can lift his visor in order to thrust into his face. And if this does not satisfy me, I can apply other stronger plays.

[11] When I closed with this opponent, his armor prevented me from striking him as shown in the previous play. So instead I push strongly against his elbow and make him turn away. Let’s see now if his armor is strong enough when he is attacked from behind.

[12] 

When I saw that my sword was ineffective against you, I quickly applied this grappling technique. I believe, see and feel that your armor will be useless to you when I put you in this strong lower bind, which is shown further in the next picture.

[13] I have you locked in the lower bind or “strong key”, and from this position you cannot escape regardless of how strong you are. I could hurt you or even kill you. I could stop to write a letter and you would not even be able to see what I was doing. You have lost your sword and your helmet, you have been humiliated and you’ll soon be hurting.

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

Indeed, by means of this lower bind you will depart on your face.
And moreover, I strike deadly wounds in your chest.

### Notes

Four combat pairs in armored sword combat. All figures wear armor (indicated by hatched drawing). The techniques shown involve close-quarters thrusting and grappling while in armor. The bottom-left pair shows one figure forcing the other down. The bottom-right pair shows a wrestling/throwing technique. Text is very difficult to read on this page with heavy abbreviation throughout. Many readings are conjectural.

## Folio 34r
**Section:** Sword in Armor

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

de una croce e dela bastarda. con lo guanto non ghe va
restro possamo. chi la scolaro la bestia. fiore a como

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
This play is of the art, a master in the guard of the cross and of the bastard [cross]. With the gauntlet he does not go there — we can from behind. The scholar, the beast. Art as I can open at the with having them. Of the art as of the art I guard, we and he open there. That the first of the art measured the other deeply.

Top-right:
When in contest of the guard. I in the manner the person. For I can art. Of the cut. In the art of placing. And the art at this of the guard. In the art of the how. Your guard. Guard, art, the Scholar. At the guard of Scholar art that we can. Against the turning. Of the meta-placement art at the guide. To this. Against. In truth the metalwork of the same. Art the guide as. Confidence. Two guards. Of these things. Who the master art.

Bottom-left:
Against one now, the scholar. That asks me. Art, the content. Higher than your Guide. The guard. I command this for the art. And for the art how much power guide of the art.

Bottom-right:
Separate even this scholar who asks me. That the guard. And all good. Of the art of Quickness. That of the high master can the art as in flight. Guide me that for the arms can. Until the Scholar master the art turns against and art.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[14] *You go to the ground because of the point of the sword,
And if I do not do you worse you will have a bargain.*

This play flows from the first Master who showed the True Cross Guard or the Hybrid Cross Guard, as follows: when the opponent makes a thrust at the Student who is waiting in guard, the student quickly steps off line to make cover, and counters with a thrust to the opponent’s face. Then the student advances his left foot behind his opponent’s lead foot as shown, in order to throw him to the ground, using the point of his sword to hook around the opponent’s neck.

[16] When I move from my guard to a close range cover and am unable to strike you with a cut, I strike you with a thrust. If I cannot strike you with either, I strike you with the cross guard or with the pommel, depending on my preference.

And when I choose to play at close range, and my opponent believes I intend to use my sword, I switch to grappling when this gives me an advantage, or, if not, I can strike him in the face with my cross guard as I told you before, whichever I like.

[17] As you saw, the student who preceded me struck his opponent in the face with the crossguard of his sword. Thereafter he can quickly strike him in the face with his pommel, as you see depicted below.

[18] Also let me tell you that the student immediately before me who struck his opponent in the face with the pommel of his sword, could also have done what I do, that is, step with his right foot behind his opponent’s left leg, and then hook his opponent’s neck with his sword handle, in order to throw him to the ground as I do.

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

You will depart on your face, with the point of the sword in the ground,
And then I would make the unluckier thing itself settle in your mind.

When the Scholar that came before me [9] cannot finish the player with a thrust, he advances his left foot behind [the player's] right. And the point of his sword he thrusts under [the player's] throat to throw him to the ground as is depicted here.

### Notes

Page number "36" in upper-right corner. Four combat pairs in armored sword combat. The upper pairs show techniques from the cross guard positions described on the previous pages. The lower pairs show counters and responses. All figures are armored. The top-left text references "posta de una croce e dela bastarda" (guard of the cross and of the bastard), connecting to the guard names from fol. 32v-33r. Text remains challenging to read with many abbreviated forms.

Again this play is from the alert guard. And in this manner. For the Scholar art of the contrary in this guard. And there they go contrary. And that the force guard of the art. ### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[15] *You feel the sword that I have set at your neck
And I will show you death on the ground.*

Also let me point out that if the Student has moved to close range, and finds himself unable to destroy his opponent with his sword, then he should use his sword to grapple as shown, that is, he should cast his sword to his opponent’s neck, then step with his right foot behind the opponent’s left foot, and throw him to the ground to the right.

[19] *If I turn myself close on your left side,
Your sword will be lost from your right hand.*

This student is unable to strike his opponent effectively, so he transitions to grappling as follows: he places his sword point to the inside of his opponent’s right arm. Then the student slides his sword and his left arm under the opponent’s right arm, so as to throw him to the ground, or lock him in the lower bind, known as the “strong key”.

[20] This play also flows from the True Cross Guard, as follows when a student is in that guard, and an opponent comes against him and suddenly attacks him, then the student should step off the line and thrust his sword point into his face as you see me do here.

[26] This is a good strong grapple: as he makes his grip on the opponent’s right arm, the student steps with his left foot behind opponent’s left foot, and thrusts the point of his sword into his face. He can also throw him to the ground to the student’s right.

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

I hold the point above the neck, and you feel that.
Now you will suffer the labor of death, the fates do not deny.

This Scholar that came before me [14], if he cannot throw the player to the ground with the point of his sword and with his left foot outside of [the player's] right, he steps with his right foot in front of [the player's] left and throws his sword to his neck. And this play I make on his behalf.

The sword will fall on your right side.
I travel around quickly to the left, the limb having been drawn tight, if I am in front.

Again I, who am the fourth Scholar, say that our Master can make this play from the cover which he has made—that is, that he should step with his right foot forward, and he should thrust the point of his sword under [the player's] right arm, and then follow the sword with his left arm. And when he has passed the [player's] right arm with his left arm behind the [player's] elbow, he will twist him into the Under Bind without a doubt. That which he has not done, I do for him—he will have the honor, and I the trouble.

### Notes

Four combat pairs continuing armored sword techniques. All figures are in armor. The top-left text references "posta d'aviso" (the alert guard). The bottom-left shows a close-quarters wrestling/grappling technique in armor, with one figure throwing the other. The bottom-right shows a standing grapple with swords. This page concludes a series of armored sword combat plays. Text is heavily abbreviated throughout and many readings remain uncertain.

## Folio 35r
**Section:** Sword in Armor

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top-left:
¶ Questo si e lo contrario della maestra che rinversa
e di sotto ella sia prohata. Che vogna volta che dito contrario
po fare ala maestra che rinversa e in tuti per simile
modo po fare lo contrario. Zoe che quello che fa la maestra
po fare lo contrario. E simiantre e poy fara quello
contrario tegnando fermo la sua man dritta al brazo
sinistro desso scolaro e gli fa la volta e forza e pericolo di
farllo andar subiteno quel brazo.

Top-right:
¶ Lo zogo pixolano della maestra contrario che uol bensauere
zuogare le sue parte. Di modo le guadagno rebatter
possando soto la suo spada in la testa rebatter che venira
e stara ale mançanuersa e vibore cum gli zanbreri. E
questo zogo ella segue lo braço bilanciato.

Bottom-left:
¶ Questa spada zogha per guadagnare e per Aqua con lo
contrario zoladore e in ben praticho e gorta. La posta
della maestra e straforte e guadagna. Ella vuol
stare con la gamba dritta denançi e che torna in posta
breve. E la prima vol fare grande Streta che possa
passare e elle poy la daga ella gamba. Guello che
questo [?] e po lo braço e le brigne dove e lo scolaro
sforçare figural e questo mete deluano.

Bottom-right:
¶ Questa sidena spada grande raghone e tuta. Quello chi
posta alla se fin posta prima ale doi posture quasi la sorte
della punta ben. De respetto maestre aqua si posta
posa molte arye tutti potente so este re li posta
passare. E questo zambreri sulle vado elloy li posta
ella potente vuole offrie sua brente punente ali[?] grande.

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
This is the counter of the master who does the reversal, and from below it shall be proven. For every time the said counter can be done to the master who reverses, and in all similar ways the counter can be made. That is, what the master does, the counter can do. And similarly, then that counter will be made, holding firm his right hand at the left arm of the scholar, and he makes the turn and force and peril of making him go suddenly to that arm.

Top-right:
The small play of the master counter who wants to know well how to play his parts. In this way the gain, to beat back, passing under his sword to the head, beating back what will come and he will stand at the left hand side and strikes with the legs. And this play follows the balanced arm.

Bottom-left:
This sword plays to gain and for water with the counter player and is well practiced and short. The guard of the master is very strong and gains. She wants to stand with the right leg forward and then returns to the short guard. And the first wants to make a great close that can pass, and then the dagger and the leg. That which this [?] and then the arm and the reins where the scholar is to force the figure and this puts far away.

Bottom-right:
This shows the great sword reason and all. He who is in guard, first to the two postures, almost the fate of the point well. With respect to the master, water in guard, he places many airs, all powerful, so these are the guards to pass. And this strikes well on the sides and the guard wants to offer his cutting point to the great [?].

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[27] This is the counter to the Remedy Master and all of his students. It is the case that every counter you use against a Remedy Master will also break the plays of all of that Remedy Master’s students. And this is true for the spear, poleaxe, sword, dagger, and grappling, that is, for the entire art.

Let me return to address the Remedy Master: when he is making his cover, the Counter-Remedy Master places his left hand behind his opponent’s right elbow and gives it a powerful push, turning him in order to strike him from behind as you see drawn next.

[28] I am the student of the Counter-Remedy Master who came before me and I complete his play as follows: when I have turned my opponent, I immediately strike him from behind, either under his right arm, or under his coif into the back of his head, or into his buttocks (excuse my language), or into the back of his knee, or in any other place where he is unprotected.

[29] This sword can be used as a sword or a poleaxe, and should not be sharpened from the guard down to one hand’s-width from the point. The point should be sharp and the sharp edge should be about a hand’s-width in length. The roundel below the hilt should be able to slide down the blade to a hand’s-width from the point and no further. The hilt needs to be strongly made with a heavy pommel with well-tempered spikes. The spikes should be well-tempered and sharp. The front of the sword should be as heavy as the back, and the weight should be from three and a half to five and a half pounds, depending on how big and strong the man is and how he chooses to be armed.

[30] This other sword is fully sharpened from the hilt all the way to the point, except there is an unsharpened section in the middle about a hand’s width, big enough for a gloved hand to be able to hold it there. Just like the previous sword, this sword should be sharp with a fine point. And the hilt should be strong with a heavy pommel and a sharp well-tempered spike.

### Notes

- Four combat figures shown in two registers, each pair depicting sword combat between armored figures
- Two swords are illustrated between the upper and lower registers, appearing to be cross-hilted longswords
- The crowned figure (master) appears in each pair
- Some text is difficult to read due to fading and the cursive hand; several uncertain readings marked with [?]
- Page number "37" visible in upper right corner (original foliation)

## Folio 35v
**Section:** Axe in Armor

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top-left:
¶ In sta posta breve la serpentina che meschino un altro
motto porta. Potta breve uien prouare ben che prouatissima
che manda prouare dio brore e prattiche coniugi aquiste
dottrina o che minga ben la gran.

Top-right:
¶ In sta posta brattesa volta per dou volta
contrario che in ley se conuien. E metti
ben la sforçata che de sta la posta brattesa
nulla prouare ata la segne mas da posta.

Center-left label:
Posta breve serpentina.

Center-right label:
Posta de uera crosse.

Bottom-left:
¶ Posta de donna per maestra douere cumquere. De ella
in esperta sara grande rason ella maglia fanno, che de
piasere lo per braço e subitamente son breure in lo dritto e
in lo rebater. E de la posta e a con alta posta bre e una
lunga posta lu non agge e la piu che de ogni posta e
drente la testa olto de uno manzino in rebatter e in ella
lunga zolla mena.

Bottom-right:
¶ Sto in posta de donna voi esta posta mezana
e contraria, io intendo le fuo forzo alla mea crosse
e dou oltra donna possa dou esta breve e un alto
posta ella aqua parte rason. La posta breve uno
pasa alla dritta le. Rebuterne la
volta dal ditto breve. Chi in tiy lo messe prohar e
ale ditta lo briga. Parçonando
uelo me e som la spada che fiabeche bello non vuolle e
aricorta e poy pare lo prouare, dar la ban alga e breve.
E ditene de la mea man breve lo sogo possa la nuti.
ala pogire alla postera lo brachia, ella chi ten vexe
stetien ella piu de rebuterne e parlore segnarmente.

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
In this short serpentine guard which a wretched one carries with another motto. Short guard comes to prove well, for it is most proven, which commands to prove with God's arm and practiced unions acquired by doctrine, and which leads well to the great.

Top-right:
In this twisting guard turned for two turns, the counter that in it is fitting. And you put well the forced one, for of this twisting guard nothing to prove but the signs of the guard.

Center-left label:
Short serpentine guard.

Center-right label:
Guard of the true cross.

Bottom-left:
Guard of the woman for the master to conquer. From her, the expert will have great reason and the mail they make, for to please by the arm and suddenly are short in the right and in the beating back. And of the guard it is with a high short guard and a long guard, he has not the more that of every guard, and inside the head, high, of a left-hand strike in beating back and in the long play leads.

Bottom-right:
I stand in the guard of the woman, you are in this middle guard and contrary. I intend the force to my cross and two beyond, the woman can [stand in] this short and a high guard on the water side reason. The short guard one step to the right. Beat back the turn from the said short. Who in you the master proved, and the said one the fight. Granting you and I the sword that fable well does not want, and shortened, and then appears the proof, give the ban sharp and short. And say of my short hand the play can the night. To the power at the back the arm, and she who holds you turns standing and the more of beating back and speaking by signs.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[1] The Stance of the Shortened Serpent

I am the Shortened Stance, the Serpent, with axe in hand;
If my thrust does not miss, I will made trouble for you.
 

I am the Short Serpent Guard and I consider myself better than the other guards. And whoever receives one of my thrusts will bear the scars. This guard delivers a powerful thrust that can penetrate cuirasses and breastplates. Fight with me if you want to see the proof.

[2] The Stance of the True Cross

I am the strong stance called the Cross:
Nothing can bother me, neither blows of the axe nor thrusts.

I am named the Guard of the True Cross, since I defend myself by crossing weapons, and the entire art of fencing and armed combat is based on defending yourself with the covers of crossed weapons. Strike as you wish, I’ll be waiting for you. And just as the student of the First Remedy Master of the sword in armor does, so I can do with a step and a thrust with my poleaxe.

[3] [The Stance of the Queen]

I am the Stance of the Queen, of pure loyalty:
I make great blows from a different measure.

I am the Guard of the Lady, and I go against the Boar’s Tusk guard. If he waits for me, I will make a powerful strike at him, in which I move my left foot off the line, and then I pass forwards, striking downwards at his head. And if he blocks strongly under my poleaxe with his, then even if I can’t strike him in his head I will not fail to strike his arms or hands.

[4] [The Wild Boar's Tusk/Middle Iron Gate]

I am the Boar's Tusk, full of daring:
Blows of the axe can do nothing to me.

If my Middle Iron Gate is opposed by the Guard of the Lady, we both know each other’s game, for we have faced each other many, many times in battle with swords and with poleaxes. And let me tell you, what she claims she can do to me, I can do better against her. Also let me tell you that if I had a sword instead of a poleaxe, then I would thrust it into my opponent’s face as follows: when I am waiting in the Middle Iron Gate with my two-handed sword, if he attacked me with his poleaxe with a powerful downward strike from the Guard of the Lady, then I quickly advance forward striking him strongly under his poleaxe as I step off the line, and then I quickly grasp my sword in the middle with my left hand and make the thrust into his face. While there is little difference between we two guards, I am the more deceptive.

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

Behold, with grasping hands I am called the Short Spear Position
Among mortals. And if the spear point would not try to deceive,
Perhaps I will deceive you, Man. Jupiter is near on a mountain.

Behold, I am a Position of strength, and I am called the Cross. No blow is
Bothersome to me, nor as yet the point of the poleaxe at any time.

[This Master was initially missing his crown, but a crown seems to have been drawn in by a later annotator.]

Behold, I am pure of faith standing in the Position of the Woman.
And I work deadly things by doubling a strike of strikes.

I am the strong Boar’s Tooth and, horribly daring,
By no means do I fear those strikes you make. It cannot be believed.

[The Paris image resembles the Pisani Dossi.]

### Notes

- Four figures arranged in two registers
- Upper two figures shown in named guards: "Posta breve serpentina" (Short serpentine guard) and "Posta de uera crosse" (Guard of the true cross)
- Lower two figures in dynamic sword stances
- All figures appear to be single combatants demonstrating guards/positions rather than paired combat
- The crowned figure (master) appears in upper positions
- Text is heavily abbreviated and uses dialect forms typical of Fiore's Venetian Italian

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top-left:
¶ Anche tornarle lo zio contrario posta de finestra volta e bona
maestra tegnudo posta fianno. E tenire arme de Anistri e son tuti
men che aspra ella che huomeni. Quel zapo francho forano
foreno. Chome non remouere que ela casta lo braço la punta
seglie arme e louar ella non prouare.

¶ Ella de finestra per chiamarse la finestra e una
nobulle bruça e sta la sua che botena. Corpo di contenta pro-
fichando. Sola di dare copa ella lo braço ella con-

### Translation — Claude Vision

Also to return the play, the counter guard of the window turned is a good master's proven guard. And to hold arms of the left and all are less harsh and of men. That free strike they will make. As not to remove, for the wrist, the arm, the point follows the arms and to raise it is not to prove.

Top-right:
She of the window, to be called the window, is a noble arm and stands as her good one. The body is content, profiting. Only to give a blow and the arm and the counter sign renders the window and acknowledges [?] not of renewal from the hands of guard. She takes herself in the window and the contrary the good one. And too contrary, nor who can wait, for he cannot. Watch well quickly.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[5] [The Stance of the Long Tail]

I am the Long Tail, used against the Window Guard, and I can strike at any time. With my downward strikes I can beat every poleaxe or sword to the ground, setting me up nicely for close play. As you see the plays that follow, please consider each one in sequence.

[6] [The Stance of the Casement Window on the Left]

I am named Window Guard on the left, and I am made with the right arm pulled back. This is not a good guard to wait in. Everything I do is deceptive. You think that I am going to strike a downward strike, but I pass backwards and switch guards. So while I began on the left, I actually enter on the right. And I can quickly transition to the plays that follow.

### Notes

- Page number "36" visible at bottom, and what appears to be "f5" notation at upper right (original foliation)
- Lower portion of the page shows very faint underdrawing or bleed-through from the other side
- The text is partially faded, making some words uncertain

## Folio 36v
**Section:** Axe in Armor

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top-left:
¶ Questi sono gli zoghi delle quale la remandere
prima a quattro. E sapiemo la qual poi prima contrassi
seran zugatere. Quando che po passare la daga della
adpezare se e cerere, como e que reprende re quello
quello piaga e guato che puno ella conuertire cose
le repagnie.

¶ Lo zoladore chança ale e guadagna la sua [?] aqua la spada mancha

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
These are the plays from which the sending back, first to four. And we know the one that can first be countered, they will be played. When one can pass the dagger of the approach and seek, as the one that takes back that wound and look, for he can convert the things of the company.

Top-right:
The player takes advantage and wins his [?] water the left sword and my frankness that makes the upper hand. And wins the long rest you see. And of the turning.

Bottom-left:
Also the player who asks me to make this play, when she who strikes where beyond practices. The one by free passage can rest the good one well and finds the way, my mouth to prove all to the second the fight.

Bottom-right:
The player who asks, see that I the point at the dagger of me and can good fortune and the strike. The turn and the nature to prove that leads the dagger and the pull of the house and the blow well. The counter short from the pull to the blow well. The trick of the body of the following, this that asks, or this the mouth all part.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[7] *I have beaten your axe to the ground;
Mine will quickly be thrust in your face.*

These are the plays that these guards put to the test. Each guard can do them, and each guard believes it will prevail. As is drawn here, whoever beats his opponent’s poleaxe to the ground can do these plays, and will succeed as long as the opponent fails to counter him.

[In the Getty and Pisani Dossi, the Master is missing his crown.]

[8] This student puts his axe between his opponent’s legs, and covers his eyes with his left hand. When the opponent, who cannot see, tries to turn, he will surely fall to the ground.

[9] *I am risen from the Boar's Tusk with my axe,
And with that I have wounded you in your face.*

The previous student can also do this play when he is at close range, as you can see here. He steps with his left foot on top of his opponent’s poleaxe head, and draws back his own poleaxe, then thrusts it into his opponent’s face.

[In the Getty, the Scholar's right foot is on his opponent's poleax.]

[10] *I have lifted your visor—you feel it—
And I will bore out your teeth with my axe.*

The previous student saw that it was not possible to strike his opponent in the face with his poleaxe, because his opponent’s visor is too strong. So he advances his left foot forward and lifts the opponent’s visor, and drives his point into his face with as much force as he can give to his poleaxe. You can add on this play to any of the previous plays, as well as to any of the plays which follow.

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

I will certainly throw your poleaxe down to the earth,
But mine will strike the face with listless wounds.

Now from the Boar’s Tooth and the particular poleaxe, ready I immediately sprang forth. 
And I pierced the face using that thing with the strength of oak.

[In the Paris, the Scholar wears a crown.]

Lo, I press your very own face with the strong hand, and you feel that.
My sacred poleaxe will now extract these, your very own teeth.

[In the Paris, the Scholar wears a crown.]

### Notes

- Four pairs of figures arranged in two registers, showing sword and dagger combat
- Upper-left pair shows two armored figures grappling with swords
- Upper-right pair shows a figure controlling another from behind
- Lower-left pair shows two figures in sword combat with one stepping through
- Lower-right pair shows dynamic combat with one figure striking
- The crowned figure (master) appears in multiple positions
- Text describes plays (zoghi) and the actions of the "zoladore" (player/scholar)

## Folio 37r

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
By my reason, this one who opens before you in the drawing of a strong dagger with the hilt, and my frankness in no way in the ferocity, the arm holds firm, for you wish to open the scholar.

Top-right:

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[11] *Because of my hand which I have under your arm
I will cause you trouble in the strong key.*

With this hold I can strike you in the head with my poleaxe, and with my left arm I will put you in the Strong Lower Bind, which is more deadly than any other lock.

[These two images seem to show the beginning and end of the technique.]

[12] *I will make a quick rotation through this catch:
You will lose your axe; mine will strike you in the head.
 *

With a half-turn of this poleaxe I will take it from your hands. And once I have taken it from you with this particular turn, I will strike you in the head with it, as the next student shows. And I do not believe you will survive this.

[13] This play follows on from the student before me. As he clearly told you, you will likely drop to the ground dead after being struck in the head like this. And if this blow is not enough then I can give you another. If I choose I can also drag you to the ground by your visor, which is drawn next.

[14] I am demonstrating what the student before me said he would do to you, that is dragging you to the ground by your visor. This is a grappling technique that is one of the better ones you can do.

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

By means of this taking, I will possibly have made a whirling around.
From here yours will be plundered, but my poleaxe will strike your forehead.
In this way fate wants the strong to survive.

[In the Paris, the Scholar's right foot is forward and he wears a crown.]

This of mine with the guard of the poleaxe and then that the devil uproots, for in the most beautiful making, the [?] for good proving itself and [?] guard. And I the hand the body rests that shines the setting, accompanied, and when blows in the furnace, the great master. In faithful now and endowed me behind, for in pain then another good every thing works in yours. And I guard can properly in the courts.

Bottom-center:
This is the poleaxe that we have given pennant to the girded one, the blade assuredly I promise the throw in the valley, all to prove, ready and quickly turned, and punch proves with me, the master of poleaxes, the arm to put, for more to strike, and well to put me in the great happy labor.


Top-left:
Of mine, this is the name of the striker who can well and calls quickly in good play. And when he can fly to me, ugly but much adorned he passes through, and when set, he passes to the recovery under my pleasure of the thing.

Top-right:
This of mine with the guard of the poleaxe, and then what the devil uproots, for in the most beautiful fashion, the [?] for good proving and [?] guard. And I place my hand on the body that shines the setting, accompanied, and when blows in the great master's furnace. In faith now, and endowed behind me, for in pain then other good everything works in yours. And I guard properly can in the courts.

Bottom-center:
This is the poleaxe that we have, given pennant to the girded, the blade assuredly I promise the throw into the vale, all to prove, oven and quick and turned, and punch, to prove with me master of poleaxes, the arm to place that more to strike and well me to place in the great happy labor.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[15] This play is easy to understand, and you can clearly see how I can drag him to the ground. And when I have him on the ground, I can drag him behind me. And when the long tail of my poleaxe can no longer hold him, then he’ll feel my strikes.

[16] This poleaxe of mine is filled with a powder and is hollow and perforated. And this powder is so strongly corrosive that the moment it touches your eye, you will no longer be able to open it, and you may be permanently blinded.

I am the poleaxe, heavy, vicious and deadly. I deliver blows more powerful than any other hand-held weapon. If my first strike misses, then my poleaxe becomes risky to hold on to and is no more of any use to me. But if my first blow is powerfully made on target, then I can stop any other hand-held weapon. And if I am accompanied with good protective armor, then I can defend myself with any of the powerful striking guards of the sword.

My most noble lord, my Marquis, there are some vicious things shown in this book that you would never do. I show you them purely to aid your knowledge.

[17] This is the powder that you use in the poleaxe drawn above. Take the sap of the spurge, and dry it in a warm oven to make a powder. Now take two ounces of this powder and one ounce of powder of fior d'preda, and mix them together. Now load this powder into the poleaxe shown above. You can do this with any good caustic powder, but you won’t find a better recipe than the one in this book.

### Notes

- This page shows three groups of figures rather than four
- Upper two pairs show sword combat between armored figures
- Lower pair/group appears to involve poleaxe (polsenale/azza) combat
- The lower figures are partially faded or incomplete (underdrawing visible)
- This appears to be a transitional page, moving from sword plays to poleaxe material
- Text mentions "polsenale" (poleaxe) explicitly
- The crowned master figure appears in upper positions

## Folio 38r
**Section:** 4th Master

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top-left:
¶ Guarda mea supra douera delle veden arnobolada
che baço arguabille in arte e chaprisole e che ben bragha
o primo e che lo e reuende forende che pia bagno.

Top-right:
¶ Si questa daga e questa del mia scolaro leze in centra
e mia brute possa pitta e dittore e tutta. Nol me
a questo trare e quel ben presto e che ella contraria
me ogli siago frustrare.

Bottom-left:
¶ Del si miatre ben e magistro de piu che altri meri-
damente tuto bon che bey domamdare ben nen forire le
contraria. Che e per molto gli contraria la mea ella a
cade ono reguagare e si bon maistro ne lo nuy scolaro
alle sforçare ben domara coglo giustamenta prouare badagine.

Bottom-right:
¶ A per si miatre che craçatiro la maistro tegnudo
motto chi sedo con traga che fero
e questo male alla gue [?] le brene e gue la mossa
pon motto amarre presto le arme bragne. Medesfello meglio
e fortissima pruça. Vuy presta de remuoua esto prouare
questo me magistro.

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
My guard above must see the ennobled ones, for the low one arguable in art and capricious, and that well in breeches, the first, and that he sells and makes strong, for the greater bath.

Top-right:
If this dagger and this of my scholar read in the center, and my ugly power paints and dictates and all. He cannot pull this and that well quickly, and the contrary one cannot frustrate me of the eyes.

Bottom-left:
Of the master well and master of more than others, worthily all good, for well to ask, well not to strike the contrary. For very much the contrary of mine and falls to recover, and a good master nor the scholar to force, well to tame with just proving and watchfulness.

Bottom-right:
And for the master who the crucifier, the master held, the motto who sits with the pull that strikes, and this evil to the [?] the short and the move puts the motto to love quickly the brave arms. Better by itself and most strong arm. You quickly of removing this to prove this my master.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[62] I will give a turn to your dagger,
And in that way it will be quickly taken from you.

With my right hand I will make a horizontal turn to your dagger, pushing it round close to your arm that I am holding. And your dagger will be mine to control. And then I will deal with you as you deserve.

[63] If I raise your dagger upwards close to your elbow,
You will feel it instantly taken from you.

If I raise your dagger upwards close to your elbow, I will keep it in my hand and strike you for certain. But I will need to make this play very quickly, to make sure that you cannot counter me with your left hand.

[65] Against the Master who covers with both hands
I make this counter as my defense.
 

I am the Counter-remedy against the Fourth [Dagger] Remedy Master. And I counter all his plays that came before me. And with one quick wrench like this I will ruin this student’s hand and his master’s too. And if they are well armored the ruin of their hands will be all the more certain.

[66] *I want each of my students to know
That you cannot defend against the collar grab unless you move quickly.
And with the strike that I make against your elbow,
I will quickly feel your arm dislocate.*

I am the Fifth Dagger Remedy Master who defends against the collar grab made by this player. Before he can strike me with his dagger I destroy his arm like this, because the grip he has on me is actually to my advantage. And I can do all of the covers, holds and binds of the other remedy masters and their students who came before me. And I say this from experience: all who study this art should be aware that you cannot successfully defend the collar grab unless you move quickly.

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

In this way, &lt;I&gt; myself coil your dagger up using the whirlwind,
Because I would capture you, whether you would prevent or you would fight back.

Now if I attempt to lift your elbow [and] very own dagger,
You yourself certainly will see it has been suddenly freed.

By this means I will now seek the opponent, using both palms
In order to defend myself, just as the master does
Who seizes the companion with both hands during wrestling.

[The Paris resembles the Getty illustration, but the Master is missing his garter.]

You would grasp my chest. Thus far you have not been able to wound me.
I will, nevertheless, dislocate this, your shoulder, during wrestling.

### Notes

- Folio 38 is misbound in the physical manuscript (MS Ludwig XV 13). It belongs between folios 14 and 15, as it contains additional dagger plays (abrazare/dagger material) rather than the sword/poleaxe content of the surrounding folios 37 and 39.
- Four pairs of figures shown, all depicting unarmed/dagger grappling combat (abrazare)
- Figures are unarmored, shown in short tunics with visible hose
- The crowned figure (master) appears in the dominant position in each pair
- The combat shown is wrestling/grappling, consistent with the dagger section of the treatise
- This misplacement is well-documented in scholarly literature on the manuscript

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
As master, and you another way of unquartering the breach, and the master beyond part to part, and in this way he flees, and the blow of the dagger that can from afar long, and that well it were, and the mouth and well reaffirmed harness with which to strike from the guard if proven.

Top-right:
As master, and you another to leave also or maneuver of the poleaxe for good length. And the blow of the dagger that for the crowd and calls well and the gain of the one who follows you in the default, and the more void. Little and master's way, not in her the majestic wild Matagio, where the daggers my master and strongly. And if the dagger all flower of mind to lead my flowering. ### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[67] *After striking against your elbow, I will continue on
To quickly seek to find your dagger.*

This is another way to destroy the arm. And from this play I can move to other plays and holds…

[69] *If I want to get this spear off me,
I had better hit it hard from above,
So that I will break the staff of your spear
And then I will want to come to the close.*

This is another way to make you let go, and is also a better method of breaking off the head of a spear…

[71] *I am confident and certain that you will go to the ground,
And I care little or nothing for your dagger.*

I will throw you to the ground like this, before your dagger can get near me. And if your dagger comes down the center line to strike at me, I will release my grip and deal with your dagger, so that you will not be able to injure me in any way. Then with the remedy plays I will make you suffer.

[72] *I choose to try this method of throwing you to the ground,
And if this does not work I will try a different play.*

This play will make you let go of me. And in addition, if I advance my right foot behind your left foot, you will be thrown to the ground without fail. And if this play is not enough, I will try others on your dagger, because my heart and my eyes are never focused anywhere other than upon taking away your dagger quickly and without delay.

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

I would now strike close by your elbow. You will then move past me,
And I, the strong one, will unexpectedly attempt your dagger.

I am able to safely believe that you will go into the ground now;
Neither will your dagger be able to accomplish harming me.

I put to the test where I would at once lay you sharply on your back.Or “I put to the test where I would at once bend you back acutely.”
If, perchance, I do not strew you, I will [scribal error] something better.

### Notes

- Folio 38v continues the misbound section. Like 38r, this page belongs between folios 14 and 15 (dagger/abrazare section), not in its current position between ff. 37v and 39r.
- Four pairs of figures showing grappling and close-quarters combat
- Upper figures show standing grappling/wrestling techniques
- Lower figures show similar close-combat techniques
- All figures are unarmored, consistent with the abrazare (wrestling) section
- The crowned master figure appears in dominant positions
- Text is notably difficult to read on this page; many readings are uncertain

## Folio 39r
**Section:** Spear

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top (spanning both figures):
¶ Nuy siamo tre açogate in guardia in nostro
campo e conguistamo guadagne quello dello spada
e con lo se forire che in la falire possa lo braço per posta
e collatione. La longa ven lo zanghon tutti chi via prestamo
[?] con lo per bruttare alla tromphe de Fiore e ferere
conguisto si bia ban tempo relordino un possa franchamente
guadagne presta alle voltare e fori no ben prachio a le fe-
lore e le miatre. E dopo chi con le per sollanno.

Bottom (spanning both figures):
¶ Una mossa posta de fierro se ma e posta alla longa
reguardia. E de forire puo mia prima dello ben
se volle me mia longa e dagare che vollatare e possa
le Fiore le el braço. E ch per fellaritade che in questa possa
se un maitra longa e miotra spada ferro le de
fioritura e guadagna tutto suna e reguardar longhe e
assornamente punta. Ella guadagno che gira e fianchaza
contraria e colatione. E te colpo fioritura e de me mestre
desse delli guarda.

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top:
We are three joined in guard in our field, and we conquer the gain of the sword, and with the striking, for in the failing the arm can be in guard and collation. The long one comes, the hook, all who lend the way, with the dirtying at the trumps of Fiore and to strike, conquest well in good time, I reorder a frank gain, quick to turn and outside, not well practiced at the happiness and the master. And after, with the solemnity.

Bottom:
A move from the guard of iron, but is a guard at the long distance. And to strike, my first can of the well, if my long one wants and to give that to turn, and the Fiore can, and the arm. And for felicity, that in this guard a long master and my sword of iron of the flowering and gains all together, and to guard long and assuredly pointed. The gain that turns and the flank contrary and collation. And your blow of flowering and of my master gives of the guard.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[2] *I wait in this guard with my lance shortened:
My method is to beat and to exchange thrusts.*

We are three masters using spear guards that are closely related to the sword guards. I am the first, which is the Full Iron Gate. I am positioned to quickly beat aside my opponent’s spear, and to do that I step crosswise off the line with my right foot, crossing his spear and beating it to the left. When you step and beat aside together, that combination is hard to beat.

[3] *Your lance is extended and mine is shortened:
Attack and do not flee, so I may do you villainy.
 *

I am positioned in the Middle Iron Gate. My method is to beat aside then counter-strike. Come against me as you will with short spear or staff, when I beat your weapon aside as I step I will never fail to strike you. When you are using a short spear or sword, all guards that are made with the point offline are sufficient for you to wait in when facing any long hand-held weapon. Guards that cover from the right are followed with a step and a thrust. Guards from the left side can also cover and beat aside, but these will wound with a strike, because they cannot effectively place a thrust.

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

This is commonly employed: changing, and withdrawing
The spear which has been extended in opposition; now I delay all with a short javelin.

We are three Masters in guard with our lances, and we conform to the grips of the sword. And I am the first, and I am set in the Full Iron Gate to beat the lance of the player quickly—that is, that I will step with my right foot out of the way to the side, and crossing his lance I will beat it to the left side such that the step and the beat are made in a single pass with the strike. This is a thing that cannot fail.

Although my very own spear is shortened, nevertheless you will 
Go away pierced. And you would throw only if it pleased you before. Do not
Flee from that place. Tearful rewards would flow to gloomy you.

I have set myself in the Middle Iron Gate with shortened lance, and to beat and to strike is always my method. Come whoever wishes with extended lance or staff and I will beat with a step and I won't botch the strike. And all the guards that stay off to the side with shortened lance or shortened sword are sufficient to await any extended hand-held weapon; those of the right side cover, and with the cover step and thrust the point, while the guards of the left side cover or beat and then strike with blows, but cannot thrust very well with the point.

### Notes

- Four figures arranged in two registers, each showing paired sword combat
- Upper pair shows two figures with raised swords in high guards
- Lower pair shows two figures in extended/long guards with swords pointed forward
- The crowned master figure appears on the left in both pairs
- Page number "41" visible in upper right corner (original foliation)
- Text describes guards (guardia) and techniques related to Fiore's system
- References to "posta de fierro" (iron gate guard) and "longa reguardia" (long guard)

## Folio 39v
**Section:** Spear

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
In this is the model guard of the right window, which turns and strikes frankly for one outside, the long masterful wound. Worthy of the guard and arriving. The elect short one, for in this with this, that other one can gain and [believes?] the contrary. For I am to prove well, and she can prove. And the reordering, and that my way [?] for the flowering.

Top-right:
In the guard of [?] [...] with the counter of guard [...] of one, by way [...] not the long turning [...] My Fiore flowering, we flowering.

Bottom-left:
With this the fifth flowering, the gain that well short. The master, I flower of good spirit. And the forced right, the master of flower of good spirit and labor believes, and that one you flower of the stars and the good first.

Bottom-right:
The master and the counter in the guard, his master of the long refolding. And of her I turn, placing my flowering branches. And of the trench and the arm and the poleaxe, that all and him with it and the [?] were affixed and the doctor. He who knows from the gain, guard the assured way.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[4] *I will beat your [lance] with my lance as I step
And then I will quickly encase it in your chest.*

I am the noble Right Side Window, always ready to beat aside and counter-strike, and a long spear hardly bothers me. Also if I was using a sword I would wait for the long spear in this same guard, which beats aside and obstructs all thrusts. I can also exchange thrusts, or beat them to the ground without difficulty. I would finish this play with the play shown next.

[5] *This is the strike of these three previous Masters,
And in this way their lance should end in your face or chest.
 *

The three guards shown above (Full iron Gate, Middle Iron Gate and the overhead Right Side Window) should all finish with this strike, which is used to end the play and demonstrate their skill. Here I show the finishing strike for each of them.

[In the Getty, the Master is missing his crown.]

[6] 

This is the counter to the three spear masters shown above, who all finish their play with the strike shown above. Let me explain how to do it: When the Master believes he has driven my spear off-line, I rotate my spear and will strike him with the butt end, which is steel-capped, like the point. Thus, the plays of these masters pose little threat to me.

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

Now penetrating, my spear refutes your javelin,
And in your chest I will rush forth a great wound.

I am the noble Stance of the Casement Window on the right; I am always quick to beat and to strike, and of extended lances I care little. I would also await an extended lance with a sword by standing in this guard, which beats and retards every thrust. I could also make the exchange of thrusts, or beat them to the ground (which cannot fail). In the play hereafter I want to finish.

[In the Paris, the Master's spear leaves his face uncovered, appearing to go behind his head.]

This particular thing lies in ruins by means of the three preceding masters; it concerns striking back,
And the method is to cross the man through the chest with the javelin,
Or sooner the face and the visage with sad blood.

The three guards which came before should finish in this play—that is, the Full Iron Gate and the Middle Iron Gate and the Stance of the Casement Window, high on the right. In this play they finish the plays of their art. And I strike this man in the face on their behalf…

[In the Morgan, the Master is missing his crown.]

So that you would not harm me more, I stir up counters in opposition,
And I wedge into you, resisting, with beaten spikes.

…This is the counter to the three Masters of the lance that would finish in this play and in the way that was said. When the Masters believe my lance to be away from harming their persons, I give a turn to my lance and strike with its butt, since it has as good iron in the butt as in the point. The plays of these Masters bother me little.

### Notes

- Four pairs of figures in two registers showing armed combat
- Upper-left shows two figures with lances/spears held overhead
- Upper-right shows similar lance combat stance
- Lower-left shows two armored figures in close sword combat
- Lower-right shows a crowned master figure facing an opponent
- Page appears to continue the spear/lance (lancia) section of the treatise
- Top-right text is partially damaged or cut off at the page edge
- Text references "posta de finestra" (window guard) adapted to the lance

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[7] *We are three Masters who play on the left side:
Come one by one whoever wishes and we will waste him.
And for the fourth Master that finishes our plays in the art,
We have given him his fair share of the Crown.
This guard can suffice for me against any lance,
I cover myself both above and below by beating and stepping;
I do it with the butt low and with the other end high,
And I could make a cover and a strike without trouble.*

We are three left side guards, and I am the first, in the Boar’s Tusk. The left side guards do the same as the right side guards. We step offline advancing our lead foot, and then we strike with our thrusts on the left side. Both right side and left side guards beat aside then thrust, because other attacks with the spear are not as effective.

[8] *I know how to ward any lance with this guard:
Strike, for I want to harm you with mine in your chest.*

I am waiting for you in the Guard of the True Cross. You have clearly approached too close to me. I will pass backwards with my leading right foot, beating your spear offline to my right. My thrust will not fail me. Yours however will fail you.

[9] *I will finish you without fail in my turn,
Because I am the Master of all exchanges.*

I am positioned in the Left Side Window Guard. If I do not strike you with a thrust you will be lucky. I will step offline to the left with my left foot, with my point held high and my arms low. Then I will thrust into your face and you will have no defense. The play that follows is the finish used by all three Masters. If you try it once, you won’t wish to try it again.

[10] *The art of the lance makes its end here.
 armor and without, this is its greatest defense.*

Here we end the plays of the spear that are made from the left side against threats and attacks. These three guards shown above are carefully chosen to easily defeat the long or the short spear, since they are effective in offense or defense.

The counter to this thrust is easily done: when your thrust is beaten offline, you turn the butt of your spear and strike with that. And with that I have now shown you enough of the plays of the spear.

[In the Getty, the Master is missing his crown.]

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

We are three guards of the left side and I am the first, in the Boar's Tusk. Those guards that are on the right side do the same as we do on the left side: we step out of the way, first advancing the foot which is behind (as was said, out of the way), and with our thrusts on the right side we strike a bargain. And to finish the beat, both the right side and the left converge in a thrust so that other offenses with the lance cannot follow.

I wait in the Stance of the True Cross, for you are too close for my play. I return backward the right foot which is in front, and I will beat your lance out of the way toward the right-hand side. My thrust will not fail: yours will be the failure.

I appear in the Stance of the Casement Window on the Left; if I don't strike you with the point, you will have a good deal. I will hold the point high and I will carry my arms low, and I will step out of the way to the left-hand side with the foot that is behind. Then I will thrust the point in your face without any possible defense. We three Masters can all make the play which is after me; once you try it, you will not want to taste any more.

The play of the lance finishes here and I make it from the left side; I am besotted with their plays. These three guards which were previously make me think that they will not fail against lance extended or shortened, for they are guards of great protection (in that in one jaunt they make both defense and offense).

And the counter to this thrust can be done well. When the thrust is broken with that strike, the butt should be turned [forward]. This may be enough of the play of the lance.

[In the Morgan, the Master is missing his crown.]

### Notes

- Four pairs of figures in two registers showing lance/spear combat
- Upper register shows two pairs of figures wielding long weapons (lances) in high and extended guards
- Lower register shows paired combat with shorter pole weapons or half-sword techniques
- Page number "42" visible in upper right corner (original foliation)
- This page continues the pollaxe or lance section of the treatise
- Text is particularly difficult to transcribe on this page due to faded ink and the dense cursive hand
- Many readings are highly uncertain; this page would benefit from comparison with other manuscript witnesses
- The word "fioritore/fioritura" (flowering) appears frequently, likely referring to techniques or the author Fiore's name
