## Folio 41r
**Section:** Mounted Fencing

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top:
¶ Ie porto una Lanza in posta de donna la sinestra cum la tuta bene acorpiata / che la piu ben couertar . ¶ Si mi uenra Lanza per gli occhi contra / io la rebattero e feriolo cum la mia punta . ¶ E tal sor inganno faro cum lo mio cauallo ben in ordene / che stia in ordene di Podere / che alla incuntrada usciria in man . e la sua lanza de lo piu baso leuare e cortrar. E lo piu largo / e la sua bassa . Chi mi uol ferir non po compir / Che per lanza a rebatter / per lo stabile leuare de me e gran / moder posse . ¶ E nessun gli po far del stu. che h[?]

Bottom:
¶ Questo fio lo contrario delle guardie e lanza che denançi / e quando loro contra uenano dalarme a ferro stretto . e Io li rebatto la lanza . e fero lor sempre basso in scontro de conzalluego / poy che la lanza longha e bissa e fiancha las Remedion e / volta la lanza sotto la Punta e schavrir [?] primerela / basso la sua . p che la contra no si possi rebatter et in lanzare / e lo stabile che dou e depressia .

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top:
I carry a lance in the guard of the lady (posta di donna) on the left, with the body well covered, which provides the best cover. If a lance comes toward my eyes, I will beat it aside and strike him with my point. And such a deception I will make with my horse well in order, that it stands ready in Power, that at the encounter I will come to hand. And his lance from the lowest position I will lift and counter. And the widest, and his low one. He who would strike me cannot succeed, for by beating aside the lance, by my stable position and great power. And no one can do anything about this.

Bottom:
This is the counter to the guards and lance shown before, and when they come against each other in close combat with weapons. And I beat aside the lance and always strike them low in the encounter of engagement, since the long lance is low and strikes the flank as a Remedy, and turns the lance under the point and first uncovers, lowering his. So that the counter cannot be beaten aside and in the throw, and the stable position that holds and presses.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[3] In the Boar's Tusk I carry my lance;
To beat and to strike are always my method.

This is the counter to the previous play when one rides against another with sharp steel, but one has a shorter lance than the other. When he who has the shorter lance carries it low in the Boar’s Tusk, then he with the longer lance should similarly carry his lance low, as drawn here, so that the short lance cannot beat aside the long lance.

### Notes

- Four mounted figures shown: two in the top register and two in the bottom register, all on horseback with lances.
- Page numbered "43" in upper right corner (old foliation).
- Top pair shows two riders in posta di donna; bottom pair shows the counter technique.
- Some text is difficult to read due to ink fading, particularly at the end of the top text block.

## Folio 41v
**Section:** Mounted Fencing

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top:
¶ Questa e la terza guardia de Lanza . dita Lanza . Questa maestra a contra Lanza . e ala guerra . E possa de donna la Plonfera . come seri vedesti . e vedureti ala via le compagnie .

Bottom:
¶ Anchora questa maestra porta le sie Lanze e posta de donna la Plonfera . E rebattere la lanza che la compira che inta Longijana . E possa rebatter e ella lanza fiorire e questo fin lanza . Lanze plonz / e quello fin tuto basterio a corno perche fio la ponta .

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top:
This is the third guard of the lance, called Lance. This master [teaches] against the Lance and in war. And the guard of the lady the Plunging one, as you have seen and will see along the way with the companions.

Bottom:
Also this master carries her lances in the guard of the lady the Plunging one. And to beat aside the lance that will come from the long distance. And she can beat aside and with the lance flourish, and this ends the lance. Lances plunging, and that ends all that is sufficient at the horn because it is the point.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[4] *Because of the short lance that I hold, I come in the Stance of the Queen:
To beat and to strike, I hold myself certain.*

This is another way to carry your lance when fighting another lance. This Master has a short lance, so he carries it in Guard of the Lady on the left as you can see, so he can beat aside his opponent’s weapon and strike him.

| 
This Master also carries his lance in Guard of the Lady on the left, in order to knock aside the spear his opponent is about to throw at him. Just as he can beat it aside using his lance, so too he could beat it aside using a staff or a short sword.

[In the Pisani Dossi, the Master on the right is missing his crown.]

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

Behold! I come, holding the lance in the Woman’s [Position] at the chest.
I do not fear touching the earth with pliant knees.
And I would strike a bargain by staining, nevertheless your lance will thrust forward.

This is another way to carry the lance. This Master has a short lance and carries it in the Stance of the Queen on the Left as you can see, to beat and then to strike his companion.

Again, this Master carries his lance in the Stance of the Queen on the Left to beat the lance that the companion wants to throw. And that beat which he wants to strike with the lance he could also do with a staff or with a sword—except that if he throws his lance into the chest of my horse, my beat will be turned to failure.

[In the Morgan, the Master on the right is missing his crown.]

### Notes

- Four mounted figures: two in top register, two in bottom register.
- Top-right and bottom-right figures wear crowns (masters).
- The figures demonstrate lance guards and counters on horseback.
- Text is somewhat faded and difficult to read in places.
- No old foliation number visible on this verso page.

## Folio 42r
**Section:** Mounted Fencing

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top:
¶ Io nesso maistro che fago che e armado e filo largo cauallo corrente . e Sempre vuy battagliando lo porriero cum la sua lanza in do . E si farme de compaignia . ¶ Et alla Rebattuda della ponta e parte / lo posta da brutto de houare la guardiana de Duch lanza . ouero in posta de houare la Plonfera . e voltare e Ferir e per lor de la ponta . rossa / lanza cossi doi lanza .

Bottom:
¶ Dou questo portare de spada corrono lanza . e malto fare e rebattere la lanza . contracouallo de lo la posse / strode della compaignia . E questo che intra [?] che / forno contracouallo lo ferro contra . mandandogle per atro / chi . Vafframi . Spada . e corona .

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top:
I am the master who acts armed and broadly on the running horse, and always you battling, I will carry with his lance in two. And to form a company. And at the beat-aside of the point and part, the guard of the ugly one to raise the guard of the Duke's lance, or in the guard of raising the Plunging one, and turn and strike and for them from the point, red lance thus two lances.

Bottom:
Where this carrying of the sword counters the lance, and greatly to make and beat aside the lance, counter-horse from the power/street of the company. And this one who enters, who were counter-horse the iron against, sending them for another. Champion. Sword and crown.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[6] *Fleeing, I cannot make any other defense
And so I turn myself to the right and will make you offense.*

This master who is fleeing is not wearing armor and rides a horse built for speed, and as he flees he constantly throws his lance point behind him so as to strike at his opponent. And if were to turn his horse to the right he could quickly enter into the Boar’s Tusk guard with his lance, or he could take the left side Guard of the Lady, to beat aside his opponent’s weapon and finish him in similar fashion to the first and the third plays of the lance.

[In the Pisani Dossi, the Master is missing his crown.]

[7] *With my sword, I will beat your lance,
And with either the point or the edge I will strike you.*

This method of carrying the sword against the lance is well suited for beating aside your opponent’s lance when you are passing him on his right side. And this guard is effective against all hand held weapons, namely pole axe, staff, sword etc.

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

Correct in opposition, I would make you strong pains.
Whoever &lt;I&gt; runs away cannot defend his own body.

This Master who flees is not armored and is on a running horse, and he is always throwing thrusts with his lance backward to strike his companion. And if he were to turn to the right side he could easily enter into the Boar's Tusk with his lance or into the Stance of the Queen on the Left, and beat and strike as he could do in the first and third plays of the lance [on foot].

The regal Form of the Woman is suitable, and piercing you
With the sword and raging against [you], this spirit sends 
To the shadows; the divine will of heaven would favor that manner.

This carry of the sword against the lance is very good for beating the lance while riding to the right side of your companion. And this guard is good against all other handheld weapons—that is, against the ax, the staff, the sword, and so forth.

[Morgan text accompanies subsequent pairing.]

### Notes

- Four mounted combat figures: two in top register, two in bottom register.
- Page numbered "44" in upper right corner (old foliation).
- Top-left figure carries a lance; top-right wears a crown (master) and holds a lance.
- Bottom-left figure appears to carry a lance in a forward thrust; bottom-right figure holds a sword.
- The text discusses the interplay between sword and lance on horseback.
- Some words are difficult to decipher due to the script style and fading.

## Folio 42v
**Section:** Mounted Fencing

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top:
¶ Questo fio contrario del Zugho che breuigo / che questo contrar che la lanza . la ponta bassa . p fora lo caualle . o in la testa . o in lo petto . ne la / compagnia chi po rebatter chi la stade anda basso .

Bottom:
¶ Io nesso e un altro contrario del lanza contra / spada . che questo tola lanza . uoltare e metter sua lanza / sotto la ponta contra . p che chi po rebattere . / ma lanza . ¶ E fon molto ponto fiore chi piu lanza . quella della spada .

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top:
This is the counter to the Play that I describe, that this counter with the lance, the point low, through the horse, or in the head, or in the chest. In the company he who can beat aside, who stands low.

Bottom:
I am another counter of the lance against the sword, that this one takes the lance, turns and puts his lance under the opposing point, so that he who can beat aside, but the lance. And it was a very fine point, Fiore, he who [has] more lance, that of the sword.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[8] *I make the counter to your guard,
And your horse I will strike without any trouble.*

This is the counter to the previous play. This Master attacks with his lance held low in order to strike his opponent’s horse either in the head or the chest, and the opponent will be unable to beat aside such a low attack with his sword.

[In the Getty, the Master on the right is missing his crown. In the Pisani Dossi, both Masters are missing their crowns.]

[9] *So that you do not beat my lance out of the way,
Under my left arm I carry it in rest.*

This is another counter of lance versus sword. In this one, the man with the lance couches his lance under his left arm, so that his lance cannot be beaten aside. And in this way he will be able to strike the man with the sword with his lance.

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

This is the counter to the play that came before. And this Master with the lance carries it low to strike the horse in the head and in the chest, because his companion cannot reach so low with his sword.

Again this is another counter of lance against sword. He of the lance sets his lance in rest under his left arm so that his lance cannot be beaten aside. And in this fashion he can strike him of the sword with his lance.

[In the Morgan, the Master's opponent is wearing a crown.]

### Notes

- Four mounted figures: two in top register, two in bottom register.
- Top-left figure wears a crown (master) and rides a rearing horse with lance; top-right holds a lance.
- Bottom pair shows two armored riders on horseback with lances engaging at close range.
- This page continues the discussion of lance vs. sword counters on horseback.
- The text is relatively brief compared to some other pages.

## Folio 43r
**Section:** Mounted Fencing

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top:
¶ Io nesso ella la Spada . Guardia questa alla la logi / ella guerra alo leuare de caualluçaro . Cum in questo della lanza . e lo cauallo apresso . lo maistro de la Spada / insegna fira lanza in fiorir . in contra quatro . Remedie . el fierir se per lo malstre sono la Spada e sallo questo / a menar e ferine de ben arlene de Spada .

Bottom:
¶ Io nesso e lo contrario della taglia de lanza . e dou / Spado che dissepare . que che questo dia la lanza . e fora . lo contra la rimedio della fior e breuigo . Se questo della Spada . e che chi po dissipare la lanza cum la / spada si bassa .

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top:
I am the Sword. This guard at the place of war at the rising of the horseman. With this of the lance and the horse near, the master of the Sword teaches to strike the lance in the flourish, in counter four Remedies. And the striking through the master are the Sword and knows this, to lead and strike from the good art of the Sword.

Bottom:
I am the counter of the cut of the lance, and where the Sword separates, that this one gives the lance and outside. The counter the remedy of the flower and I describe. If this of the Sword, and he who can separate the lance with the sword so low.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[10] *At mid-lance thus I come, well-enclosed
So that you will delay in beating my lance.
I trust I will strike your horse without fail;
You will see my play carried out hereafter.*

[In the Pisani Dossi, the Master on the right is missing his crown.]

| 
Here the man with the sword awaits the man with the lance, and he is waiting in the Boar’s Tusk guard. As the man with the lance approaches him, the Master with the sword beats aside the lance to the right side, covering and striking with one turn of the sword.

[In the Getty, the Master on the left is missing his crown.]

[11] *So that you cannot cross your sword with my [weapon],
I carry it low to waste your horse.*

This is the counter to the preceding play of lance versus sword. Here the man with the lance strikes his opponent’s (the man with the sword) horse in the head, because he cannot beat aside the lance with his sword since it is too low.

[In the Pisani Dossi, the Master is missing his crown.]

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

Drawing the members close at the same time, I, the harsh one, seize the javelin &lt;I delay the javelin&gt;
In the middle. You will have been hindered in breaking [me] open. Finally,
Your steed , having been struck a lethal wound, will depart.

[In the Paris, the Master on the right is missing his crown.]

This one with the sword awaits him with the lance. He waits in the Boar's Tusk as he with the lance comes, and then the Master with the sword beats his lance away toward the right side. And thus can the Master do with the sword—that is, he can cover in one rotation of the sword.

[In the Morgan, the Master on the left is missing his crown.]

This is the counter of the play of the lance and the sword that came before: that is, that he with the lance strikes to the head of the horse of his enemy (that is, of him with the sword), because he cannot beat a lance or sword which is so low.

[In the Morgan, the Master's opponent wears a crown.]

### Notes

- Four mounted figures: two in top register, two in bottom register.
- Page numbered "45" in upper right corner (old foliation).
- Top-left figure is armored with sword; top-right wears a crown (master) and carries weapons.
- Bottom pair shows a mounted lance thrust being countered; the left figure has struck with a lance low, the right figure sits mounted.
- This section deals with sword vs. lance techniques on horseback.
- Text is faded and partially illegible in several places.

## Folio 43v
**Section:** Mounted Fencing

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top:
¶ Anchora altro portare de Spada fio e dissipara possa de cola / lanzaro . e fio motto done contra lanza . e contra sercola [?] / e contracauallo di suo per e spada / rolla star trinciare . ¶ E come hoy a menar ello la pena . a la testa . manda felo sesso . ¶ E lo colpo de ferendo [?] e ricamasso . ella se plu da fontario contra / parte la spada dello fior contrigo . e poi fiore chi corgo / frontudo lo figamo lo eparsse .

Bottom:
¶ Anchora questa spada . fone quadra . de estaba leuare / fio lanza . e gialdo hoy che foni incumanar . ella spada . de / lanza manda como a moi presta contra armezada . E posta . / dou questo e qui contra . e contra rotta chi po de porere . / dorese . a la por manda . ¶ E per notte questi / che la rebia . in contra Spada dello fior leuare posse . / la colla lanza chi po e diuoto de colla lanza .

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top:
Also another carry of the Sword done and separated, guard of the lancer, and done with many women [techniques] against lance and against circling, and counter-horse from his power and sword, the standing cut shall be. And as today to lead with the pain to the head, send the blow itself. And the striking blow and recovered, and if more from afar against, parts the sword of the flower against. And then Fiore who runs, a frontal we figure the parting.

Bottom:
Also this sword, it was square, from the stable rising the lance, and the shield today that was beginning, and the sword, from lance sends as to me ready against armored. And guard, where this and here counter, and counter broken he who can from power, backs to the sending. And for the night those who have rage, in counter Sword of the flower rising I can, the with the lance he who can and devoted with the lance.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[12] *Such a carry of the sword gives me four plays to make:
I could strike with the point and the edge without fail,
And also throw someone from horseback or take his sword.
Seldom are these things failures to me.
*

This way of carrying the sword is named “the Long Tail Guard”. When you are riding to your opponent’s right side, this is a very good guard to use against the lance and all other hand held weapons. Keep firmly in your mind that thrusts and strikes from the left side should be beaten aside to your outside line, beating them diagonally upwards, not vertically. And the downward strikes should similarly be beaten aside to the outside, lifting your opponent’s sword a little as you do so. You can make these plays as these drawings show.

[13] *Of these two guards I make no comparison;
Whoever knows more, his judgment will overcome.
And whoever will know to watch for deception
Will be able to make the four aforesaid plays well.*

This version of the Long Tail Guard is a good guard when your opponent attacks you from his sword on his left shoulder, as this opponent is shown doing here. And be advised that this guard will work against all attacks from both the right and the left sides, and against anyone, whether right handed or left handed. Hereafter begin the plays from the Long Tail that always begin with beating aside the opponent’s weapon, as you saw drawn in the first guard of the Long Tail.

[In the Getty, the Master on the left is missing his crown.]

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

Truly there are four ways of carrying a sword;
Verily he moves toward the plays. And by means of the sharp point being forward,
I would hit you. And he will cut the open limbs with cutting,
And again from your seat you will plainly depart 
Without a sword. And that method seldom disappoints a man.

This carry of the sword is called the Stance of the Long Tail, and it is very good against lance and sword and against all other handheld weapons, while riding to the right side of the enemy. Bear in mind well that the thrusts and the backhand blows should be beaten out to the side and not upward, and the downward blows should also be beaten to the side (lifting the sword of the enemy slightly); [this guard] can make all the plays corresponding to the figures that are depicted.

Again this same Stance of the Long Tail is good when one comes against you with the sword on the left-hand side, as this enemy of mine does, and know that this guard counters all blows from the right side and from the left side, and counters anyone, be they right- or left-handed. And hereafter commence the plays of the Long Tail, which always beats in the fashion that was said earlier in the first Guard of the Long Tail.

### Notes

- Four mounted figures: two in top register, two in bottom register.
- Top-left figure holds a raised sword; top-right wears a crown (master) with weapon.
- Bottom pair shows two riders at a walk, both carrying weapons.
- The page continues sword vs. lance mounted combat techniques.
- Text is particularly difficult in this section; many readings are uncertain.
- The Venetian dialect and abbreviations make precise transcription challenging.

## Folio 44r
**Section:** Mounted Fencing

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top-left:
¶ Questo e la prima Zugho che eisse de la guardia / piciola lanza che qui monstro . che stabille molto ro / tara la Spada . Delle sue riuoltare . e metterse cha la posta / in lo ponto . ouero in la sosta como qui dipartito .

Top-right:
¶ Questo fio la segonda Zugho che per do / quatro reponere . le Remi contrari dopano la testa / che vogi a lor delli chi e mortale ala testa .

Bottom-left:
¶ Questo e un altro Zugho lo terço . che rebattudo / ella Spada della fior dissipare . alla propria . e la manere / ponto che filo alla testa la Spada . e messi chi possi fiorir / lo ponto .

Bottom-right:
¶ Questo fio la quarto Zugho che la Podeno / chi sol ferir . la taglia . a trar che la Spada . p fillo / modo che caderno que impartito .

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
This is the first Play that comes from the guard of the small lance that I show here, that stable greatly will turn the Sword. Of its turnings, and to put oneself in the position at the point, or in the pause as here departed.

Top-right:
This is the second Play that for the four repositions, the Remedy-counters cover the head, that they wish to strike them who is mortal to the head.

Bottom-left:
This is another Play, the third, that having beaten aside the Sword of the flower to separate, at the proper one, and the manner point that strikes at the head the Sword, and put who can flourish the point.

Bottom-right:
This is the fourth Play that the Power who alone will strike, the cut, to draw the Sword, in such a manner that they fall here departed.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[15] *This point I gladly have set in your throat
Per the third Master [13] who demonstrates such a guard.*

This is the first play that comes from the Long Tail Guard shown above. Here the Master beats aside his opponent’s sword, and then places a thrust into his chest or his face, as you see drawn here.

[16] *Per the first Master that is in guard with the sword
I have given this strike to your head.*

This is the second play that you can do after beating aside your opponent’s weapon. Here I strike this man over the head, because I see his head is unarmored.

[17] *By crossing ahead of your sword I have deviated it
And with mine I have given a great blow:
And also I could have given it to you with my point;
And none of the weapons that you have amount to anything to me.*

This is the another play, the third, where, after beating aside your opponent’s sword, you grab it with your left hand and strike him in the head. You could also strike him with a thrust.

[18] *You will lose your sword because of this catch
Or you will go to the ground without any defense.*

This is the fourth play, in which the student strikes his opponent in the head and then takes his sword in the manner shown here.

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

I pierced through the exposed neck with the point of my sword.
For instance, the third master taught me thoroughly using principles.

This is the first play which belongs to the Guard of the Long Tail which appeared here before: that is, that the Master beats the sword of his enemy and thrusts the point into his chest, or into his face as depicted here.

[In the Paris, the Scholar wears a crown.]

Using a wound, I, the fighting one, terrify the neck with a wound.
Prudent with regard to this sword, the first master teaches me truly.

This is the second play which can give a beat. I strike this man over the head, for I see well that he is not armored on his head.

[In the Paris, the Scholar wears a crown.]

Here is another play, which is the third that beats the sword of his enemy; he grasps with his left hand and strikes the [enemy's] head, and he could also strike thusly with the point.

You, shamefaced, on account of this will either perhaps abandon your sword,
Or you will lie down, prostrate on the ground; there is no preventing [this].

This is the fourth play that the scholar wants to make—that is, take the sword in this way that you can see depicted here.

[In the Paris, the Scholar wears a crown.]

### Notes

- Four mounted combat pairs, one in each quadrant of the page.
- Page numbered "46" in upper right corner (old foliation).
- Each pair demonstrates a numbered play (zugho): first through fourth.
- All figures are mounted and engaged in sword combat on horseback.
- The plays appear to be sequential techniques from a single guard (guardia piciola lanza).
- Top-left and bottom-left text blocks have red initial capitals; top-right and bottom-right have blue/dark initials.

## Folio 44v
**Section:** Mounted Fencing

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top-left:
¶ Questo fio la quinto Zugho che fio la coltra / [?] taglia ferire de la mandritto / al la star talhare fallaro . deno tuta la Spada ella / la mettere piana lo fallare cum lo metter de lo ferir / fio la propria Zugho . Ben che plando contrario de / dou fio chi taglia .

Top-right:
¶ Questo fio lo sextaro che vol tuta la Spada al co / pogno . ala la contrario de la Spada battire manda sempre / Lanzaro in gra . ¶ Bella mano chi caderno la Spada . E / zorno .

Bottom-left:
¶ Questo fio la Setterna Zugho che sedussi / del quesso . lo ferire chi fio la segondo / alo stabille volta . ¶ E lo compaignie fatti contrario de / ala stra volla . ¶ E lo propria Zugho Ben che plando contrario de / dou fio chi taglia .

Bottom-right:
¶ Questo fio la octaua Zugho che monstro la . e tutti / chi corgo . che no fiassi donante . e chi reuolçario tu della . / lanza che lo spar fio lanza . ¶ Chi quello lo manda e [?] / moltare forno su la lanza . Cauan lo chi plu che forno . / possa contra rasoro cayo . e fiorir chi me riuoltare . cresta / e ponta la lo costro . e per so passo questo profito / della la ponta e Rondo chi fiorir dou lo testa . e / zorno .

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
This is the fifth Play that is the quilted [?] cut striking from the right-hand blow, to the standing cutting and failing. Giving all the Sword and putting it flat, the failing with the putting of the strike is the proper Play. Well that flat counter from where it is the cut.

Top-right:
This is the sixth that wants all the Sword at the grip, at the counter of the Sword beating sends always the lancer greatly. Beautiful hand who drops the Sword. And the day.

Bottom-left:
This is the seventh Play that reduces from the [?], the striking that is the second, to the stable turn. And the company made counter from the other turn. And the proper Play well that flat counter from where it is the cut.

Bottom-right:
This is the eighth Play that I show, and all who run, that there is no gift, and who will turn you from the lance that the spar is lance. Who that sends and greatly turns upon the lance. Horseman who more than turns, can counter razor fall, and flourish who turns me, crest and point at the side. And for his step this profit of the point and Round who flourishes where the head. And the day.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[19] *So that my sword would not be taken from me
Against you I have made this turn:
Such that that which you were wanting to do to me
Through this counter I will do to you.*

This is the sixth [fifth] play, where you take away your opponent’s sword. You use the hilt of your sword to lift his hilt upwards, which will make his sword fall from his hands.

[20] *From horse to ground it will behoove you to go;
Maybe I will then know what I should do with you.*

This is the fifth [sixth] play that flows from the cover where you beat aside his sword. Here I throw my arm around his neck and turn quickly, and with the base of my sword I drive him to the ground.

My counter is the second play that follows me, but this counter will not work if your opponent is armored.

[21] *If it would behoove me to go to the ground, [sword] and all,
I could do no defense other than this strike.*

This is the seventh play, which is the counter to the fifth [sixth] play above. It employs a strike to your opponent’s leg. But if your opponent is armored, you can’t trust this counter to work.

[22] *I want to make my defense against the point and the edge,
Such that the sword will not be taken from me nor caught,
And neither will I be thrown to the ground from my horse:
I will strike your face with my pommel without fail.*

This is the eighth play, which is the counter to all of the preceding plays, but especially the plays of the mounted sword when the masters are in the Long Tail guard. When the Masters or their students are in this guard, and when I strike or thrust at them, and when they quickly beat my attack aside, then I quickly turn my sword and strike them in the face with my pommel. Then I move quickly from my position and strike them in the back of the head with a horizontal backhand strike.

[The Pisani Dossi shows the horse position from the Paris and the hand position from the Getty.]

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

This is the fifth play, in which he wants to take the sword of his companion with the hilt of his sword; the other hilt he will have above, and the sword will fall from [his companion's] hand for certain.

He disengages lest I trample the beating heart on the ground.
Anything that I would like concerning you I will be able to try afterward.

This is the sixth play that makes a cover with the beating of the sword. I throw my arm to his neck and quickly turn, and I will throw you to the ground, sword and all, without a doubt.

My counter is here after and is the seventh play. Well that he has not achieved being armored.

[In the Paris, the Scholar wears a crown.]

This is the seventh play which is the counter—that is, the strike that he makes to the leg of the other one. If your companion were armored, you could not rely on this.

[In the Morgan, the Master is missing his crown.]

I now protect myself from the cutting, and also the strong point.
And I strike the face with the hilt lest this sword be seized
From me. I shall not yet be thrown to the farthest ground.

This is the eighth play and it is the counter to all the plays that came before, and especially of the plays of the sword on horseback and of the Masters that are in the Guard of the Long Tail. And when the Masters or Scholars stand in the aforesaid guard and I strike with a thrust or another blow, and they quickly beat my sword, I immediately give a turn to my sword and with my pommel I strike them in the face. And I can pass with my cover quickly and strike them behind the head with a backhand middle cut.

[The Morgan shows the horse position from the Paris and the hand position from the Getty.]

### Notes

- Four mounted combat pairs, one in each quadrant.
- Continues the numbered plays: fifth through eighth.
- All figures are mounted and engaged in close combat with swords and lances.
- The eighth play (bottom-right) has a notably longer text block than the others.
- Red and blue initial capitals alternate between text blocks.
- Text is challenging to read; many passages remain uncertain.

## Folio 45r
**Section:** Mounted Fencing

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top-left:
¶ Io nomo fior che fago contra lo contrario che / fior mondo . ¶ Che questo dello sua contra che fior stado . / fior la sua contrarissimo usciria . come usciria Septate / la ponta el esser in la testa . in chon tuto açon . offo / la spada . e fiora e vender . E lo questo me flabte aloçi de / la testa . Nella resguarda della Spada . lo ditto in lo fior. / Rende . ¶ I questa Lanzairo [?] la contracauallo de / ala spada e fiorda . la pou in . poi Dui . Vera bosse .

Top-right:
¶ Questo e questo de Abruzzaro . per dou falcade / brauça . e chi stadi grandi . Che . Caderna . fiora de Spada . fior de pagno . ne che alo ella este fina stabil . / Serena ne la pugni . me la figarnada . i delle beuanze / la parte . a Podena . e chi ella contrape . e qui lo lanza / brauça in ponta . e lo chi e fiore . chi più . e tutto molto / alla stabille . chi e fiore delli . e che modo fion sti ferir / la ponta contrada .

Bottom-left:
¶ Questo e contrario del Zugho che denouere chi / renouij . e chi molto figuriro . contrado in chi zagotro . pi che so / ala spada . quella alla breze la pugni . E lo sono [?] de la / zagalta . dopno saltare . demeramente . E ello la brauça e posso / grellare lo la fior . contraste .

Bottom-right:
¶ Questo findare sorda de lui ostare questo ha contrade / la alo stabille la pugni . e la plonfera . la brazzo da esso . / lonto chi sso [?] . al lo contra ella lagarda . e gonato . in della / della zugacha . ¶ E lo chi sta ella Ello dou fior plonfera . e la / e per la pugni . chi stabille elle donere . e per me . / contra . a Simillo Pedra . chi questa chi donomo . e per / ponta .

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
I am named Fiore who acts against the counter that Fiore [shows to the] world. That this of his counter that Fiore has established. His counter shall come out, as shall come out severed the point and being in the head, with all action. I hold the sword and Fiore and to sell. And this one makes me appear at the place of the head. In the rearguard of the Sword, the said in the flower. Render. And this lancer, the counter-horse of the sword and flowers, the power in, then Two. True stroke.

Top-right:
This is the technique of the Abruzzare, for where the arm sweeps and who stands great. That the fallen, flower of the Sword, flower of the cloth, that at this end remains stable. Serene in the fist, my figure, and of the beverages the part, at Power, and who counters, and here the lance arm in point, and who is the flower, who more, and all very much at the stable, who is the flower of them. And what manner these are to strike the point countered.

Bottom-left:
This is the counter of the Play that denounces who renews, and who much figured, countered in who engages, more than at the sword, that at the arm the fist. And the [sound?] of the engagement, must leap, demeaningly. And the arm and I can grind the flower, counters.

Bottom-right:
This find a deaf one of him to obstruct, this has countered at the stable the fist and the plunging one, the arm from him, far who [knows?], at the counter the guard and [kneeled?], in the engagement. And who stands there where the flower plunges, and the for the fist, who stable the giving, and for me, counter, to Similar Stone, who this who gives. And point.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[23] *So that you could not hit me in the face with your pommel,

I have taken your blow with the hilt of my sword.*

I am the ninth play, which is the counter to the counter that preceded me. When he turns his sword, I quickly place my hilt as you see drawn here, so that he cannot strike me in the face with his pommel. And if I raise my sword up, and turn it to the left, you could well have your sword taken away. And if I am unable to do that, I could instead strike you with a backhand strike to the face, or with a quick turn of my sword strike you in the head with my pommel.

Here ends the plays of sword against sword on horseback. If you know more of this, please share it.

[This Master is missing his crown. In the Getty, the player is wearing the garter rather than the Master.]

[24] *In such a way have I grabbed you, running up behind,
That I will throw you from the horse—this I believe.*

This is a grappling play, that is a play of the arms, and this is how you do it: if your opponent is fleeing from you, you come up behind him to his left side. Now with your right hand grab the cheek piece of his bascinet, or if he is unarmored, grab him by the hair or by the right arm from behind his shoulder. In this way you will make him fall backwards to the ground.

[In the Getty and Pisani Dossi, the Master is missing his crown.]

[25] *You wanted to throw me from my horse
But with this counter you will go to the ground instead.*

This is the counter to the previous play, and that play will not work when this counter is quickly applied as follows: when he grabs you from behind you quickly switch hands on the reins, and with your left hand you lock him up as shown here.

[26] *I want to lift your leg with the stirrup,
And because of this, to the ground you will go.*

This student is about to throw his opponent off his horse, by grabbing the stirrup and pulling it upwards. If his opponent does not fall to the ground, he’ll be helpless in the air, and unless his opponent is tied to his horse, this play will not fail him. If he does not have his foot in the stirrup, the student can grab him by the ankle and raise him up into the air in the same way, as I described above.

[In the Pisani Dossi, the Scholar wears a crown.]

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

The ninth I am, who makes the counter to that which came before me, so that when he gives a turn to his sword I quickly thrust my hilt (as you see depicted) so that he cannot strike me in the face with his pommel. And if I raise my sword high and give a turn to the left, it could very well be that his sword will be taken from him. And if that fails me and I cannot do it, so quickly will I make the turn that I will give to his face with the false edge of my sword (or I will strike him in the head with my pommel).

This finishes the mounted play of sword against sword, and whoever keeps it in mind will give a good deal.

[In the Morgan, the player is wearing the garter rather than the Master.]

I hold you captured by the helmet, whereby you turn your back backward.
Afterward, I will send you with flying chest into the ground.

This is a play of grappling, and inasmuch as it is a play of grappling it is a play of the arms, and it is done in this way: when one flees from you and you come up behind him from the left side, grab him on the cheek of his helmet with your right hand (or, if he is unhelmed, grab him by the hair or the right arm from behind his shoulder), and in this way you will make him fall backward such that you will make him go to the ground.

[In the Morgan, the Master is missing his crown.]

It is useful that you merely beat the ground
With the trampled corpse. The counter actions accomplish this. Spiteful,
You nevertheless wish to attempt that same thing on myself.

This is the counter to the play that came before; this counter goes in this way with the catch that was made: that is, that quickly when he grabs him from behind, [the Master] should immediately exchange the hand on the reins, and with his left arm he should grab him in this fashion.

Lifting the leg simultaneously by the stirrup, this, my Powerful right [hand], turns you to the furthest. Nor will your leg be made better.

This Scholar wants to throw this one from his horse—that is, he grabs him by the stirrup and lifts him up. If he doesn't go to the ground, he would clearly be floating in the air! Assuming he isn't lashed to his horse, this play cannot fail. If he does not have his foot in a stirrup, grab him by the ankle and it will be even easier to lift him up than I said before so do as was written here earlier.

[In the Paris, the Scholar wears a crown.]

### Notes

- Four mounted combat pairs, one in each quadrant.
- Page numbered "47" in upper right corner (old foliation), though partially obscured.
- Top-left text has a red/blue initial; top-right has a blue initial; bottom-left has a red initial; bottom-right has a blue initial.
- All figures are mounted and engaged in sword/lance combat at close quarters.
- The text on this page is extremely difficult to read; many words are uncertain.
- References to "Abruzzare" (the grappling/seizing technique) appear in the top-right text.

## Folio 45v
**Section:** Mounted Fencing

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top-left:
¶ E contrario del zugho Rimedio . cum e brazzo / del lo suo pugni / fior la sua contrarissimo usciria . como usçiria dopo septare / la ponta . lo esser in la testa . Che possa leuare la spada / e chi la testa . in Posta e fioro e assere . E lo questo me flabte e la / la spada . e fioro e vender . e lo questo me flabte . assar fiore mesalte / ala stabille . la po uo . me per manno . Dui . Vera bosse .

Top-right:
¶ Questo fio contrario del Zugho . lo dou Beuanze . e contrasti / la ala contrario . de la Spada battire . metti / Serena in la pugni . me le fegernada delle beuanze le lo elle / la parte . a Podena . e chi lo contrape la qui e lanza / brauça in ponta . e lo fior . chi e stabille . e chi molto fionda / ala pugni . ¶ E qui fioro . chi e stabille . chi metti fiori delli / la Ponta . e Rondo . chi fiore . dou la testa . E / zorno .

Bottom-left:
¶ Questo e contrario del Zugho che lo renouore che / renouij . e che molto figuriro contrado in chi ello . e in me la / alla stade . quello chi le preghe . E lo sono de la / zagalta . dopo saltar . Fiore mando . ¶ E lo brauça e posso / grellare . lo lo fiore . contraste .

Bottom-right:
¶ Questo findare souda de lui . ostare questo la contrasse / lo e ello stabille la pugni . e la plonfera . E lo brazzo de esso e / lo modo chi elo contra le Zagarda . e gonato le della / della zugacha . E lo stadi . ello . e la brauça e posso fio la / la gonde [?] . chi lo por fiore . e por de tuto . e per stabille . / a le chi del contra . ¶ E per posso dou quello de donarre e la / ponta .

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
And the counter of the Play Remedy, with the arm of his fist. His greatest counter shall come out, as shall come out after severing the point, the being in the head. That [one] can raise the sword and who the head, in Guard and flower and to be. And this one makes me appear, and the sword, and flower and to sell, and this makes me appear, the stable. The power, by my hand. Two. True stroke.

Top-right:
This is the counter of the Play, the where Beverages, and counters at the counter of the Sword beating, puts Serene in the fist, my figures of the beverages the parts, at Power, and who counters here the lance arm in point, and the flower, who is stable, and who much slings at the fist. And here flower, who is stable, who puts flowers of them the Point and Round, who flowers where the head. And the day.

Bottom-left:
This is the counter of the Play that the renewal that renews, and that much figured countered in who he, and in me the standing, that who the prayers. And the sound of the engagement, after leaping, Fiore sends. And the arm and I can grind, the flower, counters.

Bottom-right:
This finds a firm one of him, to obstruct this the countered, and the stable the fist and the plunging one. And the arm of him and the manner who counters the Guard, and knelt the engagement. And the standing, and the arm and I can do the [?], who for flower, and for all, and for stable, to who of the counter. And for I can where that of giving and the point.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[27] *You wanted to throw me well from my horse;
With this counter, to the ground you will go.*

Here is the counter to the previous play: when your opponent grabs your stirrup or your foot, throw your arm quickly around his neck, and in this way you will be able to unhorse him. Follow this advice and he’ll end up on the ground for sure.

[28] *I want to throw you and your horse to the ground;
The chest of mine will go to the crupper of yours:
I will not release the bit of your horse,
And in the end you will not avoid the ground;
And when one is well-armored, this is a fine hold,
Because an offense cannot be made with weapons.*

This is a method of throwing your opponent to the ground by throwing his horse. It’s done like this: when you and your mounted opponent close, ride to his right side. Then throw your right arm over the neck of his horse, and grab the bridle close to where the bit enters its mouth, and forcefully wrench it upwards and over. At the same time make sure your horse’s shoulders drive into his horse’s haunches In this way you will bring down both him and his horse at the same time.

[In the Pisani Dossi, the Scholar is wearing a crown.]

[29] This is the counter to the play before, where you throw your opponent to the ground together with his horse. This is an easy counter: when the student throws his arm over the neck of your horse to grab the bridle, you should quickly throw your arm around the student’s neck, and you will effectively make him let go. Just do as the drawing shows.

[30] *I seek to take the bridle from your hands
And I want to throw it over the head of your horse:
And when the bridle will be thrown over its head,
With my position I will lead you to a different country.*

In this play you take the reins of your opponent’s horse out of his hands, as you see drawn here. When you and your mounted opponent close, ride to his right side, and throw your right arm over his horse’s neck and grab the reins near his left hand with your right hand turned down. Now pull the reins over his horse’s head. This play is safer to do in armor than unarmored.

[In the Pisani Dossi, the Scholar is wearing a crown.]

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

Look how strongly I hold your neck by the shoulder, 
Which in this way you evade the attempt: you vainly try 
To fling the unarmed [man] to the ground. But the counters conquer you.

This here is the counter of the play that appeared before it, so if one grabs you by the stirrup or by the foot, throw your arm to his neck. You should do this quickly, for in this fashion you could dismount him from his horse; if you do this, he will hit the ground without fail.

[The Morgan and Paris resemble the Pisani Dossi.]

I will throw you and your horse, prevented by none, 
By whose raging haunches the chest of mine will stay. 
I will not relinquish the resounding bridle of your quadruped 
While you would strike the muddy ground precipitously with the crown of the head.
That best deception certainly prevails when [one is] wearing armor; afterward
He himself begins to fear being unable to injure anyone in armor.

This is a play of throwing one to the ground, horse and all: that is, the Master rides to the right side of his enemy and throws his right arm over the neck of his [enemy's] horse. And he grabs the bridle of his [enemy's] horse behind the bit, rotates the head of the horse up, and he should spur his horse with his foot striking the rump or flanks. And in this way he will fall, horse and all…

[In the Paris and Morgan, the Scholar is wearing a crown.]

…This is the counter of the play that came before in which he wants to throw his companion to the ground along with his horse. This is an easy thing to remember, that when the Scholar throws his arm over the neck of his horse to grab the bridle, the player should quickly throw an arm to the neck of the Scholar, and thus he is forced to release it. Following that which you see depicted here, so should you do.

This is a play of taking the bridle of a horse from the hand of your companion in the way that you see depicted here. The Scholar, when he goes against another on horseback, should ride to the right side and throw his right arm over the neck of the horse, grabbing its bridle near his hand on the left-hand side, and so take the bridle off the horse's head. And this play is more secure in armor than unarmored.

[In the Morgan, the Scholar is wearing a crown.]

### Notes

- Four mounted combat pairs arranged in quadrants.
- All figures are engaged in close mounted combat with swords.
- Significant overlap in language with fol_45r; these two pages form a continuous sequence of plays and counters.
- Text continues to be extremely difficult to read; script is small and faded in many places.
- References to "Zugho" (play), "Rimedio" (remedy), and "contrario" (counter) structure the techniques.

## Folio 46r
**Section:** Spear vs. Other Weapons

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top-left:
¶ Che in fina tres compagnye che rebattino ala la dou / questo maistro . La prima lo uole ferir sotto laza / de porta fira lanza e reuolti lanza . e altro ferina . la lanza sorpillar . e la tolta lanza . La terza coltello / contrario chi fira lanza . ¶ E chi lo posta . chi e lanza chi / di Ratto fassote piu spiga quello . ¶ Treduro lolitra . no fiori e chi oltra .

Top-right:
¶ E lo pona adma e dou . chi mal manero . chi / e restando in que chi mal maistro . e portare . / la tolla de trapassare . chi uole noste sola Regalti . / che chi lo uso que la lo dou fior maistro fir lo finalo . / la lanza me no ussira e finoro . di chi lo e molte / mano che posso fiollare . ¶ Io nello ello fiore / la giornata usira . chi lo bontro az lo spado lo Fiore . / quello e citrar bontro . questo fiore lo miri / questo chi notta dandolo .

Bottom-left:
¶ Questo fio Zugho del maistro che denauere che / demota in la giomadra . girolo de cauallo . dio / e pericolo in portir . che ferir . dou pagare / chi la forço oueramente che zugho . e dapoi . ello / lo ueglo me lo la fio la [?] . ello e Zugho e / ponto lo questo fiorir in la testa . E fionde ponto / la suo Armadura cula propria .

Bottom-right:
¶ Anchora a questo Zugho del dou maistro chi / demauiga in posta du fiorde lo sogattuarmo . e fiore . / la lanza . e rebattitela . in uoltira essa lanza . e fio / la fiore . chi la podesta . chi questo fiore ello assi / godo . a di nello aquile .

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
That in the end three companions who beat aside at the place of this master. The first wants to strike under the carrying of the lance and turns the lance, and another strike, the lance to surprise, and the taken lance. The third knife counter who strikes the lance. And who the guard, who is lance who quickly done more than that. Thirteen beyond, no flowers and who beyond.

Top-right:
And the placing of the soul and where, who bad manner, who remaining here who bad master, and carrying, the taking of passing through, who wants our sole Regal, that who uses this at the place of flower master does the final. The lance from me will not go out and I finish, of who is many hands that I can bloom. I in the flower the day will go out, who the good at the sword the Flower, that and enter the good, this flower look at it, this who notes giving it.

Bottom-left:
This is the Play of the master that denounces who demotes in the engagement, circling the horse, God and peril in departing, that striking, where to pay who the force truly that Play, and after, the old man to me the done. And the Play and point this to flourish in the head. And slings point his Armor with the proper one.

Bottom-right:
Also to this Play of the where master who leads in the guard of the flower the disarming, and flower, the lance, and beat it aside, in turning that lance and done the flower, who the power, who this flower he so enjoys, of the eagle.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[16] Here are three opponents who wish to kill this Master. The first intends to strike underhand, and he carries his spear at the mid-point. The second carries his lance couched and fully extended. The third intends to throw his spear. They have agreed that no one will make more than one strike each. Also they will take it in turns.

| 
[17] *Even if Rolando and Pulicano were to make me an invitation with lances,
I would await them in this match with spear or with staff;
I will beat their lances and I will strike their heads
As I depart from this guard.*

Attack me one after another if you choose. For I am not going anywhere. I am ready and waiting for you in the Boar’s Tusk guard. When the spear is launched against me, whether fixed grip or thrown from the hand, I quickly step off line by advancing my right foot and stepping crosswise with my left foot, beating aside the spear that comes to strike me. Even if I were attacked a thousand times, my defense would not fail me even once. What I can do with my ghiavarina I could also do with a staff or a sword. The defense I make against the spear I could also make against a sword or a staff. My plays are shown next.

[18] *I cut you in the head with my spear
From the guard of the Master which is so quick.*

This is the play of the Master who waits with his ghiavarina in the Boar’s Tusk for an attack from the three on horseback. To enter into this play he steps off line and beats aside his opponent’s spear. And although he knows this play, I will demonstrate for him that my ghiavarina is so fast that I can strike my opponent with both thrusts or cuts against his head.

[19] *With the butt I have struck you with my spear,
But I have another point which is even sharper.*

This is also a play made by the Master above, who waits in the Boar’s Tusk. In his place I can make this play instead of the other: if he beats aside my spear with his spear, I rotate my spear and strike him with the butt, which is capped with well-tempered steel.

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

Here are three companions who want to strike this Master: the first wants to strike underhand and carries his weapon at the middle of the lance; the other carries his weapon in rest at the full of the lance; the third wants to throw his lance. And they are agreed that none should strike more than one blow per man, and that they should do it one by one.

If Roland, and likewise Pulicanus and the rude one, would attack me, the foot-soldier
With ashen spear, I would wait while considering,
And on the right he would hold either a javelin or a wild staff.
And I will strike a more furious blow against the spears.
To the greatest extent with this action I would withdraw while striking down the high heads.

Come one by one whoever wants to come, and I will run from no one. Again, I am positioned in the Boar's Tusk to wait, and when the lance will come against me (carried or thrown by hand) I immediately avoid its path—that is, I advance my right foot out of the way and with my left I step on the traverse, beating the lance that comes to strike me such that of a thousand, I couldn't fail to beat even one. This I do with the spear, and with the staff or with the sword I would also do it, and the defense that I make which is against the spear, I could also make against the sword or the staff. I ask that you view the plays that are hereafter.

Now that lance cuts your gloomy head with a great wound.
And he moves with the caution of a proud master.

This play belongs to the Master who came before, who awaits him of the horse with his spear in Boar's Tusk. In stepping out of the way and beating as he does, he enters into this play and so that it is understood, I do according to his word and with the edge and the point I could strike him in the head, since I carry my spear with such readiness.

I, the clever one, strike your lips with this hard thrust,
Anticipating renewing a wound with the heavy point.

Again is this the play of the aforesaid Master who was previously in the Stance of the Boar. In his stead I do this, which he also could have done: when the lance is beaten, I turn my spear and I strike him with the butt (since it is well-tempered iron).

### Notes

- Four mounted combat pairs arranged in quadrants.
- Page numbered "48" in upper right corner (old foliation), though partially visible.
- Top-left pair: one figure grapples while mounted; top-right pair engages with lances at close range.
- Bottom-left pair: one rider appears to be pulling or wrestling the other from the saddle; bottom-right pair shows similar grappling.
- This page features mounted grappling and disarming techniques (abrazare on horseback).
- The text is extremely difficult due to small script, fading, and Venetian abbreviations.

---

## Folio 46v
**Section:** Mounted Fencing

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top-left:
¶ Io contrario del questo Rimedio . cum e brazzo / chi piu lo contrario . la Spada . Per la sperientia . che la / le loy far . battagliamento . contrado in lo giro in per lo so / ala parte . ¶ E la griti . e chi molto lo ponto . / la lo metti . e Dou fio que ello andare di era per per / guaste .

Top-right:
¶ Questo fio un altro de ferime . per Dou fui modo / e figarnado de lo brauça . Che ello contrar fiore . ella cum / le parte / de lo dello fior contir . e ello brauço . la fior / posta lo por e fiore . e per lo chi zugho . e lo chi quello / e come . la fiore . che chi lo noto . e fiore . contraste . / qui chi e quello . che fio la sesta . solla questa . chi lo / e altra figarnado . ¶ E poi per questa . chi lo fiorir / lo brauço . p que fiorir dou lo piu . che fiore . ¶ E per / fiore . la piu manere . mossardo .

Bottom-left:
¶ Questo fio zugho del maistro chi dou e / di mota in la Zugarda . giroli de cauallo . ¶ Dio / e perichollo in portir chi fiorir . dou pagare / chi la forço . che zugho . E dapoi fior ponto / lo questi fiorir in la testa . ¶ E fionde ponto / la suo Armaduro cula propri .

Bottom-right:
¶ Questo fio dou questo de noi la brena fio cauallo . / la mano del compagnere e mudo chi uoltir e dissipare / e lo plasere . contrade . chi fio e fiomero de chi uoltir de la / lo piu lanza bonna . a lo fiore . ¶ E cherando chi / la fio lanza bonna . e lo brenzo nulla chi dou fiorir / la ponta contra sena . e chi piu e lo fui quello chi / fiore . in la odora . ¶ E per posso don quello . la / la fiu morire . riuempio . chi dou nuelle tutti / la brenza . che fio . e per figarnado brouçe brella / chi Soferimento .

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top-left:
I am the counter of this Remedy, with the arm who more the counter, the Sword. By experience, that the laws make, the battling, countered in the turn for his part. And the cries, and who much the point, the put it. And where done that to go from the era for the ruined.

Top-right:
This is another of the striking, for where I was in manner and figured of the arm. That the counter flower, with the parts of the flower to counter, and the arm, the flower guard the power and flower, and for who plays, and who that and like the flower, that who notes, and flower counters. Here who is that, which is the sixth, only this, who is and another figure. And then for this, who the flourishing the arm, for which flourishing where the more, that flower. And for flower, the more manner, shown.

Bottom-left:
This is the Play of the master who where and dismounts in the Guard, circles the horse. God and peril in departing who flourishes, where to pay who the force, that Play. And after flower point this flourishing in the head. And slings point his Armor with the proper one.

Bottom-right:
This is where this of ours the rein is horse, the hand of the companion and mute who turns and separates, and the pleasure, countered, who done and riverman of who turns from the more lance good, at the flower. And seeking who the done lance good, and the arm nothing who where flourishes the point against sense, and who more and was that who flowers in the scent. And for I can give that, the done dying, refilling, who where new all the arm, that done, and for figure arm beautiful who Suffering.

### Translation — Colin Hatcher (Getty)

[31] *This Master has lashed a cord to his saddle
And to the foot of his lance, which is cruel and destructive,
To throw to the neck of his enemy,
In order to drag him to the ground; so do I say.*

This Master has bound one end of a strong rope to his horse’s saddle, and the other end to the butt of his lance. First he strikes his opponent, then he will cast the lance to the left side of his opponent, over his opponent’s left shoulder, and in this way he can drag his opponent from his horse.

[32] This scoundrel was fleeing from me towards a castle. I rode so hard and fast at full rein that I caught up with him closed to his castle. And I struck him with my sword in his armpit, which is a difficult area to protect with armor. Now I withdraw to avoid retaliation from his friends.

*Here ends the Flower of the Art of Combat,
In this way one man can stand against another:
Made by Fiore Furlano, son of Sir Benedetto;
Those who knew him can believe his words well.*

Here ends this book that was written by Fiore the scholar, who has published here everything he knows about this art, that is to say, everything he knows about armed fighting is contained within this book. This same Fiore has named his book “The Flower of the Battle”. Let he for whom this book was made be forever praised, for his nobility and virtue have no equal, Fiore the Friulian, a simple elderly man, entrusts this book to you.

### Translation — Michael Chidester (Morgan)

This Master has lashed a strong cord (that is, one end) to the saddle of his horse, and the other end is lashed to the foot of his lance. First he wants to strike, and then to put the tied part of the lance to the left of his enemy, throwing it over his shoulder, and thereby to be able to pull him off his horse and onto the ground.

Florius, the most skilled authority, previously brought forth
This book. It is therefore him, an accomplished,
Contributing man of the Friulian people, you are honoring.

### Notes

- Four mounted combat pairs arranged in quadrants.
- Top pairs show sword combat on horseback; bottom pairs show grappling and horse control techniques.
- Bottom-right text block is the longest on the page and discusses controlling the rein and horse.
- Bottom-left text closely parallels the text on fol_46r bottom-left (possibly a repeated or related play).
- Text remains very difficult throughout; numerous uncertain readings.

## Folio 47r

### Original Text (Claude OCR)

Top:
¶ Io nesso bruto chi e lo incornato da fiore . cum du / ala Sella del duo cauallo . que uene cum le . Lontro / Lud fuorir . e per la Lanza morti testa . Sella per gi / omore . Sella . e per morasse . fior la Spada butar / lattoro . e pretorir lo si del cauallo frontisimanro .

Bottom-left:
¶ Questo Obaldo me fassone e una fiore . che / questo contri . che lo Sforço apresso le fierença . con / troueço in forço . E dou fiore . fiolle lo fioro / cum lo stabille . e chi modo fio e gir ferine seruo re / e ponta . de pir contrari nello contramente .

Bottom-right:
¶ Dou ferendo la lidere chi e tutto lo fiollado . chio / armado chi pe si guitiuere . ¶ qui la possa . Io chi / lo Sella . chi fio e guererre . Questo in nomo / Guardaco a como ala ferimeno . ¶ Questo posso / la brouço . fior argettare chi fio stabilo in questa / chi farmo . La priniçalo . fior fradaro estre il con / molato . Portare actrue .

### Translation — Claude Vision

Top:
I am the brute who is crowned by the flower, with two at the saddle of the two horses that come with the distant Lud to come out, and by the lance dead head, Saddle for love, Saddle, and for the morass, flower the Sword to throw the lateral, and to pass through the of the horse frontmost.

Bottom-left:
This Obaldo makes me a fashion and a flower, that this counters, that the Force near the striking, found in force. And where flower, flows the flower with the stable, and who in manner done and goes to strike the servant king and point, of for counters in the counter-mind.

Bottom-right:
Where striking the leader who is all the flowered, I am armed who for himself guards. Here the power. I who the Saddle, who is and warrior. This in name Guard-here as to the striking. This I can the arm, flower to throw who is stable in this who we arm. The principal, flower brother outside the with consoled. Carry behind.

### Notes

- Three mounted combat scenes on this page (not four as on previous pages).
- Page numbered "49" in upper right corner (old foliation), partially visible.
- Top scene shows two mounted figures with lances in a joust or charge, both horses at full gallop with lances extended.
- Bottom-left shows a smaller scene with a mounted figure near what appears to be a barrier or structure.
- Bottom-right shows two mounted figures.
- The page layout is less dense than previous pages, with more blank space in the lower portion.
- Text continues to be very challenging to decipher.
